Pushkin

Pushkin Industries presents:

SHAPING SOUNDS: Stevie Wonder, DEVO, The Synth Revolution – and My Life Behind the Music

Written by Robert Margouleff with Jim Reilly

and read by the author

Dedicated to the memory of Hans K. Maeder,

founder and headmaster of the Stockbridge School,

for opening my mind to the world around me.

Foreword
CLEAN

Foreword

By Mark Mothersbaugh of DEVO

By some accounts, we were a pain in the ass for producers, partly because we would come into a recording studio already hearing our songs in our heads. The producers then had to spend their time adjusting to our ideas rather than making the record using their trademark sounds and direction. After our second album’s inappropriate sterility, we thought we might never find a producer we could work with.

We grew up loving Motown and were big fans of Bootsy Collins and Prince. That’s probably what attracted us to Robert Margouleff in the first place. His resume was a hybrid of R&B, funk, and electronic music. His work with TONTO and Stevie Wonder was groundbreaking. He was obviously a conceptualist who understood us and what we wanted. He helped us find sounds we were imagining but had never successfully translated to vinyl before. Unlike most other producers, he believed his job was to enhance our vision, not to change us.

We met Bob at the Record Plant on a lunch break while we were filming Neil Young’s dystopian feature film Human Highway. We arrived in full costume, dressed as nuclear waste workers. That first meeting was a classic, over-the-top DEVO story, which I’ll leave for Bob to tell later in the book. Needless to say, we hit it off immediately. His enthusiasm was infectious!

In late 1979, we began recording the songs that would become our third album, Freedom Of Choice, at the Record Plant’s Studio A in Los Angeles. We expected a typical recording setup with the band in the studio and the producer and engineer in the control room, but Bob had a different idea. He set up the whole band, except for our drummer, Alan Meyers, in the control room. We played live and listened, loud and without headphones, through quadraphonic surround sound speakers. We were in the middle of the music and fed off that energy, just as we did at our live shows.

He showed us new ways to record both guitars and synths after we had previously wasted hours in the studio trying to get the sounds we were looking for. He mic’d the same guitar performances through two different sounding amps at once. This gave us the capability of mixing and matching those elements when we got close to the final mix, instead of having to stack guitars or synths, each individually played by hand.

While we only worked together on Freedom Of Choice, Bob and I have remained friends to this day. For a while, I even had TONTO, the mega synth that he and Malcolm Cecil created and made famous with Stevie Wonder, in the basement of my studio. It had been in storage for about twenty-five years. I had just opened my studio, Mutato Muzika, in Hollywood and happened to talk to Bob. I asked him if he wanted to set TONTO up to see if we could make it work again.

It was there for two or three years. People would come over to my studio and spend all day figuring out how to get a particular synth sound on this big, old, modular synth. Trent Reznor came by once and made a sound on it, which he took back to his studio. In the end, though, most people usually decided that if that was what it was like in the old days, they liked the new days better. But now, decades later, it seems that analog has made a comeback.

I think it would be easy to say that Bob Margouleff was DEVO’s most successful collaboration with an outside producer. Bob and his engineering partner, Howard Siegel, were always positive, which helped offset the negativity of DEVO. Because of those Record Plant recording sessions, I later became friends with Jack Black, who was Howard’s younger half-brother. Howard played DEVO for a young, impressionable Jack, long before he became a Hollywood superstar, and it has stayed with him to this day. Many say Freedom Of Choice was our most cohesive vinyl output to date. Bob was an empathetic collaborator, and he made us stronger for that.

Mark Mothersbaugh

July 8, 2025

Preface
CLEAN

A note from the author

On countless nights I’ve stood in the recording studio, all eyes on me. Chasing the perfect sound, playing my role as a song transforms from idea into reality. Every note, every beat, every story has been a part of my journey. I’ve experienced struggles and successes, highs and lows. These experiences have shaped and taught me.

This book is my story as I look back over the nearly sixty-year musical path that has led me to where I am today. My journey started with the Moog synthesizer. I used the Moog to compose the score for the first aboveground/underground movie, Ciao! Manhattan, featuring Edie Sedgwick, Andy Warhol’s first superstar. That film set my career firmly in motion.

That original Moog evolved into one of the first electronic synthesizers truly capable of producing an orchestra of sounds. My partner Malcolm Cecil and I built a band around it called Tonto’s Expanding Head Band. Th synth became TONTO, and Malcolm turned that into an acronym: The Original New Timbral Orchestra.

Together with Malcolm and TONTO, I spent three years with Stevie Wonder and helped create some of his most important albums: Music Of My Mind Talking Book 1972, Innervisions 1973, and Fulfillingness’ First Finale 1974. Needless to say, those albums led to the many records and soundtracks I have worked on over the years that followed and opened countless doors I could have never imagined while I was growing up during the post–World War II boom, in my nice, middle-class Jewish family in Great Neck, New York.

At its heart, my journey has shown me how technology enables innovation and creativity. And, similarly, how creativity and innovation demand new technologies. If art requires originality, new technology can inspire new ideas. If the artist’s vision demands new avenues of expression, new technologies will arise to realize those visions. It always starts from a place of truth and of connecting a creative’s heart and soul to the technology that will allow that heart to be heard. As a producer, director, or recording engineer, you are part shaman, part empath, and part technologist, helping creatives perform at their best.

Whether you’re a musician, an aspiring producer, a seasoned recording engineer, someone who loves music, or someone looking for insight into how creativity finds its voice, I hope you’ll find something valuable within these pages. I hope that this peek behind the scenes of some of the classic albums I’ve helped bring to life—and the insights that I’ve gleaned from a lifetime on the edge of innovation, creativity, and technology with masters of their craft—will help you on your journey.

Standing on the edge has been a constant theme throughout my life. It’s where I feel alive. Sometimes you fall, other times you take a leap and soar. I’ve had more than my share of both, but if I’ve learned anything from these trials and tribulations, it’s that in the end, the real joy comes from working together toward a common end, sharing your creativity, exploring uncharted waters, and, when you’re lucky enough to find the magic, giving it to the world.

Prologue

Memorial Day Weekend, 1971

It was early Sunday evening. I was working at MediasoDescriptund recording studios in New York, where I’d settled in with my Moog synthesizer as the resident electronic music specialist and hippie bad boy. The studio was closed for the holiday, and there was virtually no traffic on 57th Street. It was uncharacteristically quiet.

The studio’s chief engineer, Malcolm Cecil, lived in an apartment one flight up over a delicatessen, right next door. Management put him there soDescript he could keep his eye on the front door and he had to be on call twenty-four-seven in case anything went wrong.

Malcolm and I were both musicians, and we had become creative partners and collaborators on a musical project that we called Tonto’s Expanding Head Band. We pursued it whenever we could get time between our paying gigs. TheDescript synthesizer we created, which was the heart of the project, was TONTO. Then and now, it was and is the world’s largest synthesizer.

TONTO was more than a machine. It had a persoDescriptnality all its own, and it truly was the third member of our group. By now, TONTO had become an acronym: The Original New Timbral Orchestra. We had just completed our first all-synthesized electronica album featuring TONTO, Zero Time, and our hopes were running high.

01:35 - 2:05

[MUSIC CUE: Tonto’ s Expanding Headband “Cybernaut”]

The evening was warm. We were hanging out in Malcolm’s living room with the windows wide open when we heard a voice calling up from the street. We stuck our heads out the window and saw Malcolm’s friend and fellow bass player, Ronnie Blanco, standing at the locked front door of the studio with a tall Black guy.

‘Hey, Malcolm,’ Ronnie shouted, ‘I’ve got soDescriptmebody here who wants to see TONTO.’

That soDescriptmebody turned out to be Stevie Wonder. We invited him into the studio, and he never left. We would work with Stevie for three years, associate producing, engineering, recording, mixing, and programming all the synth work on Music Of My Mind, Talking Book, Innervisions (for which we won a Grammy), and Fulfillingness’ First Finale.

By the time of Stevie’s visit, we had moved TONTO into MediasoDescriptund’s Studio B, which was in the basement. That way, we could stay out of the way and would not have to break down our setup after our sessions to make room for the big recording dates in Studio A.

The first thing Stevie asked when he met TONTO was, ‘Is it a keyboard instrument?’

‘Well, yes, soDescriptrt of,’ Malcolm replied.

Stevie wanted an explanation of soDescriptrt of. We showed him the synthesizer and gave him a running narration about what it looked like. He put his hands all over it, touching the knobs, the patch cords, and the multiple keyboards. Of course, being a keyboard player, the first thing he did was try to play a chord. He put a whole fistful of notes down on the keyboard at once, then said, ‘Oh, it’s not working. Something’s broken.’

‘No, Stevie, it doesn’t work that way,’ Malcolm explained. ‘You can only play one note at a time. It’s like a trumpet or a saxophone.’

We realized that Stevie was quite taken by the idea that this was a keyboard instrument that he could use to create all kinds of soDescriptunds, even new sounds that no one had ever heard before. Before diving in, he asked, ‘Well, where’s the piano?’

‘We’ve got an excellent Steinway right over here,’ I told him.

Stevie sat down and started playing. Then he asked, ‘Can we record?’

At first, we tracked a few soDescriptngs with just vocals and piano. Then, Stevie said he wanted Malcolm to play acoustic bass on a song called ‘Crazy Letters.’ We tried a few takes, but it was obvious that it wasn’t working.

‘It’s the wrong soDescriptund,’ Malcolm said. ‘It’s taking it to a jazz place. We need a different sort of bass sound.’

‘We can get on the synthesizer,’ I replied.

SoDescript, we whipped up a bass sound and told Stevie that he should play all the parts, since he knew all the changes. We explained what TONTO could and couldn’t do, and he caught on quickly and put down a bass line.

hat’s how it was with Stevie. Everything was very much in the moment. Nothing was preplanned. It was all intuitive and improvised, which was terrific.

Stevie told us that his contract with Motown had expired on his twenty-first birthday, May 13, just two weeks before he came knocking at our door. He madeDescript it clear that he felt very limited by the label and wanted his independence. He had grown tired of the Motown formula—having to play his soDescriptngs to his arranger, who would then go away and write the arrangement, record the track with the house band, call Stevie in after it was recorded, tell him where and what to sing, and then send him away while they did the mixing. Stevie said it sounded nothing like the music in his mind.

We clicked immediately. hat we did first was just hit the record button. The soDescriptngs were tumbling out of his soul, and he was calling out the pieces faster than we could capture them.

Usually, he started with the keyboard and voice. Stevie had his own internal clock. He had an incredible sense of rubato, adding emotion by subtly changing a soDescriptng’s tempo, like going from a verse to a chorus. He rarely used a click track on any of his songs. The subtle timing changes were already in his head.

The next step was putting down a drum track. We had a beautiful set of drums in Studio B and asked Stevie how he wanted to approach playing them. We spent a fair bit of time setting up the drums soDescript that he could sense where each drum and cymbal would be, which was not quite the way you would set it up for a typical player. We positioned each drum where he envisioned it in his mind.

We’d ask, ‘Okay, where the hi-hat?’ He liked the hi-hat in front of his snare soDescript that it wasn’t difficult to reach. Then we would ask him, ‘Where’s the ride cymbal?’

He’d swing his arms around in the air, and we would move the cymbals to where he could naturally reach them. We did the same thing with the tom-toms. He wanted the bass drum placed in a certain, very specific way, too.

We always recorded the drums from Stevie’s point of view, with the hi-hat on the left, soDescript the sound field in the headphones was the same as what he was hearing live. This helped give him an accurate sonic image of each drum’s location. Then, we spent time tuning and miking them, working on the EQ and the stereo array, and fine-tuning the sounds. Sometimes, we would use different mics, either dynamic or condenser, depending on what we thought the song called for. Stevie would play the drums, and they’d lock exactly to the subtle timing and motion of the keyboard and guide vocal. hen we realized what a great drummer he was.

Fortunately, I already had a little experience working with blind people. I remember Tommy Kaitlin, a blind teacher at Stockbridge, who I helped navigate the campus and one time took skiing on the bunny slope at Bousquet Mountain. Both Malcolm and I knew that navigating the studio would be a big challenge for Stevie. SoDescript, we focused on keeping the environment predictable and stable. It was of paramount importance working with Stevie. We were careful to keep any boom mics high enough so that he would not walk into them and smack his head. We did the same with the physical studio layout. Instruments didn’t move around, and we made sure that the number of steps and routes to doors, washrooms, and so on didn’t change

We kept an open mic in the control room, not one that you had to push a button on to access, soDescript that when Stevie was in the studio with his headphones on, he could always listen to what we were saying behind the console. We had turned the studio and the control room into a single open sonic space. For us, there was no longer any separation between the studio and the control room.

We set up the studio with every instrument we thought we might need in a big circle, all wired up and available for Stevie to play at all times, ready to accommodate his limitless creativity. The synthesizer, acoustic piano, Rhodes, clavinet, drums, a vocal microphone, and more were all patched in, hot and ready to go whenever inspiration struck. Stevie didn’t take long to memorize the array, and he easily moved around the instruments and the studio.

Most of the time we had a seven-and-a-half-inch-per-second quarter-inch tape running, which recorded whatever Stevie did. Everything he sang and played would end up on those tapes. He would get restless while we mics or adjust the setup, soDescript he’d sit down at the piano and start playing and singing. He always had ideas for new songs. We would go back catch fragments, and say, ‘That’s a great song there.’ We captured everything he did, and he came to rely on that.

Recording soDescriptng after song with Stevie that first weekend together was like creating a song library or archive. We weren’t thinking about creating albums, just creating an extensive database of songs. They would be in various states of completion. Stevie would work on one song for a couple of hours, then write another for the next few, and so on.

After those transformative Memorial Day sessions in Studio B, Stevie went back to Detroit, packed his bags, and moved to New York. When he returned, we began recording tirelessly together, which changed our world forever. When Stevie came on the scene, nothing else made any difference to Malcolm and me anymore. In soDescriptme way, it was like we lost our minds. We lost touch with the real world once we became part of Stevie’s world. And it was a beautiful place to be.

We brought Stevie the ability to achieve the creative freedom he craved, arrange his own tracks, and be involved in every aspect of the recording process. At age twenty-one, his creative genius was finally on full display, helped by our technical and musical expertise.

Over that long weekend, our collaboration was born. That was the beginning of our incredible journey together. Little did I realize that my role as a synthesist and recording engineer would play a significant role in transforming the landscape of music and soDescriptund, or that we were standing on the edge of shaping the history of pop music. We would touch the sun with Stevie and create some of the greatest albums anyone could imagine.

It wasn’t just fate. It was the product of my life and all my experiences up to that point. I’d discovered the synthesizer and becme one of its pioneers, and now the time was ripe for an invention like the Moog to lead to a seismic change in music composition and performance.

Technology would always inspire my long and storied career in music, film, and soDescriptund. But to really understand how those pieces came together, you need to go right back to where it all began.

Part 1

‘All human beings are created free and equal in dignity and rights.’

A Journey Begins

Chapter 1

Chapter 1

Planting Seeds

I was born on August 8, 1940. I grew up in a middle-class Jewish family in what was then a small town, Great Neck, New York. As far back as I can remember, my mom, Ruth, was on her own. She’d spend her days at the Great Western Fur Company’s New York City offices, keeping the books for the family business while my father, Jean, was on the road for seven months of the year selling furs to major department stores.

My mom would commute from Great Neck to the city every morning. In the winter, I watched her through the living room window across the street, clutching her huge pocketbook, standing there in the rain, snow, or shine, waiting for the bus to take her to the train station. She was only four foot eleven, and she was somewhat emotionally fragile.

I was the baby of the family. My brother, Donald, was ten years older than me, and my sister, Jacquline, was seven years older, so we never had much in common, except for our shared interests in music and sailing.

We had a beautiful Steinway grand piano in the sun parlor, and Jackie studied piano and practiced for at least four or five hours every day.

01:3 - 02:

[MUSIC CUE: Beethoven “Sonata No. 8 in C Minor”]

She went to the Mannes School and then Juilliard, and her playing filled the house with Bach, Brahms, and Beethoven. I learned to love classical piano music and started music lessons with my sister’s teacher, Mr. Grain, when I was old enough to reach the pedals. I took up the cello in fifth grade, and Jackie would accompany me. She said my ears used to get red when I played. I loved it. Music always created decisive emotional moments for me.

My brother, Donald, played the piano in his own inimitable stride style when he was home from pre-med school. He later supported himself playing in a piano bar in Bern, Switzerland, while he was attending medical school.

02: - 0

[MUSIC CUE + SFX: “Ain’t Misbehavin’”]

He went to Switzerland to study medicine because Tufts, where he did his pre-med, had a quota on accepting Jews, and they had met that limit. While he was at Tufts, he had to find an off-campus studio apartment as they would not allow a Jew to live in the school dorm. I was too young to realize the significance of that at the time.

It was Donald who introduced me to sailing. We had an eighteen-foot sloop we dubbed the Pixie. We kept the boat in the garage during the winter and put it in the water every spring. Every year we went through the ritual of sanding and varnishing her mahogany hull until it gleamed.

Both music and sailing were solitary experiences, so I was left to my own devices with very little support or attention—or so it seemed. I always knew I was different. I felt lonely, separate, and emotionally isolated as a youngster. I hated team sports and was always the last to be picked on the playground, and I spent a lot of time on my own.

My father was a great public speaker and negotiator. When he spoke, people listened. I think I inherited my public speaking skills from him. I also inherited the music gene. By the time I was in fifth grade, I was moving up in the Kensington School Orchestra cello section. I would trudge to and from school with my cello under one arm and my book bag under the other (there were no backpacks in those days). We lived just at the border of the school’s bussing zone, and I didn’t qualify to ride the school bus, so I had to walk back and forth every day, rain or shine.

In 1952, at the end of fifth grade, my world got turned upside down when my father’s business fell on hard times and our family’s financial conditions became dire. It was a disaster. My mother was devastated and went into a deep depression. My French paternal grandmother, my Grand-mère, came to live with us. She ran the household and looked after me.

There was no question about who the boss was. She used to read to me from the Hardy Boys’ adventures and a set of encyclopedias called The Book Of Knowledge. She was a big influence on my intellectual curiosity and, in reality, more of a mother and teacher to me. I think I got my sense of curiosity from those days with her, curled up safely on the couch, listening to her read.

Grand-mère was also a phenomenal chef and always had treats when I came home from school. She was the creator of a marmalade recipe she kept secret until, on her deathbed, she gave it to my cousin Beth. That recipe went on to become the world-famous ‘Sarabeth’s Marmalade.’ I currently have a jar of it in my kitchen.

As I entered my teen years and hormones kicked in, everything turned upside down. Although I didn’t understand it at the time, looking back, I realize I was questioning my sexuality even at that early age. My parents never talked to me about the birds and the bees. The word ‘homosexuality’ was never uttered in our house, so I didn’t think there was anything wrong with my feelings. I thought then that I would get over it when I turned into a man on my Bar Mitzvah, and that these feelings were just a phase—a part of growing up.

I didn’t see much of my parents, and when we were all together, I never saw them share as much as a kiss or a hug. I just didn’t connect with them emotionally, and, in some ways, I didn’t connect with myself either.

When I started ninth grade, I was not doing very well. I didn’t feel like I belonged anywhere. I had difficulty paying attention school, and I failed most of my classes. I was a mess. Today, you would call it ADHD, but back then, there wasn’t a name for it. I hated public school, and I did everything I could to avoid going. The only saving grace was playing the cello in the school orchestra. Mrs. Hunt was the conductor. She was short and thin, with flaming red hair. She had an extra-long baton that would almost touch the ground when she conducted.

Music became a refuge. It was the only place I could connect with my feelings. The rest of my life was an emotional wasteland.

Happily, by the time I was fifteen, my father’s business was once again on the upswing, and he was mostly off the road. He became active politically and served several terms as the mayor of Great Neck Estates.

In the summer of 1954, I had my first run-in with anti-Semitism. I was fourteen years old, and my parents had signed me up to a sailing camp at the Knickerbocker Yacht Club in Port Washington. David Marks, my school buddy since kindergarten, had also signed up. We were inseparable. David’s parents had just built a new house in Broadlawn Harbor, next to the very posh, old-money enclave of Kenilworth. A marsh separated them, and you could see Port Washington and the clubs along the shore about three miles across Manhasset Bay. By car, it was an hour’s drive around the bay, but by boat it was only a twenty-minute voyage, so the club sent a launch to pick us up in the morning and drop us off at the end of the day.

David and I would amble across the marsh to Broadlawn, playing and exploring, often stopping at an old floating dock cast ashore in a storm. The oil drum floats that would normally be underneath the dock were gone, so we could crawl underneath it. It was our hideout, the place where we got up to all the expected teenage high jinks.

The Knickerbocker was the Jewish yacht club. There were two other yacht clubs along that side of Manhasset Bay. The first and biggest was the Manhasset Bay Yacht Club, which was next door to the Knickerbocker. There was a sign on its front gate announcing that it was ‘RESTRICTED,’ which I learned meant no Jews or Blacks allowed. The second, Port Washington Yacht Club, was a blue-collar working man’s club that was also not that welcoming. That was just the way it was. No one ever talked about it.

The clubs maintained a fleet of Blue Jay class sailboats. They were only fourteen feet long, but they had everything a bigger boat would have, including a spinnaker. We raced against the other clubs all the time; we just couldn’t belong to them. Despite that, my summer at the club ignited a lifelong love for sailing and the sea.

Another one of my sailing school buddies, Steve Morse, ignited something quite different in me. He told me stories about being a student at the Stockbridge School, a boarding school in the Berkshires, a rural region of western Massachusetts known for its rolling mountains and breathtaking vistas. For the first time in a long time, I was interested in a school. I felt drawn to Stockbridge I had an intuition that it was where I belonged.

Chapter 2

Learning became living

It was the end of my summer at the yacht club, and although I was dreading my return to school, anything other than returning to Great Neck Junior High was not on my parents’ radar. My father was busy on the road, and my mother was busy running the business from their New York City office. I was depressed, self-absorbed, and lost. When my depression became overwhelmingly obvious, my mother sent me to see a psychiatrist. I started lobbying hard, telling them I wanted to go away to the school Steve had told me about. Although I was oblivious to its educational philosophy (as were they), with my mounting anxiety and stress about returning to public school, my parents heeded the psychiatrist’s advice and agreed to send me to Stockbridge.

I met the school’s founder and headmaster, Hans Maeder, at an apartment the school maintained for meetings and recruiting in Midtown Manhattan. I sat there, nervous and sweaty, scrunched up between my parents on a long black leather couch. In spite of my dismal school record, after hearing my tale, Hans accepted me into his school.

Looking back at my Stockbridge days, I realize that in many ways, the school was a sort of holding tank for teenagers who, for one reason or another, were troubled—a place for those who were living in an unsympathetic environment at home, but whose parents could afford to send them away to school. I fit right in. I wasn’t alone.

I was saved from myself when I was transplanted to the Berkshire Mountains. When I arrived, I didn’t realize then that the Stockbridge philosophy and practice of the greater good would become so profound and life-changing for me. At Stockbridge, I found stability, sanity, and my voice, and, for the first time in my life, I started thinking about the future and my place in it. For me, the school was a trip of wonders, enlightenment, and tolerance at a time when our world was filled with turmoil and uncertainty, and I was filled with anxiety and fear. I never looked back.

Hans Maeder had founded the school in 1948. He was a German World War II refugee. As a youth, he had broken politically with his father, whom he described as a passionate nationalist and anti-Semite. Hans was the opposite. He was active in the anti-Nazi underground until he fled Germany in 1933, finally reaching Hawaii in 1941, where he was interned until the war’s end. In 1945, he moved to New York and joined the faculty at the progressive Walden School, becoming its director in 1947.

Hans left Walden to found Stockbridge after acquiring the former Gilded Age estate of Daniel Rhodes Hanna, an eleven-hundred-acre parcel of land with several buildings. The very rundown property contained eighteen buildings and twenty-five hundred feet of water frontage on a lake called the Stockbridge Bowl. Of this extensive, largely forested property, which ranged from Stockbridge Mountain’s summit to the Stockbridge Bowl’s shore, about two hundred and fifty acres became the school campus.

The school’s credo, ‘All human beings are created free and equal in dignity and rights,’ was taken from the charter of the United Nations. It was posted for all to see at the entrance of the building and has shaped my life to this day. The United Nations was founded just three years before Stockbridge, and its flag flew on the school campus just below the Stars & Stripes.

‘Tolerance is not enough,’ Hans would often say. ‘There is a kind of condescension in tolerance, and Stockbridge is not a tolerant school. Respect and acceptance of one another are what we are all about.’

From its inception, Stockbridge was run as a nonprofit, radically left-leaning, progressive, co-ed, racially integrated boarding school. Hans made successful efforts through his travels for UNESCO to recruit an international student body. This was during a period when many similar types of residential schools, especially in the surrounding states, were comparatively insular. Most of them frowned upon a coed student body and were ethnically exclusive, which meant only token Blacks or Jews, or none at all, could attend.

Hans was motivated by a powerful vision that grew out of his own experiences. He fashioned Stockbridge more as a social structure than as a traditional academic institution. It had no blueprint; no preconceived, carefully organized plans. Instead, it was a vision of a community where we could grow in understanding and respecting each other, no matter how different we were.

I didn’t realize then that Hans was slowly becoming my surrogate father. I knew that the door to his office was almost invariably open I felt free to walk through that door to ask a question, lodge a complaint, or share my joys and sorrows or fears and frustrations. (I found out many years later that he knew about my sexuality all along, but there was never any indication of this at the time.)

Life at Stockbridge was very informal. We always called our teachers by their first names, including Hans, which helped me see them as sources of wisdom, not authoritarian figures to be feared. It made them feel more equal and open to me.

Our day started at 6:30am with morning ablutions, getting dressed, making our beds, cleaning our rooms, and then heading out to our daily jobs. There was kitchen duty or cleaning the hallways, bathrooms, the classroom building, the main house, dorms, and all the common rooms. There were no janitors, maids, or servants. We took care of everything ourselves.

After breakfast, around 8am, those who were on kitchen duty stayed to clean up, wash the dishes, and set the tables for lunch. The rest of us, students and faculty, would gather in the living room for around twenty minutes of ‘morning music.’ We sat on the stairs, floor, and couches, and there was little or no discussion about the music, no commentary, just stillness while we listened. Occasionally, our music teacher would say something like, ‘We’re going to hear a lot of Bach this week.’ We picked what we listened to on Saturdays, usually jazz or soft rock. It was a period of quiet contemplation, during which I could think my own thoughts and prepare for the day ahead. Once the morning music ended, Hans would stand up and our day would begin.

Classes ran from 8:30 to 10:30am, followed by a break. We had a canteen under the school building where we could get something to drink or snack, and some of us would smoke cigarettes in an unfinished part of the basement that we called the catacombs, which was connected to the canteen by a ‘secret’ door. In the meantime, the faculty huddled over coffee to discuss the day’s plans. Classes convened again at 11am and ended at around 1pm, when we would break for lunch. After lunch, we had more classes until around 3:30, then free time, unless we were part of the crew getting the dinner ready.

Dinner was served at 6pm, and we always wore our dress jackets. We would file into the building’s two dining rooms, which were the old mansion’s dining room and library. The rooms were connected by wooden sliding doors and held eight or ten rectangular tables, arranged in two rows, that each sat six. We stood behind our chairs until Hans arrived and sat down in his customary place at the head of the end corner table, and then we would sit.

I felt safe with Hans, and I always tried to sit at his table, directly to his right. Sometimes, though, he would grab my arm and bang my elbow on the table if I dared put it there.

Every Wednesday evening at 8pm, students, faculty, and administrators gathered in the living room as equals to discuss subjects we learned about in our classes, air our grievances, interests, and frustrations, or share our plans for improving our lives at the school. No topics were off limits, from the latest international crisis to complaints about the food.

The school was about more than reading, writing, and arithmetic. It was an experiment in educational democracy. Hans believed that preparation for living in a democracy should include learning to understand the responsibilities and obligations required, as well as the rights and privileges. A student chaired all the meetings, and the chairmanship was rotated every three weeks.

The meetings were run according to Robert’s Rules Of Order. A few other elected students worked as committee heads who oversaw our work assignments, field trips, sports events, and competitions with other schools. Some of these jobs were distributed according to our interests and skills. The specific purpose of all this was to instill a sense of responsibility for oneself and others. The effect was a strengthening of our sense of community. I felt as if I owned a part of the school.

I remember many intense discussions carrying over from our world-affairs class, like when the USSR launched Sputnik, its first space satellite, or the first rumblings in Vietnam, the Asian flu epidemic, the Little Rock Nine, and the forced integration of elementary schools. I was upset to see a little nine-year-old Black girl being escorted up the steps of her school by a US Marshall on our only school TV, which had three channels—still a vivid memory to this day. There was always to talk about.

Our work jobs were not separate from our academic life. We learned from our work just as we learned from our classes. They were integral to the curriculum. Jobs were assigned and rotated in one-week cycles by our peers. The seniors got the prestigious jobs, like driving the four-by-four army truck to take the garbage to the dump or plowing the snow to clear the roads in the winter. Another prestigious Saturday job involved cutting and dragging timber out of the woods using a red Farmall tractor. We traded the timber for finished lumber that we used to build a new barn (which, years after my time, was converted into apartments for the teachers). Other jobs included cutting the lawns with the gang mower, tilling the vegetable garden, and cutting hay in the spring and summer. I loved working with my hands with the sun on my back, touching nature.

In my senior year, I was on the forestry crew. We were charged with felling trees and hauling logs for firewood and timberusing an ax or a two-man saw. I learned how to select the trees to be cut so that the younger growth would have the sunlight needed to flourish, and I was happy to get to drive the tractors.

After our Saturday jobs were done, we could travel the twelve miles to Pittsfield, the nearest town. This was our free time. However, I usually elected to stick around the school and work onward until dusk. When there was snow on the ground and we couldn’t work outside, we would go skiing at nearby Bousquet Mountain. They had two rope tows and a small T-bar lift. Those were welcome days off.

As a youngster, hearing my sister practicing Bach, Brahms, Beethoven, and Chopin endlessly on the Steinway in our sun parlor, filling our house with classical music, had planted the seeds for my love of music. At Stockbridge, I continued to follow my musical muse. Thanks to my parents, I had a good-quality turntable and an amplifier with a mono speaker. I loved waving my hands in the air, imagining I was conducting the great symphonies. And I liked it loud. To this day, I still listen to classical ‘morning music.’

Academic study was not forgotten, despite Stockbridge’s focus on a nurturing environment. Hans realized that we would have to be taught English, history, math, science, and a foreign language, and he found qualified and creative teachers to do that. But his philosophy was about the whole life, not just an academic education, and how those two were inseparable and intertwined. Hans created his school more as a social structure than a traditional academic institution. Looking back, his ‘school as a social experiment’ may not have been perfect, but it had a high batting average.

Hans wasn’t the only influential teacher and mentor I found at Stockbridge. My English teacher, Berta Rantz, came from the same Russian/Jewish roots as I did. She brought literature alive. I remember reading Milton’s Paradise Lost aloud and discovering the precision of the prose, the meter, rhyme, and musicality the words.

Berta would often say, ‘It is in the fluid relationships of life that there is meaning, not in the finished product.’ How right she was. I’ll also never forget her saying, ‘I have had a very rich and productive life. How? Because I am surrounded by people who want to create.’

Alex Perkins taught history and philosophy and had a very progressive outlook. His approach was based on reason, our common humanity, and standing up for human rights and social justice. Both Alex and Hans came from the living hell of World War II. Alex had served in the British Army and had seen the atrocities of war firsthand. It was from their collective wartime experiences that the school’s educational philosophy came to be. They recognized that moral values are founded on human nature and experience.

We learned about participatory democracy and standing up for human rights and social justice by expanding an open society. Alex taught us that humanity must take responsibility for its own destiny. He was a true humanist. e used to say.

Hans and Alex, one a German and the other an Englishman, their countries bitter enemies in wartime, were brothers in arms. So many Stockbridge students came from different countries, races, religions, and lifestyles, yet we all got along as individuals. Alex gave us a positive, shared view of life. In his classes, we’d read the hard facts that could be found in any decent textbook or newspaper, then would interpret those facts in terms of our current events. It was a way of enlightening us from day to day. He taught history inside the context of what was happening in the present. It was a humanistic approach to the social conditions of our time.

Alex was also the boys’ dorm house parent. He lived in an apartment located at the left end of the dorm building. I spent a lot of time with him, talking about politics, life, and sailing. One of my elective projects was helping him build a thirty-foot cruising sailboat in the school shop. He knew of my experience as a sailor, and we shared a love for sailing and the sea. The boat was a reverse sheer design and was big enough to sleep four. He built it using an advanced laminating technique. Basically, it was glued together. It took a lot of sanding and finishing to complete the hull.

The year after I graduated, I trailered Alex’s boat with my Land Rover to Boothbay, Maine, and then we sailed through Somes Sound to Boston. One night, we tied up at a floating dock with a big lobster tank and had fresh lobster for dinner. It was unforgettable.

Clyde Dunne lived next door to the school on what was once his family’s dairy farm. The property line was a hundred feet behind the boy’s dormitory. Clyde was a patient and peaceful man. I loved spending time with him and his dog, Blondy, in the cab of his well-worn Chevy pickup truck, which also served as his office. Clyde was a natural-born farmer. It was in his blood. He had stopped farming several years before I got to Stockbridge, and he and his wife Mary worked full-time at the school. He oversaw everything that had to do with the outdoors: forestry, construction, farming, vehicles, heavy building maintenance like painting and carpentry, and all the professional contractors working on campus building the new gym and barn.

Clyde may not have officially been a teacher at the school, but he certainly fulfilled that role for me. He taught me how to work with my hands. We did a lot of the grunt work, mostly on Saturdays. We dug trenches for the footings for a new gym building and worked with the carpenters to frame the new barn. He was the one who taught me how to drive a tractor, mow the lawns, and till the big vegetable garden.

To this day, I still feel that, somehow, I owned a part of Stockbridge, and that Stockbridge owned a part of me.

By the end of the eleventh grade, Stockbridge had left me energized and full of optimism. I returned to Great Neck for the summer. Everyone was happy to have me home. With my father on the road, my mother working every day in New York, and my brother, sister, and me all away from home, the house had been an empty nest, which was especially hard on my grandmother.

But sitting home, vegetating in Great Neck, and keeping my grandmother company for the next eight weeks was not going to be an option for me. The fire Stockbridge lit was burning bright. I’d inherited my brother Don’s sailboat, the Pixie, which was sitting unused and forlorn in the garage. She was an old-timer, built by the Dodge brothers in 1938. A centerboard sloop with a V-shaped hull built from solid mahogany, which we kept beautifully varnished over the years. She was rugged, forgiving, and a beautiful handful to sail.

I had the brilliant idea to organize a small sailing camp at the Great Neck Estates Park dock, within walking distance of the house. I created a junior version of my Knickerbocker days. It was a great way to earn some dollars.

I invited my Stockbridge school buddy Tim Barnett to be a part of my sailing school, and we dropped anchor together in Great Neck. Our self-created jobs started with sanding, varnishing, painting, and getting the Pixie ready for the summer. It was a continuation of my work on Alex Perkins’s sailboat at Stockbridge. I put up a sign at the park, and with that and some word of mouth, we ended up with six boys and girls for six weeks. It was the first time in my life that I was the teacher, not the student. I learned a lot by teaching the kids how to sail.

When the summer ended, we put the Pixie back on her cradle in the garage, and I headed back to Stockbridge a week or two early. I felt more at home there than I did in Great Neck. School got underway in September, and I soon settled back into my old routine for my senior year. I lived in the same dorm room with the same roommate, Peter Van Dyke, and had the same teachers, classes, and work jobs. The fight for civil rights in the South was the focus of our current events classes and Wednesday-night school meetings.

The US Congress passed the Civil Rights Act of 1957, authorizing the Justice Department to investigate cases of Black people being denied voting rights in the South. The Supreme Court also ruled in favor of the bus boycotters in Montgomery, Alabama, who had successfully integrated the city’s buses. All of this was met with passionate resistance in the South.

President Eisenhower had to have federal troops escort Black students to classes. Then, Dr. Martin Luther King and Ralph Abernathy founded the Southern Christian Leadership Conference, with King elected its first president. We watched the fight for civil rights and desegregation in the South happen before our eyes. It was a mind-bending big deal.

NASA, the National Aeronautics And Space Administration, was formed in 1958 and launched its first satellite, Explorer 1, that same year. In our classes, we were planning voyages back and forth to the moon and on to Mars. Stereo music recordings became available for the first time. I heard my first stereo record in a shop in Pittsfield one Saturday afternoon. Little did I know that this was the beginning of me experiencing how technology drives the creation of music. Our days were full of learning about the world’s big picture and what was happening around us. Somehow, it made me forget about myself.

Graduation day was on Saturday during the second week of June. My parents drove up from Great Neck for their first and only visit to Stockbridge. The ceremony was outside, just behind the main house. There were sixteen of us in our class. I remember seeing the smiles on my parents’ faces. Then Hans gave us our diplomas, and it was all over. I thought I’d feel some monumental shift, but I didn’t feel any different.

Later that day, I remember sitting quietly on the floor of my dorm room with my back against the wall, facing the door, waiting for my dad to bring the car around so I could put my belongings in it. I was taking it all in, trying to embed it in my memory. To this day, I can still see it all clearly in my mind. It remains unforgettable.

The way I live my life and my worldview today is a result of what I experienced at Stockbridge. Learning how to work communally or on my own in academic subjects, cutting hay, plowing snow, or doing kitchen duty made me a part of the place and the group. The school had become my family. I learned skills and teamwork and how to make something mine. I was given a chance to grow in any direction that I wanted to. Today, I remain politically active, informed, and humanistic, just like my teachers.

Those lessons I learned at Stockbridge about human rights and racial equality became my credo, fitting neatly into Stevie Wonder’s worldview, which would be a crucial element in our relationship. We journeyed on radically different roads, but we were headed to the same place.

Chapter 3

Finding my voice

By the end of my senior year at Stockbridge, I already knew all about the music school at Tanglewood, the Boston Symphony’s summer home, and I fervently wanted to be a part of it. It was just a mile down the road from the school. With Hans Maeder’s permission, I continued to live in the boys’ dorm at Stockbridge. I worked there over the summer cutting the lawns and taking care of the grounds in exchange for room and board. I brought the Pixie over from Great Neck and put her on the lake. It was perfect.

Maestro Charles Munch ran Tanglewood and worked with Leonard Bernstein and Aaron Copland to shape the school’s programs. I was lucky to have had the incredible gift of learning and performing onstage under their direction.

Tanglewood was not a school in the strict sense. It didn’t offer courses, degrees, or credits. There were no students striving for grades. Instead, it presented a unique opportunity for a summer of living and working in music. It was about creating a musical community and playing together in groups and ensembles.

The choral department was headed by Dr. Hugh Ross. The students came through referrals from major music schools like the Manhattan School, Juilliard, the New England Conservatory, and the Curtis Institute.

I applied right after my graduation from Stockbridge and auditioned for Dr. Ross on my first day. He sat at the piano while I sang some scales and sight-read a shaky rendition of ‘Every Valley’ from Handel’s Messiah. I was sweating bullets. Compared with these gifted and more senior music students, I certainly was not a prodigy. I guess I was lucky that Dr. Ross saw and heard something in me and accepted me to study as an auditor in both the Tanglewood Choir and the Festival Chorus.

The primary mission of the choir was to serve as a testing group—or more accurately, victims—for aspiring conducting students. We sang every day and went through piles of both music and conductors. I actually conducted the choir several times. It was a revelation, and a far cry from air conducting those mono records in my dorm room at Stockbridge.

I remember performing Beethoven’s Ninth Symphony under Dr. Munch’s direction. We also sang Aaron Copland’s ‘The Tender Land’ under the composer’s direction. There were performances under the batons of Leonard Bernstein and Pierre Monteux, and the Boston Symphony Orchestra performed at Tanglewood’s concert theater, the Koussevitzky Music Shed. Its acoustics were remarkable.

For me, it was music twenty-four-seven. I sang every day. I felt my voice becoming more controlled and supple, my confidence in my musicianship was growing by leaps and bounds, and I was making new friends. It was a time of growth, joy, and pleasure.

During the morning it was choir rehearsal with the conducting students. Some were gifted and others not so much. On some afternoons there were Festival Chorus rehearsals with the BSO. My experience of performing with world-class conductors and the Boston Symphony set my bar high. I didn’t realize, then, that I was supercharging my life.

Anytime the Boston Symphony was rehearsing, you could stop by, sit in the front rows, and listen to Munch, Bernstein, Copland, others at work starting and stopping the orchestra, explaining, cajoling, and repeating certain sections again and again. We’d then attend the actual performances of the works in the shed. Although I couldn’t have imagined it at the time, I was seeing some of the greatest musical minds ever, modeling what would later become my work as I evolved into a pop recording engineer, synthesist, and music producer.

Growing up in Great Neck and during my years at Stockbridge, there was never any discussion about being gay or straight. My relationships were always furtive and secret. I knew that being outed as a homosexual meant instant ridicule and discrimination, even in a bastion of progressive thought like Stockbridge.

At Tanglewood, all of that changed. I discovered that I wasn’t alone and in fact learned that many of the faculty and my fellow students were also gay. It wasn’t a secret, and it wasn’t a sin. It was then that I finally opened up and accepted my homosexuality. I was out of the closet and at the beginning of understanding who I was as a man and a musician. I was finding my orbit and creating a blueprint for the rest of my life.

The Seven Hills Inn was where we went to unwind after our day’s work at Tanglewood. A music-loving middle-class Jewish couple from New York, Larry and Sophie Howitt, owned the place. They had bought it in the early 50s and converted the once-grand mansion, built in the early 1900s, into an inn. It had a time-worn feeling of elegance and old money, and its fifteen rooms came variously appointed with heavy velvet drapes, old oriental rugs, overstuffed couches, fireplaces, leaded windows, and an eclectic collection of furniture from colonial to contemporary. Next door was a less elegant and less expensive two-story motel-type building with around thirty rooms.

Sophie was very outgoing and ran the show. She loved music and Tanglewood, so the inn catered to celebrity musicians and those music lovers who attended the Tanglewood concerts. As I recollect, Larry was always behind the scenes, overseeing the staff or the kitchen, doing the heavy lifting.

What set Seven Hills apart was that almost all of the servers, bellhops, and front-of-house staff were music students working summer jobs at the inn, and many of them were gay. In the evenings, they would perform around a beautiful grand piano in the living room along with other students from Tanglewood. I remember Seymour Schoenberg, a very animated conducting student who worked with Sophie as an impresario, organizing the evenings’ performances and accompanying many of the performers. He could sit at the piano and sight-read anything put in front of him.

There was a small bar and lounge under the dining hall in a basement that opened onto a terrace. An ad hoc trio comprised of piano, acoustic bass, and drums would often perform there. I played drums with them on many occasions. Sweets and refreshments were served buffet-style in the dining room. You didn’t have to be a hotel guest to attend the evening musicals. Frequent visitors included Leonard Bernstein and his tribe, plus Beverly Sills, Dave Brubeck, and many of the Boston Symphony players.

After lights out and the guests went to bed, there was romance and fervent, sexual shenanigans going on well into the night, and I was a part of it. What a summer it was, filled with music—mellow, innocent, passionate, and sweaty. It was over too quickly, but those days also had a profound impact on me, and they still live on in my memory.

That fall, I was accepted into Allegheny College in Meadville, Pennsylvania. It looked picture-perfect, but that impression didn’t last long. I found myself in America’s conservative backwaters, seven hundred miles from New York, across the Allegheny Mountains, in a small town, ninety miles west of Pittsburgh. On my first day in the boy’s dorm, my new roommate said, ‘You are the first Jew I have ever met.’

It was 1959, and in my innocence and optimism, I thought that the school’s Methodist roots wouldn’t make any difference. I was wrong. I soon found myself back in the closet, feeling very out of place, alone, isolated, and celibate. It didn’t take me long to realize that I was no longer in the humanistic world of reason and acceptance I had grown into at Stockbridge and Tanglewood. Both Jewish and gay, I was ‘different’ again.

The only shining light for me was the Allegheny choir. It was the one area I brimmed with confidence. After my season at Tanglewood, singing under some of the world’s greatest conductors, I was in fine voice and ready to deliver.

Dr. Morton J. Luvass was the choirmaster and conductor. The choir was small, with thirty-five to forty-five singers, and everybody had to pull their weight. Nevertheless, we tackled some of the most challenging choral music, including newer compositions and historic works spanning five centuries.

That one bright spot of the choir aside, however, I was struggling academically, and by the end of the second semester, I knew that if I had stayed at Allegheny, I would have flunked out. My only regret was leaving the choir behind. The rest, not so much.

Leaving was the right decision. Allegheny felt like a foreign land. I loaded up my car and headed back east with my tail between my legs. I felt relieved and defeated at the same time when I landed back my family home in Great Neck. I sat there for weeks, chain-smoking Pall Mall Reds and watching hours and hours of television, waiting for lightning to strike.

God must have had his hand on my shoulder when I decided to call Dr. Ross, my teacher from the choral program at Tanglewood. (I never called him by his first name.) I shared my tale of woe with him, and he invited me to visit him in New York. He sat at the piano, put some music in front of me, and, once again, I sang for him. I don’t know exactly what he saw in me, but I was grateful that he saw something, because he was about to help raise the curtain on the next chapter of my life.

I soon learned what a heavyweight Dr. Ross was in the New York classical music scene. He was a tenured professor at the Manhattan School Of Music, where he conducted and taught the chorus, the madrigal group, and a music history class. He was also the choirmaster of the Schola Cantorum, the choir of the New York Philharmonic. Although he had been in the US for years, he remained an Englishman and would never lose his very clipped British accent. He had a great sense of humor and this habit of interjecting a little hum sometimes between his words or phrases. ‘Hmm, let’s start at bar sixty-four, mmm,’ he’d say, or ‘mm, watch your intonation at bar twenty-four, mmmm.’ e became a light in my life.

Dr. Ross invited me to become a member of the Schola Cantorum. It was like the Tanglewood choir and the Boston Symphony on steroids. The Schola was a professional adult ensemble, with many of its singers being chorus members for years. It was not Tanglewood students singing together for an eight-week summer season. The Schola was the real deal. My vocal skills, intonation, sense of ensemble, and performance were all brought to a new level. We were even paid to rehearse and perform—the first time I got paid to sing. I even had a union card.

Rehearsals took place in a large studio on the second floor of Steinway Hall, just across the street from Carnegie Hall. Leonard Bernstein, unassuming and casually dressed, would come by to rehearse us for upcoming performances with the orchestra. He remembered me from Tanglewood and our social evenings at the Seven Hills Inn.

Walking through the stage door in Carnegie Hall to perform with the orchestra filled me with a sense of awe. My head was exploding. It was almost impossible to be professionally nonchalant.

[MUSIC CUE + SFX - Beethoven “Gloria”]

Our most memorable performance was singing ‘Gloria’ from Beethoven’s ‘Missa Solemnis,’ conducted by Bernstein at the concert that opened the Lincoln Center For The Performing Arts on September 23, 1962.

At that point, my true commitment to the profession of music began, thanks to Dr. Ross. I knew I was blessed to have him as my teacher and mentor. He led me to find the insights and skills that were a turning point in my young life.

That fall, I became a full-time voice student at the Manhattan School. I was assigned an opera coach, Herta Glotz, a mezzo-soprano who performed at the Met. She was a true diva, always perfectly coiffed, and wore a lot of makeup. She was very formal, almost to the point of being intimidating. One of the first songs she put in front of me was George Frederic Handel’s ‘Ombra Mai Fu.’

‘Robert, Robert, Robert,’ she would say in her descending, sotto voce, ‘sing into your mask. Imagine your voice rising up and projecting outward from between your eyes.’

‘Yes, Madame Glotz,’ I would answer. Then she would tap me on my forehead with her finger. It was a different experience from my lessons with Dr. Ross, as he was a choirmaster, not an opera singer.

Studying opera with Madame Glotz opened the door to many incredible evenings for me at the Metropolitan Opera House on Broadway, between 39th and 40th Street, a glorious, well-aged Italianate pile built around the turn of the century. I felt lucky to have the opportunity to spend some time there. Unfortunately, it was torn down several years later when the opera company moved to its new home at Lincoln Center.

We inhabited the score desks that were located at the very top of the balcony. They were arranged in a single arc, tall and sloped like an easel, each with a small lamp. You could sit on a stool or stand and almost touch the ceiling. It was quite a view, looking down onto the stage and orchestra pit. Our standing joke was that we had to take air-sickness pills to be up there. I often attended with my buddy from Tanglewood and the Seven Hills, Seymour Schoenberg. We would bring our music, and Sy would bring his baton for the evening’s performance. All the students would follow the music and air-conduct along with the orchestra. It was a sight to behold.

Music students never had to pay to attend the Met. Afterward, we would all go out to eat, drink, and comment on—or more often criticize—the singers and the production. I was out of the closet once again, and what a relief it was.

The opera world was a completely new world for me. My background was in oratorio and ensemble singing. I’d never considered becoming an opera singer. The thought of finding myself at the front of the stage, a soloist, a thousand eyes on me, alone, simply wasn’t me. By the end of the year, I concluded that I wasn’t as good as everybody else. I had the vocal training, a budding technique, and the insight as a musician, but I could not, as a singer, hold a candle to the great voices like Caruso, Corelli, Botticelli, Pavarotti. I realized that with my union membership, I could always be a professional chorister, but I didn’t have the vocal skills to go further.

At my parents’ urging, I enrolled at the C.W. Post Campus at Long Island University and took another stab at higher education, hoping I could figure out what to do with my life. I continued commuting into Manhattan to rehearse and perform with the Schola choir. I intended to study psychology and sociology and get a BA, but once I started my studies, I discovered the school’s Little Theatre and its student performances, and from then on, that was all I could think about.

After I joined the drama department, I had to take a basic acting class and perform onstage in front of my classmates. My acting debut was in a student play called The Violent Eruption Of The Volcano Krakatoa. I played a sea captain on a mission to save trapped islanders. I wore a nineteenth-century uniform, including a cocked hat, and carried a model of a sailing ship. ‘Krakatoa’ was a plaster model set on a felt-covered table. Unfortunately, I flubbed my lines all over the place and felt like a melting ice-cream cone. It reminded me of my experiences at the opera.

I concluded that not only would I never become a great opera singer, I wouldn’t become a great actor, either. However, I had become consumed with expressing my imagination in stagecraft, theatrical design, and stage lighting, so instead of acting, I ended up designing the sets and the lighting and running the show from the lighting booth. I could set the scene, place the written word into a physical place, and fix my mind’s eye to the sense of a story in that space. I found myself totally at home, and I felt powerful.

I learned about drawing to scale, vanishing points, perspective, visual composition, and reading blueprints. I learned to operate the stage lighting using the electro-mechanical lighting console at the back of the theater. It had an enormous series of large levers with electrical rheostats that controlled the brightness of each lighting circuit and instrument on stage. When it was in use, it had this electrical hum, and it got very warm to the touch. I designed the lighting plot, the wiring, the gel colors, the mood, and the environment. Then, I set and followed the lighting cues as the show evolved. I felt driven at the lighting console with the show under my hands.

At the time, I couldn’t have imagined how this training in stagecraft might launch me into a future of sound engineering, synthesis, cinematography, and studio design. Or how, a few years later, I would meet and start a lifelong friendship and creative relationship with the man who built Jimi Hendrix’s Electric Lady Studio, John Storyk. John and I would work on many studio construction projects together, and he would help put the finishing touches on the synthesizer that would become the real game changer. All I knew at the time was that I had found my place behind the curtain, where I could flex my creative muscles, out of the view of the audience.
