Chapter 4

The East Village . . . via Uncle Sam

Not only did a lifelong love of music and sailing take hold during my teen years, but I also found a passion for photography. My mother’s older brother, Uncle Philip, was the designated photographer for all of our family events, and he gifted me my first camera for my Bar Mitzvah. It was an Argus, a competitor to the Kodak Brownie, with a fixed lens that used 120 film. Retailing for $25 at the time, it was quite a luxurious gift. I was hooked.

My school buddy David Marks also got a camera when we were both about thirteen. We’d head out and snap pictures of the same stuff, just to see how differently we saw things. We got our photos developed at Gilliar’s Drug Store in Great Neck. Although David and I drifted apart when I went away to Stockbridge, he grew up to be quite a fine photographer. And I never stopped taking pictures either.

In 1963, while I was still at C.W. Post, the Vietnam War was in its early stages. The US was obsessed with stopping communism at any cost, and I could feel Uncle Sam pointing his finger in my direction. I had a choice: get drafted or enlist. If I waited to get drafted, it was anyone’s guess where I’d end up. If I enlisted, I’d get to choose my own Military Occupational Specialty. I learned from the recruiter that one of my choices could be something creative, maybe even something artistic that I already loved: photography, or more specifically combat photography.

I quit my studies at C.W. Post, enlisted in the US Army, and signed up to be trained as a combat motion picture photographer. I completed eight weeks of basic training and then sixteen weeks of advanced training at Fort Dix, New Jersey. The army school was intense and focused on both still photography and cinematography. I loved it was consumed by it. There were no classes about film history or aesthetics. It was all hands-on camera techniques in the field, film processing, and some simple editing. Changing film in the field had to be done in a black changing bag with hand sleeves, because the unprocessed film was so light-sensitive. Even the smallest bit of light would ruin the film.

I learned about lenses, f-stops, and focal length. The handheld movie cameras had spring drives. You could get around thirty seconds of run time for each wind, so you had to be careful about what you were shooting. There were two combat film cameras, the Filmo for 16mm and the Eyemo for 35mm, which could each hold about three minutes of film per load.

There was never a second take for a combat photographer under battlefield conditions. We practiced our camerawork on new recruits undergoing basic training. I always tried to tell the whole story by editing in camera while shooting. After all, you couldn’t choose your shots on the battlefield. A lot of editing and cropping had to be done in the moment, while you were filming. I learned how to fill the frame with action and decide the sequence of images and angles that would best tell the story in real time, the way an editor would in postproduction.

After finishing advanced training, I traveled on a troop ship to Hamburg, Germany, and then was transported to Kaiserslautern, near Stuttgart, where I was stationed with the VII Corps. Being an enlisted man was very hard to take. The routine was a grind. I was sent on some interesting assignments with my Filmo 16mm combat camera, but most of the time I was handed a big Graflex still camera with a pocket full of popping flashbulbs and spent my time taking pictures of generals and dignitaries drinking, dining, shaking hands, and back-slapping at one event or another. I was very adept in the darkroom, however, and I ended up processing and printing much of the unit’s photo output.

Once I was established and in my routine on the base, I was able to get overnight passes. I’d take the streetcar and head out to downtown Stuttgart. By chance, I met several of the principal male dancers from the Stuttgart State Opera Ballet in a gay bar near the Staatsoper. They were physically beautiful, graceful, intelligent, and very gay. They invited me to hang out with them in the opera house’s canteen, which was where they spent their time offstage during the performances. Most of the time they were still in costume, eating or relaxing until they went back onstage. My off-base overnight passes soon became my passport to a secret life with some of the most beautiful, virile men in the world.

I started bringing my 70mm still camera with me and discovered my true passion: the military had unwittingly trained me to become an excellent ballet photographer. Thanks to Uncle Sam, I could use my army cameras and the base photo lab to develop my work after hours. And, because of my classical musical background, I could study the score and story for every ballet performance and shoot during the performances from the prompter’s box, directly at the front of the stage. The dancers liked the pictures because they made their legs look very long.

I loved the exquisite nobility of ballet. I even started taking a few dance classes to better understand their movements and techniques. I attended as many opera performances as I could. They always had a promenade after the performances in a beautiful reception hall, replete with sparkling crystal chandeliers, hors d’oeuvres, wine, and champagne. Everybody was well dressed, strolling counterclockwise around the elegant foyer. I was making new friends, talking about the performances, and learning to speak a little German.

The opera house itself was a glorious baroque/rococo stately pile. It had been bombed during World War II and then completely restored. I think this where I wondered for the first time, What’s a nice Jewish boy from Great Neck doing in a place like this? But I loved it.For me, it was music, ballet, photography, and my sexuality, all combined.

After a year of living a double life as a straight army combat photographer by day and a gay dance photographer by night, I finally had to face my sexual issues. I wasn’t asked, but I had to tell. The stress of keeping my two worlds apart was just too much. The minute my commanding officer heard my story, I was granted an immediate general discharge.

I stayed in Stuttgart for another year after my discharge. I found a small apartment in the attic of a private home on the Neue Weinsteige, just above the city center, and built a small photography lab. I bought a good camera and a car, a used Porsche Cabriolet, and started my journey as a freelance photographer in the city and occasionally in Munich. The drive to Munich with the top down and the pedal to the metal on the Autobahn was unforgettable.

My army training had paid off. I was earning enough as a photographer to make ends meet (with a bit of help from home). My folks wanted me back in the States, though, and now, with the skills and confidence I had gained training for combat photography, I felt more than ready to head back and start making movies.

I was twenty-six when I returned to New York with my cameras and the used red Porsche I’d bought in Stuttgart. I settled into an old railroad flat on the top floor of a tenement building on East 5th Street, at the heart of the emerging hippie scene, full of the druggy haze of pot and LSD, as well as peyote, hashish, and speed. There was always weed around, and I loved it. You could smell it in the air. It seemed that everybody was smoking every day, including me. Our culture was evolving from the Beat Generation to the Hip Generation. It was all flower power, pot, and psychedelics, and I felt like I belonged.

I had grand plans to go into the film business. My naïve strategy was to start by importing theatrical short subjects from some of the filmmakers I had met in Germany and offer them for sale and distribution to exhibitors in the USA. ntertainmentwhere I could let my hair down, figuratively and literally, and return to being the liberal, free-thinking self of my youth.

I found an office in a converted catering hall on East 5th Street with a shooting stage and a 35mm screening room. The neighborhood was rundown and grubby, but it was slowly mutating from an old Jewish community into a hippie paradise. I soon discovered that there was much going on there: music, theater, art, and social change—a creative spirit that would influence my choices as a filmmaker and, ultimately, as a recording engineer and a music producer.

Around the corner from my office, the theater that would become the Fillmore East was taking shape. I watched as an old burlesque house and Yiddish theater morphed into the church of rock’n’roll. I remember walking into the theater a few days before the first show, and all the old seats were piled high on the floor. A huge model airplane was suspended from the ceiling. I met Bill Graham and his right-hand man, Kip Cohen, and watched them set up a killer sound system for their first concert and we smoked lots of weed together.

Directly across the street was the La MaMa Experimental Theatre, one of the first Off-Off-Broadway theaters. It was on the second floor, over a One-Hour Dry Cleaners. The theater was run by an extraordinary impresario, Ellen Stewart, who established La MaMa as a haven for under-represented artists to experiment with new work without the pressures of commercial success. With my experience doing theatre at CW Post, I soon found myself building stage sets and designing lighting. And I got to run around with some of the playwrights who would become living legends in theater and film, including Lanford Wilson, Leonard Melfi, and Tom O’Horgan (who went on to direct Hair on Broadway), to name a few.

Despite the peace and love vibe, it was still a really rough neighborhood, grimy and run down. One night, around three in the morning, Tom and I were heading home. We turned the corner onto 3rd Street from 2nd Avenue and right into a couple of guys with guns. One of them shoved his gun into my face and demanded our cash. I’ll never forget looking down the barrel of that gun. I hadn’t even been at that end of a gun when I was in the army. They took our wallets and left us shaken but otherwise unharmed.

I spent many hours with Ellen, just the two of us, sitting on the stairs outside the theater during the performances. I had become an active member of the underground theater scene at La MaMa. I was in my element, and I was loving every second of it. Somehow, I had once again landed in a hotbed of music and theater, right in the middle of the city’s most vibrant, fresh, and exciting scene. The Fillmore and La MaMa, my apartment, office, and screening room, were all within one city block.

One night, on way home to my East Village digs, I met Bobby Anderson at the Ninth Circle, an artsy gay bar on Christopher Street frequented by some of the gay intelligentsia. Bobby had just been discharged from the hospital, having been seriously ill with hepatitis. In the hospital, he had been told almost every day that he wasn’t going to make it. He had lost his apartment and everything in it. I said I was looking for someone to take care of my apartment, clean, cook, look after the place—basically, to be my very own majordomo. I offered him free rent in exchange for his services, and he jumped at the opportunity.

Bobby lived with me for three and a half years. He furnished the apartment and slowly turned my ramshackle six-room railroad flat into a fabulous hippie heaven. He decorated every room differently: a psychedelic room, a Victorian room, a Georgian room. The kitchen was outstanding. He painted the walls to look like an American flag and the refrigerator to look like a Coke vending machine. I loved what he did.

I often said that we should start a magazine called Better Homes And Garbage to feature his work, because he found pretty much everything he used to furnish the place on the street. The house became a salon, and it was often filled with people from Andy Warhol’s Factory, Max’s Kansas City, musicians, filmmakers, and the theater folk from La MaMa.

I would soon become a filmmaker, but before that could get off the ground, music began calling me again. This was the beginning of my career of changing careers, following a dual track that would ultimately lead me to the synthesizer and electronica.

It was a hot, humid New York afternoon, and it was just starting to get dark. Run-down tenements lined both sides of East 5th Street, with rows of trash cans chained to the railings, all dented and overflowing onto the sidewalk. Sitting on one of the trash cans was a long-haired hippie with a guitar, singing to anybody who would listen. I noticed that his guitar only had five strings. We struck up a conversation, and he said he didn’t have the money to buy new strings. He started to play some of his songs. They had a Beatles/bubblegum-pop vibe. I liked what I heard, and I invited him to my place to play some more and tell me his story.

His name was Timmy Harrison, but he went by Tim Dulaine. He told me he was sixteen and had ended up in New York from Texas. I told him that I thought he had something and to go home to Texas, finish writing his songs, and then come back and see me. (At twenty-six, I figured I was a big shot.) I didn’t think much more about the encounter after that, as I was totally engrossed in pursuing my dream of a career in film. Then, about a year later, there was a knock at my door. It was late in the evening, and it was raining cats and dogs. Timmy had returned from Texas with his guitar in hand, hair all wet and tangled. He said he would take me up on my offer and was ready to start working with me.

I discovered that the multitalented Timmy could play keyboards as well as guitar, and he had formed a band called Buckwheat. It would be another six months before his posse arrived from Texas, but when they did, I agreed to manage and produce them and let Timmy sleep behind blue velvet curtains in my studio. The rest of the band found an inexpensive apartment on East 90th Street, where they could live and write. They commuted between their apartment and my production company for rehearsals. They needed transportation for their guitars and equipment, so I loaned them the red Porsche I had brought back from Germany. I had no use for car in the East Village and tended to leave it back at my parents’ house in Great Neck. Sadly, one night when it was parked in front of my building, some set it on fire. Looking back, I realize now how insensitive it was to park a German car in a neighborhood populated by refugees from the Holocaust and the terrors of World War II.

After three or four months of writing and rehearsing, Buckwheat were ready. We booked a few shows at the notorious Cafe Wha?, which quickly led to a record deal with Super K Records, a subsidiary of Buddah Records. We were off and running. We recorded the album Pure Buckwheat Honey at Broadway Recording Studios. It was produced by me and engineered by Pat Jacques, the studio’s owner. Pat was a first-class engineer and soon became my mentor, teaching me the ways of a big recording console.

I hired an old friend from my student days at Tanglewood, John Corigliano, to compose, arrange, and conduct the string recording sessions, which added an air of class. It made the record stand out from the other bubblegum-pop records of the era.

I loved producing Timmy, but I was already committed to the film business, and I had some funding to do it. I just needed customers. I joined up with a young hotshot cinematographer, Don Cohen, who was working in the same building on 5th Street where I had set up my office, shooting stage, and screening room. He was already established and had a regular paying clientele. We became partners and moved our business uptown to a new studio space on 47th Street, between 5th and 6th Avenue, in the heart of the diamond district, which was populated by intensely orthodox Jewish diamond merchants and gem cutters. They all wore long black frock coats and fur-trimmed hats and had long beards and sideburns, and they clutched their goods in their briefcases. They would gather at lunchtime and do business at Keat’s Deli, which was on the first floor of our new building. Keats was an exact copy of the 2nd Ave Deli across the street from the Fillmore and my downtown digs. I realized that I had exchanged one Jewish neighborhood with another.

We named our venture Centaur FilmsElevenLabs because I felt that the mythical creatureElevenLabs—half man, half horseElevenLabs—was heroicElevenLabs. Centaur was on the seventh floor. It consisted of a twenty-five-by-thirty-foot insert stage, a small control room, and several offices. We made one office into an edit bay and the other rooms into production offices. I got the big executive office at the end of the hall and furnished it with oriental rugs, drapes, and some fancy furniture. At twenty-eight years old, I felt immortal. I had become a film mogul, or so I thought.

Our first legit project at Centaur was to create a short-form film for Paramount Pictures called Press On Regardless. It was about racing catamarans on Long Island Sound. It rained on the shoot day, and there was very little wind. Nonetheless, Don and I got enough footage to make a gentle and poetic short subject, about fifteen minutes long. Paramount was happy, and we were off to a good start.

Next, we planned to make a low-budget ‘sexploitation’ film for Chelly Wilson called Stripped And Strapped. Wilson owned a chain of soft porn houses. She was an eccentric character with unkempt hair, a raspy smoker’s voice, and a chronic cough. There were always full ashtrays on her desk, and the film posters in her office were yellowed from the smoke.

Chelly had become a key player in New York’s adult film industry and operated several adult movie theaters, including two of Times Square’s most famous, the Eros and the Adonis. Making a film for her would be a great way to get my training wheels as a legit producer of a theatrical film and she always paid in cash.

We agreed on a budget of $47,500. This would not only give me some real-time experience in filmmaking but also solve a serious problem. At the time, you couldn’t just walk in the door of a major studio and expect a job. To get work, you had to be a member of the International Alliance Of Theatrical Stage Employees (IATSE), the union for cinematographers. However, you couldn’t be a member of IATSE unless you had a job. It was a Catch-22. Working for Chelly would be a way to open that door.

As fate would have it, however, I never made that soft porn movie for Chelly. Just as we were set to begin, I found something more exciting—something my partner Don wasn’t going to stick around for.

Chapter 5

Ciao! baby

I was commuting daily via the 7th Avenue subway to and from my apartment on East 5th Street. Most nights, I’d stop for dinner at the Tor Restaurant in the West Village on the corner of Greenwich Avenue and Christopher Street. One night, by chance, I met Charles ‘Chuck’ Wein there. Francis, a waiter at the Tor, introduced us. ‘I think you both have a lot in common,’ he said. A gross understatement.

Chuck was Andy Warhol’s right-hand man. He was the Harvard graduate who had discovered the beautiful trust-fund baby, Edie Sedgwick, at Radcliffe College, brought her to New York, and introduced her to Warhol. I had known of Warhol, his Factory, and the emerging psychedelic movement before meeting Chuck. Now, I was invited inside those circles.

Andy and the Factory folk used to fawn over Chuck. He was their god—their guide to the underworld, spinner of tales, manipulator of rhetoric, grand astrologer, and seer of the ‘white light.’ He was Andy’s blonde-haired, blue-eyed favorite, and I suspect that Andy was sleeping with him as well.

A few days after we met, Chuck and his posse paid me a visit at Centaur with a proposal. They wanted to make a film together. They envisioned a verité underground movie with the beautiful, well-bred but troubled Edie, who was now established as one of Warhol’s biggest superstars. Chuck would write the script and direct the film. He said that he and his collaborator, Genevieve Charbon, had a cast and crew in place. I would be the film’s producer, and Centaur would be its production company.

I said yes immediately. I was fascinated by the denizens of the Factory, and I wanted to be a part of it all. We were going to combine the dirt, grit, and harsh realism of Warhol’s arthouse films with professional equipment and mainstream promotion to make what I called the first ‘aboveground/underground’ movie: Ciao! Manhattan.

It was then that I also met David Weisman, Chuck’s enabler and co-conspirator, known to his friends as Raka. Raka was totally under the spell of Chuck and Andy. He was very glib but also very brilliant. He spoke Japanese, French, and Italian and worked as an illustrator and art director, designing movie posters for the likes of Federico Fellini and Otto Preminger. I was very impressed with him.

When Chuck saw my studio, I think he saw an opportunity to separate himself from the Factory, Andy was already beginning to move on from Edie and her people and now propping up his next film idol, Ingrid Superstar. I was willing to offer Chuck a weekly salary—something the Factory didn’t—and the promise of getting legit exposure.

Looking back, I realize that Edie was one of the first in an evolving line of people who owed their celebrity to Andy, only to be used and discarded when their usefulness and glamor ceased, just like his disposable Campbell’s Soup cans and Brillo boxes. It was as if the Factory heartlessly drained the tragic lives of those in and around it.

What I did like, though, was Warhol’s vérité style of filmmaking. It was very much the documentary approach that I had been taught in my army days as a combat photographer. This technique was made possible by the new lightweight cameras that had recently become available.

These weren’t like the films you’d see every day. They didn’t have scripts.There was no research. Sets weren’t constructed specifically for shots. The actors weren’t required to go through lengthy or frequent rehearsals. This new form of filmmaking drew much of its inspiration the moment itself. The actors arrived on the set with only the subject in mind, which they then elaborated on, always following their imaginations. Nothing was planned. Scenes happened almost by accident.

Andy was the undisputed leader of the movement. Edie was one of the artists who established herself in Andy’s firmament, though interestingly, she never appeared in an actual feature for him. I first met her on the Saturday before Easter, March 25, 1967. Chuck and Raka brought her to my office along with the cinematographer John Palmer (who we called Lunar). Edie was already a big deal in the press, and I was all wound up about meeting her. She arrived with Paul America, a strange, movie-star-handsome, zombie-like guru and meth dealer who was living with her at the ChelseaDescript Hotel. He was a fellow Warhol superstar, having starred in My Hustler, and, as it turned out, he would co-star with Edie in Ciao!

The scene in my office was actually very uptight. Chuck and Raka were hovering around Edie, keeping her in the shadows, not letting her get too close to my desk. She was dressed to the nines with heavy eye makeup, earrings, furs—the works. She was stunning. In my naïveté, I didn’t realize that she was stoned out of her mind and the boys didn’t want me to see her true drugged-out condition, as I learned later from Raka.

I presented her with a check for $800 and a contract, which she signed on the spot. She said it was the first time she had been paid to be in a movie. And thus began the story of my epic film, Ciao! Manhattan, starring Edie in her first and only full-length feature.

Originally, Chuck had suggested a different Warhol Superstar, Susan Bottomly, for the role, but Susan dropped out at the last minute because of her socially prominent father’s strong opposition. Chuck’s Plan B was to get Edie. Which, by the way, is why Edie’s character in Ciao! is called Susan.

Another Factory talent was there that day, and he too would become very important in my career. Gino Piserchio was also a Warhol Superstar and an exceptionally handsome man. He played opposite Edie in Beauty # 2, which was directed by Andy and Chuck Wein. He immediately became a part of the studio mélange.

It turned out that Gino was a gifted musician and could sight read any piece of music you put in front of him. He was a graduate of the Mannes School, a Ford Agency model, gay, a raconteur, a stoner—and, like Edie, a meth aficionado, so he hardly ever slept.

Gino and I would later collaborate on my early Moog synthesizer experiments when Ciao! was being edited. He wrote and performed the score for the film, and I worked on the programming with him. He would be there night and day. We had both fallen in love with the synthesizer.

I was thrilled to be making a feature film with the refugees from the Factory. It was the late 60s, the Silver Sixties, the Summer of Love. I was fascinated by it all, and I believed that I had discovered a path to fame and fortune.

As Ciao! evolved and I traveled deeper and deeper into Warhol’s world, any lingering thoughts of living a normal life went out the window. Surprisingly, my family did not bat an eye. They were immersed in their own lives and my father’s business. My parents loved the fact that I was making movies and that Centaur Film was a legit business on 47th Street. They had agreed to invest most of the money I needed to make the film. I figured we could deliver the entire project in three or four months for about $35,000. Little did I know that it would take nearly six years, in fits and starts, and require much, much more money to realize.

We began shooting Ciao! Manhattan at the Central Park Be-In on Easter Sunday. At call time, Edie was walking around, stoned, with beat poets Allen Ginsberg and Gregory Corso and the rest of her entourage. The crew included Chuck, Raka, cameraman John Palmer, and writer/editor/political intriguer Genevieve Charbon. They conspired together and sent me to shoot the aerial footage while they filmed Edie on the ground. I was to shoot the cityscapes and the Be-In from a helicopter, which is precisely what I had been trained to do when I was in the army. It also separated me from Edie and the crew, so I didn’t see what a mess she was.

Ciao! picked up where the Factory left off. Edie was unconsciously talked into thoroughly revealing herself by Chuck. She became inundated with all the drugs and attention she sought. First Andy and now Ciao! provided a centerstage for her freak-out and fashionable rebellion, fueled by the amphetamines that were so much a part of that world. It was a mad rush for pleasure and fame.

The film started out as a worthwhile project, turned into an odyssey, and finally wound up as a self-destructive lifestyle that lasted for five years. In a way, it was a very accurate presentation ofwhat the Silver Sixties were all about. It all there: the madness, the alienation, the rage, the shock, everything we were all going through—a documentation of the time. It may have started nobly, but as time went on, things got crazier and crazier.

The film slowly evolved into a nightmare. It was like painting a picture with mice dipped in paint and telling them which way to run on the canvas—except that these mice were real people who were totally self-involved, very drugged, and unprepared to listen to anyone.

Heading downtown from 47th Street at the end of each day, I would lean against the back of the elevator in the company of the Hasidic diamond merchants with their long sideburns, black hats, and frock coats who filled the elevator car. Staring at the elevator doors, I would say to myself, can’t get any worse than what happened today. The next day, it got worse, and it went on like that for months.

Edie was living in the ChelseaDescript Hotel with Paul America. She felt at home there, with the hotel’s storied and artistic past. She was immersing herself in artistic milieu that mainly existed in her mind, and she loved that the Chelsea had been the home numerous writers, musicians, artists, and actors, including Dylan Thomas and Arthur C. Clarke (who wrote 2001: A Space Odyssey there), as well as beat poets like Ginsberg and Corso.

Edie knew everybody who lived on her floor; as a matter of fact, she knew everybody who lived on all the floors. She was at the height of her fame, Warhol’s ‘genius superstar in residence,’ appearing in the pages of Life Magazine. A few months before I met her, she’d had a short affair with Bob Dylan. She had met him by chance at the Factory, and her infatuation was immediate. Their flirtations didn’t go unnoticed, and it was rumored that Dylan’s songs ‘Leopard-Skin Pill-Box Hat,’ ‘Just Like A Woman,’ and ‘Like A Rolling Stone’ were all about her. Later, when Edie learned that Dylan had married his longtime girlfriend, Sarah Lowndes, she was crushed.

The other reason Edie loved the ChelseaDescript was that it gave her easy access to drugs. At the end of May 1967, a month into shooting Ciao!, she blacked out with a lit cigarette in her hand and set fire to her room.Descript She was taken to St. Vincent’s Hospital for her burns, leaving her with bandaged hands and unable to perform basic tasks like going to the bathroom, getting dressed, and feeding herself. The fire also killed her cat, ironically named Smoke.

When my father, who was financially supporting Centaur and Ciao!’s production, heard about the fire, he was furious. He had loaned Edie $50,000 worth of fur coats to use in the film, and they were in her room at the time. They were destroyed in the fire.

After being discharged from St. Vincent’s, Edie moved into my Lower East Side apartment, where she would remain for the better part of fifteen weeks. By now, nobody wanted anything to do with her. Her so-called friends found every excuse not to take her phone calls.

On the first day she was with me, she was sitting in the living room when in walked my eccentric housemate and majordomo, Bobby Anderson. They had never met before.

‘Who are you?’ Edie asked.

‘I’m Bobby,’ he said. ‘I live here. Who are you?’

‘I’m Edie Sedgwick,’ she replied. ‘Are you my nurse?’

‘I guess I am,’ Bobby exclaimed. ‘How fabulous!’

I asked Bobby if he wanted a job that paid twenty-five dollars a day to ensure that Edie got up in the morning, washed her face, turned up at the studio in proper attire, and behaved herself. He accepted. He called Edie ‘The Big Q-Tip’ because her hands were bandaged from the burns she suffered in the fire.

Bobby tried to feed Edie omelets in the morning, but she wanted cold shrimp and milkshakes. In his most pompous voice, he would tell her, ‘Edith, please, we’re on the Lower East Side, honey. Come down a little. Do you really want me to go out and buy fucking gourmet food at nine in the morning?’

Edie had been in our apartment for a week but hadn’t taken a bath yet. ‘Oh, my bandages,’ she’d say. ‘I’ll get them all wet.’ Bobby had to bathe her and would put plastic bags around the bandages with rubber bands to keep her hands dry. In the beginning, he had to help her put on her makeup and clean herself after going to the toilet because of her bandaged hands. She still had a few light burns on her face, which Bobby covered with makeup—she was very big on black makeup at the time. Despite all that, she always looked immaculate and beautiful.

Edie seemed happy at my place. She loved being waited on hand and foot. Bobby paid strict attention to her when she went to the studio to run errands. I remember him going with her to pick up her trust-fund checks. He said that he watched her pay for the cab with a pearl-and-diamond ring one day and a gold bracelet another day because she didn’t have enough cash to pay the cab fare.

Edie wasn’t bothered by things like that. As Bobby would say, ‘She was just faaaabulous!’

There was always a stream of people turning up when Edie was in my apartment. People hustled her, mainly under the guise of getting drugs for her. She was always very trusting and open to receiving anything new to get high on. She’d hand out hundreds of dollars, and the people going out to get the drugs wouldn’t bother coming back.

It seemed like there was no end to it, and for a while, I found myself swept up into the escalating vortex that surrounded her.

Max’s Kansas City was our underground living room, our home away from home. It was a hangout for artists, a diner, and a clubhouse for the greats, the near greats, and the ingrates. The back room was where all the real action happened. Warhol had a table in the corner, and we had our regular table diagonally across from him.

Mickey Ruskin had opened Max’s with an eye to a clientele of writers, filmmakers, poets, and painters. I knew Mickey from his time managing the Ninth Circle, a gay bar I patronized. As soon as Max’s became public knowledge, it got very popular and very busy. Mickey would keep the tourists and people in coats and ties waiting on the sidewalk while all of us scruffy creatives would be given tables immediately.

Edie and Bobby’s relationship caught on like wildfire. I thought it was beautiful. At Max’s, Bobby became known as her nurse, and he loved all the attention. Some of her most fabulous wardrobe items had survived the ChelseaDescript fire, and she always dressed to impress. When she walked through the door at Max’s, it was as if royalty had arrived.

Edie kept saying to me that she expected that, when the bandages came off, she would have grotesque and deformed hands, but when the doctors finally took them off, she was happy and thankful to see that her hands were in perfect shape. During the four months that she lived with me, there were times when her true light would shine. It was in those in-between times when she wasn’t high but also not down too low or depressed. She was lucid, enchanting, empathetic—almost like a little girl. One minute she was shy and vulnerable, the next cocky, strong, and competent. She had a fawn-like quality, and she was an incredibly talented illustrator and painter. It was in those moments that I fell in love with her.

Tragically, though, Edie was ruled by her addiction, and by now she had become a professional, full-time addict. Getting her to show up on time on set and do her job became increasingly challenging. We couldn’t get her to learn her lines or take direction much at all. She had once been the queen of the underground; now, it was devastating to see how far she had fallen. I worried constantly about her. I was filled with a fear that she would not be able to finish shooting Ciao!

Chapter 6
CLEAN

Chapter 6

Tuning into the future

We were well into Ciao! when, on one of my nighttime adventures in the East Village,

00:11 - 00:57

[]

I heard some strange electronic bleeping and blooping coming from an eccentric nightclub called Cerebrum. I went inside and stuck my nose into the sound booth, where a few modules from a Moog synthesizer were set up on the floor. The sounds they were making got my undivided attention. Light bulbs off in my head, and I knew instantly that I had to have one of these things. I realized it would be a great way to compose the score for the film. Instantly, it felt like a natural fit; love at first sound. In that single moment, I had fallen in love with the synthesizer—and, as it turned out, I loved it more than making movies.

The synthesizer can most simply be described as a machine that electronically generates and modifies sounds. Its genesis can be traced back to the 1910s. In 1920, the theremin became one of the first synthesizers to gain a modicum of success. It was an unusual instrument, even by today’s standards, as it’s played without even touching it. Two antenna—one vertical, the other horizontal—create electromagnetic fields. Moving your hand through the fields changes the pitch (the vertical antenna) and volume (the horizontal antena).

Synthesized sounds gained steam in the late 1950s, when Bob Moog and others began to experiment with adjusting the voltage applied to the instrument’s circuits to alter pitch. The first commercial Moog synthesizer debuted in 1964.

It wasn’t long after hearing the Moog that night at Cerebrum that I got in touch with Walter Sear, the New York Moog rep, and bought a synthesizer right off the floor of the National Association Of Music Merchants (NAMM) trade show, which was taking place at the New York Convention Center on Columbus Circle. It was one of the first three or four systems that Bob had built. Wendy Carlos Switched-On Bach and multi-genre composer Peter Nero were the other early adopters. It was exciting.

The Moog was complex and intimidating at first. It consisted of different modules, and sounds could be created and altered by patching them together with patch cords. It looked like a telephone operator’s switchboard. It didn’t take long, however, for me to grow enamored with the sounds it could make and become totally transfixed. I ran headfirst into the open arms of electronica.

Walter Sear and Tom Flye brought the Moog system to Centaur. Tom was the drummer in a band called Lothar & The Hand People, which Bob Moog was endorsing. The band’s star was not a musician but rather their theremin, which they dubbed ‘Lothar.’ The rest of the band, The Hand People, consisted of Rusty Ford on bass, Paul Conly on keyboards, Tom on drums; Kim King played the early Moog synthesizer and guitar, and John Emelin sang and controlled Lothar.

The band formed in Denver in 1965. About a year later, they moved to New York and quickly became a noted part of the city’s music scene. They’re credited as being the first band to use synthesizers both on stage and in the studio, true forefathers of electronic music in rock. They jammed with Hendrix and shared stages with The Byrds, Canned Heat, The Chambers Brothers, Grateful Dead, and The Lovin’ Spoonful.

We had some problems getting the synthesizer up and running in the studio, so Walter came by and did some handholding. Bob Moog came down several times with his soldering iron. He’d sit on the floor in the studio as he worked on stabilizing the keyboard drift and other assorted issues. We became good friends after that.

Tom and I hit it off, too, and he helped me convert the Centaur Film insert stage into a real recording studio. I remember him helping me hang dark blue velvet drapes on pipes along the walls. We soon had a four-track tape recorder, two mono machines, a 35mm projector, film dubbers, a small console, a monitor system, and all the other gear we needed to get started. The synthesizer and the theremin were the stars of the show, though.

Once word got out about what we were up to, there was a stream of curious musicians and industry types at the door. Tom and Kim, the guitar player, became our house engineers. It was a match made in heaven. We soon found ourselves making electronic soundtracks for TV and radio commercials. I remember making the music for Pepto Bismol and Duracell ad campaigns, which are still playing to this day.

Lothar & The Hand People had a recording contract with Records, and their exec at the label was Nick Venet. Venet was riding high on his signing The Beach Boys to Capitol in 1962. Having his support gave Lothar a big boost. With Venet’s blessing, I was tasked with producing and recording the band’s first album at Centaur. Since I owned the place, there was no time clock. We had time to experiment with blending live instruments and electronics. The combination of traditional instruments and electronic ones, like the theremin and Moog, created a unique and interesting sound. It was an important aspect of the production, and it became a bedrock moment for me.

At the time, Bob Moog was still refining his synthesizers, and the modular systems he was developing were being adopted primarily by experimental composers. Using a Moog in a rock context was groundbreaking. Most bands weren’t even aware of their existence yet. The theremin had been around for decades, but its eerie, otherworldly sound was mostly associated with classical music or science-fiction soundtracks.

06:28 - 07:00

[MUSIC CUE - Theremin music]

By bringing it into rock music, Lothar & The Hand People helped give it a new identity—one that would later be embraced by bands like Led Zeppelin, The Beach Boys, and later by progressive rock and experimental electronic artists. You could call their music experimental electronica/rock.

Lothar had been together for years and had a good idea of who they were, which helped immensely. They were a super tight garage band. We were all in the sandbox together, merging rock’n’roll and electronica for the first time. In a cloud of pot and hash, we became immersed in figuring out how to play the synthesizer and record the band’s album. There were no books or anyone to teach us anything about the synthesizer!

Capital released Presenting . . . Lothar & The Hand People in 1968. The album didn’t receive much attention outside of New York, but the single, a cover of the Manfred Mann hit ‘Machines,’ did get a fair bit of radio airplay.

07:52 - 09:05

[MUSIC CUE: Lothar and the Hand People “Machines”]

Producing that album had a significant influence on me. If you listen to my work with DEVO many years later, you’ll recognize some of the DNA from ‘Machines’ on their song ‘Whip It.’

Chapter 7

Total chaos and ‘vitamin shots’

00:04 - 00:

[MUSIC CUE - music]

At the same time as I was becoming a synthesist and producing music, I was still recklessly moving forward on Ciao! Manhattan . . . and things were going quickly downhill.

One of Ciao!’s characters was a speed-dispensing doctor we called Dr. Robert, he was inspired by Dr. Max Jacobson. Jacobson himself was often on set, prescribing his special ‘vitamin shots,’ which I think consisted of a large dose of vitamin B12 and amphetamine. He was poking up most of the cast of Hair on Broadway, and it was rumored that he had spent a lot of time in Washington, taking care of President Kennedy’s back issues. (I recently learned that he’d traveled with Kennedy to Vienna for his meetings with Nikita Khruschev.)

Raka once said of Jacobson, ‘He had a horoscope in one hand and a syringe in the other.’ He was our official Ciao! ‘astrologer-doctor.’ He got everybody high and manic on his famous shots, and he fed us all a line of mystical bullshit. I figured that since everybody was doing it, what could be wrong with it? So, I opened a charge account with the good doctor, and the crew and I went at it. I fell for it for a while, but I soon came to hate it and quit after a few ‘treatments.’ My drug of choice were always weed and psychedelics.

One of the biggest days on set was when we shot the swim party at ‘Al Roon’s World-Famous Health Spa.’ In reality, the spa was a rundown club located in the basement of the Ansonia Hotel on 74th Street. For this decadent scene, the doctor got to shoot up everybody in sight with his signature vitamin shots. There was no script, just a few ideas in writer/director Chuck Wein’s head. Chuck, Genevieve, and Raka had invited along all the folks from the Factory and Max’s Kansas City

and everybody they knew from the underground art and music scene. It was hot, humid, and noisy, and the place was packed.

Chuck’s directing consisted of getting everybody as stoned and out of control as possible. And then he said, ‘Just carry on and give yourself up to total abandon.’ Reality and fantasy merged.

John Palmer turned on his camera, and the night unfolded into chaos.

Edie later recalled in an interview with David Weisman that ended up in Jean Stein’s book, Edie: An American Girl, Edie said ‘Oh, wow, what a scene that place was, that heaven drug-down-sexual-perversion-get-their-rocks-off health spa, I was already so bombed I don’t know how I got there. I met Paul America in the pool. We were so blasted we forgot what was good for us and what wasn’t, and the whole place turned into a giant orgy . . . every kind of sex freak, from homosexuals to nymphomaniacs. . . . I was on the raft, guzzling tequila and vodka and Scotch and bourbon and shooting up every other second . . . losing syringes down the pool drains. . . . Oh, it really was some night . . . I just going on some sexual tailspin. It was one of the wildest scenes I’ve been in or ever hoped to be in. Sex and speed, wow! Oh, God. A twenty-four-hour climax that could go on for days . . . I should have been ashamed of myself. I’m not, but I should be.’

Despite his popularity among the cast and crew, Jacobson and his vitamin shots were a major factor in the demise of Ciao!. His medicine would ultimately bring everybody, including the film’s stars, to their collective knees.

Another of the film’s storylines featured a character named Mr. Verdecchio a ruthless oligarch kingpin and Dr. Robert’s fictional supplier. He wore a coat and tie and supervised serving the guests from two huge platters of hors d’oeuvres, along with two bowls of spiked fruit punch. He was played by an unknown actor in his first and only film role: my father! He just fell into the role because he was around so much and ended up loving being in front of the camera.

I was frankly astonished by my father’s natural talent. He could improvise at the drop of a hat, and he was always ready for a turn in front of the camera. But maybe I shouldn’t have been so surprised, considering his life’s work. I’m sure he picked up some acting skills from the decades he spent selling furs to major department stores all over the Midwest. After retiring from that, he became a politician. He was a staunch Republican and ran for mayor of Great Neck Estates. There’s no doubt that his people skills as a master salesman played a huge role in getting him elected. Today, there is a street named after him in Great Neck Estates: Margouleff Circle Drive.

Somehow, Centaur had become a Margouleff family business, with my father charming every and my mother writing checks. This level of personal involvement and support was unbelievable to me, considering how my parents were barely even present during my childhood and I mostly felt alone and emotionally isolated.

￼The Verdecchio character evolved as we moved further into the production. It started as a bit part, but my father was improvising so well that he became a central figure used as a theatrical device to tie all the disparate scenes together. I remember a scene in which Verdecchio is on the massage table, lecturing his pompous and dimwitted club manager, Mario. With an impatient, condescending sigh, he ad-libs, ‘Mario, Mario, Mario, you really are a wart on the ass of progress, and you better take care of this business, or I’ll see about having someone take care of your business!’

While shooting that scene, we decided that Edie and her sidekick, Youli, had stolen a considerable quantity of drugs from nightclub run by Mario. Naturally, Verdecchio wanted those drugs back.

For Edie and Paul, the Verdecchio character went even further. He was slowly turning into a surreal, paranoid fantasy of their drug-manipulated reality. They believed that Mr. Verdecchio was an actual living person. The film was slowly turning into a mutated mix of reality and fantasy.

As well as becoming a star in Ciao!, my dad was also my patron. He came around frequently to keep an eye on his investment, and, more and more, what he witnessed was my loss of control over an unraveling production.. I felt like I was trapped in a whirlwind. I was the producer, but Chuck, the director, was always trying to keep me at arm’s length creatively, one way or another. In reality, the only power I had over him was his paycheck. My brain was screaming to stop all of it right after the shoot at Al Roons’, but how would I finish the picture? There was no script, and the storyline only resided in Chuck’s head.

The next day, I returned to my office, wondering what the hell had happened. The spa scene was certainly not what I expected it to be. I knew that the production was utterly out of control and that I was in big trouble. In reality, the inmates were running the asylum, and I was stuck between a rock and a hard place, so the metaphors would say. I understood why Warhol was happy to see Chuck and his crew move on. I knew I had to get through it to save the picture. In hindsight, I should have stood up to Chuck and put my foot down then and there. But I didn’t, so the madness would continue for a few more months.

The film finally came crashing down while we were shooting what would come to be called the ‘Medium Convention.’ According to Chuck’s invisible script, this was to be a meeting of all the ‘other beings and voyagers from out of this world who came to earth to talk about the future.’

Mr. Verdecchio traveled to the Medium Convention on his mega yacht, motoring up the Hudson River with his cronies. I remember happily shooting these scenes hanging out the door of a helicopter. I was in my element. But the happiness was short-lived.

We shot the Medium Convention for several days in an old mansion that some believed to be haunted on the palisades in Fort Lee New Jersey. Emerging fashionista Betsey Johnson created the costumes. When my father appeared to shoot his scenes, he was shocked and angered. Seeing Allen Ginsberg walking around in the nude, chanting, and all of these strange people wandering around, was simply too much. After the shoot, he said, very indignantly, ‘Robert, if you put that in the film, please don’t put me or our name on it in any shape or manner, because I don’t want to be associated with it. I don’t care if he is Allen Ginsberg. I don’t know how much younger he is than me, but he certainly isn’t becoming in HIS generation, either.’

My father was mad as hell, and I deserved it. The project was going to pieces. Edie was in total meltdown and spent a lot of time loaded and being very difficult, to put it mildly. She would purposely blow her lines so she could stay in front of the camera longer or hide out with Nurse Bobby somewhere in the old mansion so we couldn’t find her for any reshoots. She had become a victim of her controlling, tyrannical father and of Andy, who aided and abetted her to his own ends. Her behavior had become unbearable. Raka called this ‘Edie’s Icarus moment.’ We then reached the inescapable conclusion that we could not finish the film with her.

Edie ran away from my apartment and disappeared after the final fiasco of the Medium Convention. At first, I thought it was another one of her games. She had specific ways of getting things she wanted, and as soon as you played the same game, she wouldn’t want to play anymore, so she split.

I later learned that Edie wanted to get back with Warhol. They reconnected and tried making a film together. It was to be called Ondine And Edie, but production was abandoned when it became clear that Edie was too difficult to work with.

I still have a hard time watching scenes from the film and Edie’s gradual disintegration. She was a wonderful, gentle, and compelling human being. It was like watching a horrific car accident in slow motion. But through it all, she created all the styles of the time, right through to the poor little rich girl look everyone reveres to this day.

Meanwhile, Chuck and Genevieve continued to collect their paychecks while editing Ciao! across the hall from the recording studio. That turned into crazy town, too. I found that they had set up some plastic Playskool toy trains and were running around the floor in the edit bay. That was the last straw. I knew things had to change.

With Edie gone and my father losing faith, Raka, John Palmer, and I fired Chuck and Genevieve. We were the only filmmakers left standing. We decided that if we were going to finish the picture, we had to leave all the astrology and the amphetamine vitamin shots behind. So, with my father’s blessing, we hired a real editor, Bob Ferren, and started assembling the footage we already had in the can.

I set out to rescue Ciao! with Raka and John, who became more creatively involved as co-directors. We shot some additional scenes with a plan to tie together the most beautiful and lucid but still somewhat unrelated sequences. We soon discovered that essentially no one was available except for my father, so we decided to expand Mr. Verdecchio’s part even further. All Raka had to do when directing my father was to say, ‘Mr. Verdecchio, talk about this or that,’ and off he would go. So, we concocted and shot all the additional material with him, trying to build pieces around the existing scenes and provide some context.

We started with the scene of the all-powerful Mr. Verdecchio on the massage table at Dr. Robert’s spa. We used that scene to introduce the idea of him spying on everybody.

14:28 - 18:34 "Driving Verdecchio" from Ciao! Manhattan soundtrack

We invented theatrical devices like this to create a storyline and finish the film, and with his phone, cameras, and monitors, Verdecchio became the principal narrator.

The only other surviving member of the team was our composer, Gino Piserchio. He had continued to hang on through the madness. Gino and I were both smitten by the synthesizer. We shared the idea that he would compose and perform the entire soundtrack on the Moog. At this point, we didn’t have a completed film, so Gino composed against some of the scenes we already had in the can. Had the film been finished in New York at that time, it would have been the first feature film to have an entirely electronic soundtrack.

As fate would have it, it didn’t happen that way, and when Ciao! eventually went back into production in California a couple of years later, only part of Gino’s score would make the final cut.

Looking back on those days, I felt like an airplane pilot leaving the runway with one engine on fire. I had to get airborne and fly the plane, and there was no turning back. Then, halfway through the flight, the other engine caught fire, and Ciao! went down in flames.

Ciao! Manhattan was over, and the door at Centaur had closed for the last time, too. I wanted to produce something emotional, sympathetic, and artistic by combining a cinema verité approach to filmmaking with an electronic music soundtrack—a film for all the folks who had put their faith in me. I wanted to deliver a return on their considerable financial investment. But that wasn’t what happened.

When Centaur finally closed, Gino and I were the last ones out of the door. We moved all the recording gear and my Moog to his place on 57th Street. His apartment was an old, stately, ornate yet ruined brownstone, just a few doors down from Carnegie Hall and the Russian Tea Room. It was above a Chinese restaurant, and the aroma from the kitchen wafted up into what had once been the grand living room.

I thought this would be a safe place for my gear to live for the time being, and I expected that Gino and I would pick up some commercial synth work. But we didn’t do much in the way of business. Dr. Jacobson continued to be an influence on Gino. I was witnessing some familiar bad behavior, and his drug use was getting in the way of his work. I knew that I had to move on from Gino and his apartment studio. Sadly, he would pass away from AIDS-related complications in 1989. I still miss his light.

I was a mess, filled with guilt for failing to finish the film as I had promised, and I went home to Great Neck to lick my wounds. Some nights, I would commute to the city for an occasional fling at the Chelsea Hotel. I ended up couch surfing and landed temporarily at Raka’s apartment in Tudor City. By now, I had lost everything: my East Village apartment, my studio, my business. All the Ciao! workprints, outtakes, and most of Centaur’s furnishings found their way to the basement of my family home in Great Neck. Movie Lab took possession of the film’s negatives because I owed them a fortune. I was left with nothing
