Chapter 8

Fire Island and the reset button

With Ciao! over and my Moog no longer at Gino’s, I was staying temporarily at Raka’s when I met Allen Kelly. He was blonde, blue-eyed, very gentle, and compassionate. He was gay and an electrologist by trade, and also an amateur philosopher, poet, and a part-time magician. He lived in an old pre–World War II apartment building on 84th Street and Central Park West. He offered me a room just off the kitchen, formerly the maid’s room, as a place to stay rent-free for the time being. I was beyond thankful.

The apartment was painted light gray and silver, and the large front windows had white voile curtains, dark gray drapes, and soft indirect lighting. The place had a wispy, ethereal feeling. There was never any sexual attraction between us, just an abiding friendship and respect. Kelly and that apartment became a safe harbor for me.

In the summer of 1968, Kelly and four of his friends decided to rent a house for the summer in the Pines on Fire Island, a long, thin, barrier island off the south coast of Long Island and a well-known resort destination. I joined them. I didn’t know much about the place, but after my first visit, wild horses couldn’t keep me away.

01:34 - 02:19

[SFX CUE - train + sea sfx sounds]

We’d head over to the island on Thursdays, hopping on the 3pm Long Island Railroad train from Penn Station. I always took a window seat and napped with my head against the window. We’d arrive at the ferry terminal in Sayville by 5pm, take the last ferry across the Great South Bay, and get to Fire Island by sundown. There were two isolated neighboring villages, Cherry Grove and the Pines. Everything was connected by a series of narrow pedestrian boardwalks and miles of white sandy beaches along the Atlantic Ocean. It was a car-free paradise. Everybody traveled foot, by bike, and sometimes by golf cart.

Fire Island was an artists’ colony as well as a haven for the LGBTQ community and a minority of enlightened straight people. Writers, painters, filmmakers, fashionistas, photographers, musicians, and actors, young and old; wealthy owners of stunning, architecturally significant beach houses and cedar cottages, weekenders, and folks like me with a share in a beach house for the summer; all found solace and a safe, welcoming space.

The famous Stonewall riots would take place in New York City the following year, but I was already seeing the rumblings of a culture in transformation. At the end of August 1968, there was a raid and a number of arrests by the Suffolk County Police on Fire Island. Apparently, it was an annual summer ritual for them to stage raids in Cherry Grove, rounding up gay men accused of engaging in public sex. Everybody was up in arms. The Mattachine Society funded a legal defense and went to court, demonstrating to the larger gay community (many of whom were closeted at the time) that gay people could band together to resist police harassment. This would be the last time the police tried to enforce the sodomy laws on Fire Island. Gay liberation was coming! A new generation was discovering itself, and I was a part of it.

On Fire Island, I saw people like me leaving their baggage behind and coming to a place where they could be entirely themselves. It was an inherently erotic landscape.

Ciao! had beaten me to a pulp. Fire Island offered me a respite from the chaos of the overstimulated life I led. These days, I think that you would call it PTSD. Fire Island was a place where my unbridled eroticism was balanced with reflection, and my joy co-existed with the space to think about my identity. It felt like I was a part of some grand creation meant solely for me. ThatDescript summer, I was finally out of the closet for good. I had found a place where I could play on the beach, hold hands with a guy, be with like-minded people, and dance all night with a man.

For me, this was a summer of long morning walks on the endless white sand beaches alone, or sometimes with Kelly, where I started to accept and understand who I was and started to put my life back together. It was my therapy.

It was the end of summer.

05: - 06:30

[MUSIC CUE - music with beach sfx]

I had been up all night, intimately involved with a very rugged ‘Quaker Oats and apple pie’ kind of a guy, Buddy T. He was a part of our circle, so we knew a lot about each other. That evening, we did some LSD together. We split a few drops on a sugar cube and left the planet together. Suddenly, it was six in the morning. I was just starting to come down. Buddy was still asleep next to me, and I decided to have a walk on the beach alone. I was still feeling super-sensitive, open, and peaceful. The sun was just coming up in the east with some pinkish clouds and a vivid blue sky. It was windy. I was wearing a loose-fitting kaftan and nothing else. I could feel it beating against my bare body and hear it flapping like a flag. The wind was blowing sand onto my face, and I was sensitive to every particle.

At that moment, I realized that accepting who I was had brought me a sense of deep serenity and freedom, which profoundly changed me forever—to this day, that moment of enlightenment turned me toward my life in music. I never returned to Fire Island, but it was not my last experience with LSD.

Part 2

‘The Original New Timbral Orchestra’

Chapter 9
CLEAN

Chapter 9

Two minds, one TONTO

While discovering myself on those summer weekends on Fire Island, I began to recover from the shock of Centaur closing and my dire straits. I returned to New York and found a new studio and home with my old friends at Broadway Recording, where I had produced Timmy Dulaine’s band, Buckwheat. It was on the seventh floor of the Ed Sullivan building, at the corner of Broadway and 53rd Street.

Pat Jaques, a great old-time recording engineer, co-owned the place with his trusted studio manager, Fran White. They gave me some compassion, a desk, a phone, and a place for me to set up my Moog. They also offered a one-room apartment across the street to crash in after late-night sessions. I learned a lot from Pat. His recording skills and knowledge far surpassed the homebrew style I had improvised at Centaur.

I moved my gear to Broadway, set up shop, and started doing some sessions. As soon as word got out about me and the synth, the bees began to fly around the proverbial honey pot, and I started to get busy. My first sessions were with Tito Puente and Richie Havens, both of whom had heard about me and the synthesizer and were intrigued to check out this new technology.

I started writing and composing my own electronic music when the studio wasn’t booked. My time on Fire Island had opened my mind and brought me some inspiration and curiosity, and now I found myself with the time to spend as a creative artist in my own right, in a first-class recording studio. I was in front of the synthesizer, a custom recording console, a great audio monitoring system, and a Scully twelve-track tape recorder. Pat also had a Rek-O-Kut disc-cutting lathe and would provide clients with a 33⅓ disc at the end of the sessions. There was no such thing as a cassette deck then. Pat showed me how to cut those discs. It was tricky, but eventually I was ready to hit record. It was just me and the machines. Pat kept his hand on my shoulder, offering invaluable support, I recorded my first fully synthesized composition, ‘Aurora.’ It was twenty-six minutes long.

Word had begun to spread about me and my Moog, and in the spring of 1970, I was invited to join Mediasound Studios as their mad scientist and electronic music expert. They had a staff of fifteen, three studios, and a full three-room mastering facility. I loved Broadway—it was my home—but Mediasound was rocking. I couldn’t say no.

Harry Hirsch and Bob Walters had founded Mediasound in June 1969 with financing from the investors Joel Roseman and John Roberts. It was in the heart of Manhattan, and it quickly became a hit-making machine. The building had once been home to the Manhattan Baptist Church and was also the former home of the composer Béla Bartók, who lived there in the 1940s. If I recall correctly, Roseman and Roberts had invested one million dollars to turn the place into a state-of-the-art studio. They were also the financial backers of the Woodstock Festival in August 1969. I couldn’t have ended up in a more perfect place, or with more like-minded people.

Studio A was more than two stories high, with a peaked roof, huge rafters and arches, and beautiful stained-glass windows. wonder of gothic architecture. I never have expected to find a church inside an old apartment building in the middle of New York City. Bob and Harry worked hard to retain much of that atmosphere, especially in Studio A, which was formerly the church’s sanctuary. It was a splendid acoustic space for drums, strings, horns, and large ensembles. The studio had a vibe all its own and a sound like no place else. It was a beautiful and inspiring place to make music.

I kept my synthesizer on a gurney, which I parked in the isolation booth, so we had easy access to it when we needed it. During the day, Mediasound catered mostly to big union string and horn sessions. They started as early as 8am but had to finish by 6pm to avoid the musicians’ union’s required double pay. At night, though, when the studios were relatively empty, I would pull the synthesizer out of its parking place and set to work. With the lights low and the stained-glass windows illuminated, I would set it up in the middle of the studio with ear-level on either side. It was an awesome sight to behold—big, complex, and intimidating. I loved filling this cavernous space with music. I saw the power of the sounds I was creating on people’s faces when they saw and heard me in action. Although the studio was now a secular space, it felt like I was making sacred music.

Standing in front of the synthesizer with my long hair and colorful, tie-dyed clothing, I looked like a disheveled U-boat captain from another galaxy, or maybe a hippie priest. Most music industry types did not know what to think of me. There were no schools to teach synthesis or figure out what it was about. It was all new territory, and I was making it up as I went. I had found a blank canvas—a tabula rasa—with no preconceived notions to jump off from and into the future.

A few months passed. Winter came, and my Moog and I had settled in nicely to Mediasound. During the day, I created electronic music and sounds for radio and TV commercials. At night, along with band sessions and time to work on my own music, there was also studio maintenance to factor in. Repairs were required nearly every night because the analog equipment started breaking the minute you turned it on. The next day’s studio setups and tape alignments had to be done. Setting up for a big orchestra and running in sync to picture took careful planning and, more often than not, upward of thirty microphone inputs. It was labor-intensive and time-consuming, and it all had to be ready for the 8am downbeat the next morning. Delays after the sessions had started could get very expensive, very quickly.

One evening, I arrived at Studio A ready to take advantage of some downtime, only to find the recording console open wide like a piano lid, exposing the massive nest of wires inside. Standing in front of the console was Malcolm Cecil.

‘You must be the new chief engineer,’ I said.

‘Yes,’ he replied, in his very British accent, ‘and you must be the man behind the Moog in the iso booth.’

‘Yes indeed,’ I said. Of course, we had already heard a lot about each other.

Malcolm was slim with a huge Afro-style haircut and thick glasses, and he usually wore denim bib overalls with a tool kit around his waist. I had my long hair tied into a ponytail down my back, and I was wearing a loose-fitting linen shirt with a gold-embroidered velvet vest. He and I instantly realized that we belonged to the same tribe and that both of us came from another planet. Most of the senior engineers at Mediasound were much more vanilla, with their white polyester dress shirts and pencil protectors.

Pointing at the board, I asked Malcolm, ‘Do you know how to run this thing?’

‘I’d better,’ he said. ‘I have to fix it.’

‘Nobody round here will show me how to operate the console,’ I told him. ‘Will you show me how to run it?’

Malcolm looked at me, pointed to the Moog, and said, ‘Do you know how to run that thing?’

‘I’d better,’ I said. ‘I own it.’

‘I’ll tell you what,’ he replied. ‘I’ll show you how to run this, and you show me how to run that.’ He held his hand. ‘Deal?’

We shook on it, and a life-changing friendship, collaboration, and partnership was born. Malcolm taught me how to become a recording engineer and how to be fastidious in the studio with audio, and I taught him how to play the synthesizer.

That night, I played Malcolm the raw, twenty-six-minute version of ‘Aurora’ that I had begun at Broadway Studios.

09:09 -

[MUSIC CUE - Tonto’s Expanding Headband “Aurora”]

I wasn’t even sure it was music, but he assured me that it was and that it just needed some ‘judicious’ editing. We cut it down to seven minutes and developed it musically by composing, recording, and overdubbing some new material. We threw away all of the conventions, like the twelve-note tone row and the idea of an eight-bar pattern. It was all very experimental. For me, it sounded like the birth of the world, and together, we composed what would later become part of our album Zero Time.

In the meantime, I was doing commercial sessions for the likes of Trans Caribbean Airways and Crazy Daisy toilet paper. What sticks in my mind from that time was a session I was on for an ad agency. The agency producer was sitting in the control room with a cigar in his mouth, ashes falling down the front of his shirt, and asked me if I could make something that sounded ‘more like a tablecloth.’ I realized then that my days in the world of TV and radio commercials were numbered.

People started hearing about Malcolm and me, and we began to attract music clients at night. Richie Havens, who I had met during my Moog days at Broadway, had a label called Stormy Forest Records. One of the artists on his label was the band Men Working. I ended up producing and engineering them at Mediasound. Malcolm and I had become the night shift, and we were steadily developing a solid list of music clients.

Malcolm and his crew had to keep the studio running and optimized. He was busy upgrading Studio B to a full sixteen-track room. The studio had just purchased one of the first Ampex MM-1000 sixteen-track recorders. The deck itself was a converted video recording machine—the first two-inch machine I had seen. The console required many modifications to accommodate it. It was a big deal. The studio was running twenty-four-seven.

Over the next several months, after we got rolling, I learned that Malcolm was not only an A-class technical engineer but also a formidable classical and jazz musician. He had a long historyas the principal bassist for the BBC’s Radio Orchestra, and he had played with many of the top jazz musicians of the time. He had been a member of the house band at Ronnie Scott’s Jazz Club, where he backed up visiting artists from the UK and abroad, and was also a co-founder of the early British blues group Blues Incorporated, whose fluid membership included, at various times, Jack Bruce, Charlie Watts, Ginger Baker, and whose sets could feature sit-in performances by Keith Richards, Mick Jagger, Rod Stewart, Jimmy Page, and other young blues obsessives in the burgeoning London scene. The club was one of the most important influences on the jazz scene in the UK. In New York, while we worked at Mediasound, Malcolm continued to play gigs several nights a month with jazz greats like Jim Hall and Lanie Kazan.

After that first winter night in Studio A, when we collaborated on ‘Aurora,’ Malcolm and I continued to create new music and a new synthesizer array together. It was all very experimental, and it would gradually evolve into our group, Tonto’s Expanding Head Band—a band with one instrument played by the two of us simultaneously. At first, we thought that Tonto’s Expanding Head Band would just be the name of the band, but in short order, the synthesizer took on a personality all its own. Our collection of modules, all connected through a maze of cables, became TONTO, or The Original New Timbral Orchestra. Malcolm came up with the acronym. In his words, ‘The Original because it was the first. New Timbral means new tone colors, and that’s what the instrument produced. And Orchestra because it was more than one synthesizer.on’t think either of us had any idea what would happen next.

Malcolm may have made TONTO into an acronym, but I had come up with the name Tonto’s Expanding Head Band in an inspired moment years earlier. It was on a chilly morning in Raka Weisman’s kitchen at the end of my Ciao! Manhattan days. I was on the downside of an all-nighter on peyote, in my underwear, eating a can of cold asparagus with my fingers, hen I said out loud, even though there was nobody else in the room except me, ‘Tonto’s Expanding Head Band!’ For some reason, the phrase stuck around. Perhaps it was just waiting for the music to arrive.

Malcolm and I usually started marinating ideas for our synthesizer experiments over dinner at a Chinese/Spanish diner across the street from the studio, after the day’s commercial sessions were over. The waiters knew us by name. The food was terrible for you and loaded with monosodium glutamate, but it tasted great, although it usually left me sweating and a little feverish.

The restaurant had paper tablecloths. By the end of dinner, those tablecloths would be covered with notes, sketches, scribbles, and ideas as we conceived and envisioned what would be the largest synthesizer in the world. Our vision centered around building something that was a real-time multi-voice performing instrument—a multitimbral electronic orchestra with several synthesizers connected by a central brain, designed by Malcolm, that allowed the various modules to communicate with one another.

There was and still is no other instrument like it. It had its own operating system and its own kind of magic. To begin with, TONTO was a collection of several different synthesizer modules, all piled up on the original hospital gurney I used for my Moog, but soon expanded, and more tables were added. In the end, it was nearly twelve feet long, with patch cords going in every direction.

In the beginning, TONTO looked like the finest of Rube Goldberg machines. It had keyboards to control the sounds, but it was more akin to a wind instrument in that you put your total emotive energy into playing one note, or in our case one event, at a time. We started with four keyboards. Each one could only control a single voice, but that gave us the capability to create four voices at once. TONTO was all analog (digital synthesizers didn’t exist yet), and we were pushing the technology to its absolute limit. However, like many acts of creation, it was those limitations that sparked innovation.

Bob and Harry, the studio owners, gave us their total support. The synthesizer had become a calling card for the studio, attracting new clients, and as long as we met our responsibilities for the Mediasound sessions, they didn’t mind what we did with TONTO. They realized that Malcolm and I were authentically different and doing something new and exciting that was totally reflective of and connected to the times.

We were fully immersed and driven. Our talents complemented each other because we came from opposite sides of the spectrum. Malcolm might focus on a bass line, and I would go to the other end of the instrument and start with white noise. As for who came up with what, I don’t know. We just found a method that made sense.

It was around this time that Ciao! Manhattan rose from the ashes. I thought it was long gone. It seemed like a lifetime had gone by, and I had already become fully immersed in music when, in the late summer of 1970, Raka Weisman and John Palmer managed to raise a few thousand dollars to get the film out of hock at the lab and resume production.

Edie was staying at the Cottage Hospital in Santa Barbara, recovering from her self-induced hell. Raka and John visited her there and asked if she wanted to resume working on the film. She needed no convincing and immediately started obsessing about it. Finishing the film became her life’s mission; after three years in and out of production, Ciao! had become a lifestyle for her. It was everything for her. Her old friends, as I liked to say, had all conveniently forgotten her phone number. Ciao! was all she had left.

Raka worked it out with the doctors to have Edie released from the hospital. He and John relocated the production to Pasadena, where filming resumed.

Even though I had moved well past Ciao! and was immersed in my work at Mediasound with Malcolm, I flew to the West Coast several times at Raka’s request to help with the audio. I stayed at his place on Arden Boulevard, but I left when we weren’t filming, so I wasn’t around for most of the LA production or postproduction. I no longer had any real creative or financial stake in the film, so I was on the outside looking in. But, still, after all the time and energy I’d invested, I wanted to see the picture finally finished.

Looking back at the raw black-and-white footage, it was obvious that while many of the sequences we had shot in New York were agonizingly beautiful and compelling, there was also a lot of trash. It was a jigsaw puzzle with all the pieces scattered, and it was going to be a huge challenge to create a cohesive story from it. So, John and Raka started again with a new plot to tie all the New York footage together with the scenes shot in California in color.

Once Edie got on set, she immediately relapsed. Her minder, Michael Post, had to have a fifth of vodka on hand at all times. She would go on strike and refuse to work if she didn’t get it.

Edie and Michael were both patients at the Cottage Hospital, and in addition to him taking care of her on set, the two were in love. On July 24, 1971, they were married. When Edie was with Michael, I saw true happiness in her for the first time in a long time. That made it even more heartbreaking when on November 16, 1971, only weeks after wrapping up filming, Michael woke to find Edie had passed away beside him in their bed. The authorities said her death was due to an accidental overdose of prescribed medication.

I went to her funeral at the Oak Hill Cemetery in Ballard, a small, sleepy farming town near Santa Barbara.

I drove up from LA with Jeffrey Briggs, who had joined Ciao! as a new cast member for the shoot in Pasadena. It was just the two of us. Raka didn’t make it to the funeral, but he called from New York and said we should buy some flowers, which I did. It was a real heartbreaker.

We stood outside until Edie’s family and friends filed in, maybe seventy-five people in all. We didn’t introduce ourselves to anyone, but we made sure people saw us so that the family knew that the Ciao! crew was represented. The casket was in the church, covered in flowers. I don’t remember any organ music or singing. We offered our condolences to Michael, who was now a widower at twenty-two years old. He was filled with grief.

It was over. Edie had become stardust.

Looking back on my ride through the steamy, sleazy, druggy world of Ciao! Manhattan, the Factory, Andy, Edie, and the Silver Sixties, although there were some good times, I am left with feeling of bittersweet melancholia. Watching Edie struggle with addiction, moving from one drug or drink to the next, I had the distinct sense that things had turned sour and was filled with anxiety and dread. There was no regard for anything or anyone else. It was a voyage into an abyss. I had become a part of it, yet I never realized that it was slowly taking our lives. I was lucky to have gotten out alive. Edie died trying.

To this day, I still can’t connect with what Raka shot in Los Angeles. I find it contrived and disturbing. These days, when I watch Ciao!, I always seem to two weeks of bad luck. As I write this, some fifty-plus years later, I working on the soundtrack for a new animated film about Edie Sedgwick’s relationships with her father and Andy called Too Late. Her memories live on, and finally, I’ve found some good from those dark times.

Meanwhile, back in New York, I was making a modest living and soon realized that I needed to leave my couch-surfing days behind. I badly needed a place to live, and I ended up moving into an apartment at the once-splendid Ansonia Hotel on Broadway, between 73rd and 74th Street.

As I settled in, quite by chance, I met Tama Starr in the foyer. I learned that she was the granddaughter of Jake Starr, who had brought the hotel out of bankruptcy forty years earlier. Jake didn’t do much to take care of the building. It needed to be brought up to code—the pipes were rusting, the roof leaked in dozens of places, and the balconies were held on with wire. The place was falling apart, a shadow of its former glory.

Tama was and is a gifted poet, writer, and storyteller. We became instant friends. She and her brother, Jonathan, occupied one of the original apartments on the second floor that looked out on Broadway and 74th Street. It was old and timeworn but stunning.

Tama shared stories about the Ansonia’s past, and I got a chance to explore the building’s old nooks and crannies with her. Many of the spaces had been closed and abandoned for years. There was a farm on the roof that was in ruins when I saw it, as well as old baggage storage rooms still filled with unclaimed steamer trunks, and an old steam-engine-powered generator in the basement dating back to the hotel’s early days, when electricity from the city was nearly nonexistent.

From the beginning, the Ansonia was designed for long-term residencies. On its official opening day, April 16, 1904, one hundred and ten families already called it home. The French Beaux-Arts–style architecture was imposing, and the building’s exterior was highly ornamented, with elegant moldings and huge windows. I learned from Tama that William Stokes, the original builder and owner, had envisioned the Ansonia as a self-sufficient utopia. The rooftop farm would supply fresh vegetables, goat milk, and eggs for the tenants; there would be twenty-four-hour room service on call, and the basement was to be furnished with Turkish baths for its residents. It was in those Turkish baths were where we had shot the spa scenes for Ciao! Manhattan.

In its original iteration, the Ansonia was a place where you could live, work, and play. It was built at a time when wealthy people usually lived in townhouses, not high-rises, so its amenities had to be five-star hotel quality. The apartments were luxurious, with multiple bedrooms, parlors, libraries, and formal dining rooms. This was designed to be a close-knit, self-sufficient community.

In its early heyday, the Ansonia was always filled with music. The legendary tenor Enrico Caruso lived in the building, as did the conductor Arturo Toscanini. Dozens of opera stars followed them to New York and took up residence in the building. Composers like Igor Stravinsky, Sergei Rachmaninoff, and Gustav Mahler were tenants. Lauritz Melchior, the foremost Wagnerian tenor in the world, lived in the building from 1926 to the early 50s.

Musicians loved the Ansonia because its walls and floors were unusually thick and virtually soundproof. They were still drawn to the Ansonia when I moved in. Many of the tenants were working singers and musicians from the Metropolitan Opera or the NY Philharmonic, along with voice coaches, piano teachers, and performers from both on and off Broadway.

I found a small, two-room apartment on the seventeenth floor. It was in the attic, which had originally been the servants’ quarters. The halls were decorated in yellow, dreary, discolored linoleum, with fluorescent lights and bare bulbs. The floor tiles were old, but the rent was more than reasonable. The apartment itself was another story. It looked out over the internal courtyard and rooftops and was filled with north light. The building was covered with lacy ornamental ironwork and corner towers topped with mansards. It was like living on the rooftops and looking out at the chimneys of Paris.

My apartment was very cozy and retained all the original interior trim from its glory days. I painted the trim silver and the walls bone white. In the living/bedroom, I had two Altec ‘Voice Of The Theater’ A7 loudspeakers powered by a Crown DC 300 amp and an Ampex 440 two-track tape recorder, an oriental rug, a mattress on the floor, a low coffee table, and a few chairs. In the other room, I had a beautiful old Steinway parlor grand piano, which I bought from a tenant who was vacating his apartment and couldnt afford to move it. Years later, that same piano traveled across the country with me and ended up in Studio B at the Record Plant in Los Angeles. Stevie Wonder would play on many of the songs we recorded with him. It was a lovely instrument.

It didn’t take very long before Tama befriended Malcolm, too, and became part of our tribe. She would make the trek to and from the studio with me on many occasions. She was a scholar and regaled us with stories of the Egyptian Pharaonic legends about death and reincarnation. The myths inspired our creation of the song ‘Cybernaut,’ which ended up on Zero Time. She wrote the lyrics for ‘River Song,’ and we named another song on the album after her, ‘Tama.’ We loved her strength and talent.

By the time I’d moved in, the Turkish baths in the basement had become the infamous Continental Baths, more fondly known by their clientele as ‘The Tubs.’ Jake Starr had leased the abandoned, marble-covered basement, with its beautifully detailed swimming pool, steam room, sauna, dining rooms, and ballroom, to a former opera singer named Steve Ostrow. He was very thin and had a substantial bleached blond pompadour lacquered to the max. He fit perfectly into the great tradition of the oddball denizens, like me, living at the Ansonia.

Steve transformed the space into the Continental Baths in 1968. The baths were a social space where gay men could meet, swim, relax, and have sex. His timing was perfect. The Stonewall riots were just a year away, and a new era of gay liberation was on its ascent. It was a weird, steamy nightclub, its mores just right for the sexual revolution. It had palm fronds, flattering spotlights, theatrical lighting, and a waterfall that emptied into the pool. Surrounding the pool was a cabaret lounge, a discothèque, and a restaurant that was always good for a late dinner or early breakfast.

The old ballroom and a few other public spaces from the old days were converted into blocks of tiny rooms, with a bed, clean sheets, fresh towels, and a nightstand. The ceilings were open and covered with a sort of chain-link material. You could hear the sounds of men making love, and lust, all night long. There was an elegant maze with trompe l’oeil-painted walls and theatrical lighting. There were several dark (orgy) rooms. The place was kept immaculately clean. The showers offered a supply of A200 ( lice-killing shampoo), a mouthwash dispenser, and KY Jelly from a candy vending machine. There was also a warning system of colored lights to tip off the patrons when the vice squad dropped in, which reminded me of the police raids on Fire Island.

Steve and I became friends, and I managed to arrange an open invitation to the Tubs via the freight elevator from my apartment on the seventeenth floor. I used to trudge home from the studio at two or three in the morning and call the front desk hey would send the freight elevator to pick me up. It was total liberation for me. I loved the place.

The most remarkable thing about the baths was the cabaret. Steve added a small dance floor and lounge with a baby grand piano. It was the perfect blend of sleazy and spectacular. People would dance around the pool in towels and bathing suits, or sometimes in the nude. Steve booked an impressive array of budding stars, including, most famously, Bette Midler, who known as ‘Bathhouse Betty.’ Her accompanist, Barry Manilow, played the piano. Patti LaBelle played the Baths too, as did Sarah Vaughan, Melba Moore, the Manhattan Transfer, and the comedian Dick Gregory. There were also many other luminaries, especially DJs, who appeared there, perfecting their art early in their careers and pioneering the dance and pop music to come. Disco and house music owe a huge debt to the Continental Baths. The sexual abandon running through the genre was a part of its musical DNA.

Bette Midler told the Houston Voice,I’m still proud of those days when I got my start singing at the Continental Baths. I feel like I was at the forefront of the gay liberation movement, and I hope I did my part to help it move forward. So, I wear the label of Bathhouse Betty with pride.’

The baths provided a sanctuary for gay men and others, regardless of their sexuality; a space where social conventions and rules did not matter, and where people could escape their ordinary lives.

Years later, after Jake Starr passed, Tama and Jonathan sold the Ansonia. It was deemed a landmark and saved from the wrecking ball. Today, it is back in its glory and home to many newly renovated condos that celebrate its storied past. Sadly, the baths have been turned into a parking garage.

Tama and I are still friends to this day. Malcolm and I would later journey with her to Maui, where she has property, which I’ve visited several times over the years. The desk I am sitting at now, writing this tale, once belonged to her, and for a time sat in my apartment at the Ansonia.

Our routine at Mediasound kept Malcolm and me busy. Away from the studio, with a wife and young son, he was living a very different reality than I was. I was living in two worlds: creative and light on the one hand, dark and erotic on the other; one above ground, the other underground, both exploring different sides of me in exciting and invigorating ways.

Malcolm and I spent as much time as possible on our music, but there was no sense that we were doing anything important. Just being able to do it and striving to do the best we could was an accomplishment in itself. We’d been at it for about six months when Herbie Mann, the jazz flutist and early proponent of world music, heard us at work in Studio A. He walked in the door and saw Malcolm standing there. Herbie knew Malcolm from his days playing acoustic bass at Ronnie Scott’s in London and asked him what he was doing here. Malcolm told him that he was the studio’s chief engineer.

Herbie had just started a record label, Embryo Records, and he was at Mediasound to check in on one of his groups who were recording in Studio B. Malcolm invited Herbie to hear what we were doing with TONTO when he was finished. When he returned, Herbie was skeptical. He wasn’t sure about this synthesizer stuff. We played him ‘Aurora.’ We put the instrument in the center of the studio’s soaring space, backlit by the stained-glass windows in all their glory, and turned up the big monitors. It sounded like the birth of the universe. It was stunning.

Herbie stood up right then and there and said, ‘Do you want a record contract? I’ve got three of my four groups. Do you want to be the fourth group?’

Herbie gave us a $5,000 advance, and for the first time in my life, I was stunned into silence.

We still needed to do more recording and mixing. We could only work on it in our time off between paying sessions. We did the final mix in a small, studio downtown called Sun Dragon. Finally, on June 15, 1971, after more than a year’s work, Zero Time was released on Herbie’s label. Much to my surprise, it received glowing accolades in a range of publications, whose reviewers recognized what we were doing with TONTO as something new, different, and exciting. In Record World, Mike Sigman wrote of how ‘TONTO captured a sense of wonder.’ He described TONTO, Malcolm, and me as ‘one of the weirder combinations of talent in recent memory’ and TONTO as an instrument that ‘seemed capable of making virtually any sound at any speed, including crashing, never-before-heard electronic sounds.’

In the issue of Rolling Stone dated August 5, 1971, Timothy Crouse wrote, ‘Listening to TONTO was like taking acid and discovering that your mind has the power to stop your heart. The realization that this instrument can do all sorts of things to you, and now that it has you, is unsettling. After all, a Moog theoretically can produce any sound and produce it instantly so that a clarinet might scale three mellow ascending notes and then on the fourth note play the sound of the sea giving up her dead.’

[MUSIC CUE - Tonto’s Expanding Headband “”]

Unbeknownst to either Malcolm or me, Stevie Wonder had also caught wind of what we were doing. As he would later recall in an interview with Elias Leight of Rolling Stone in 2021, ‘Someone had told me about Tonto’s Expanding Head Band—they’d put some music out, and they worked with a synthesizer. I was curious about that because I was very impressed with what I’d heard at the time from [electronic music pioneer Wendy Carlos. I was on a journey, because I’d just ended all my contracts with everyone. I made a decision that the money I’d accumulated in my trust fund, I would use that to create the kind of music that I wanted to.’

It was that curiosity and desire that brought Stevie to the front door of Mediasound on that fateful Memorial Day weekend.

Chapter 10

It’s Stevie time!

00:03 - 00:

[MUSIC CUE - Stevie Wonder “Superstition”]

Stevie Wonder told Rolling Stone in an interview published on April 12, 2021:

‘When I met Bob and Malcolm, I ws excited. You could create a bass sound, a horn sound, sounds you’re imagining in your head. They were trying to take it beyond the typical level. It was really an instrument of expression. I wanted something where you could bend sounds, do more with them, be more creative, not just have them be sterile sounds.

‘At that point, I would tell Malcolm or Bob the kind of sound I would want; they would work with the Moog to create it. When I got with them, there were so many things I wanted to do. There were songs I did back then; some might have appeared on Talking Book, some on Fulfillingness’ First Finale, some on Innervisions, some on Music Of My Mind. Within the first month of meeting them, I must have done about forty or fifty songs.’

That’s what Stevie said.

01:17 -

[MUSIC CUE - synth music]

As one’s life is playing out, it may seem random and full of coincidences. However, with hindsight, the paths and experiences that bring people together, regardless of their diversity, reveal themselves to be anything but accidental or serendipitous. Malcolm, Stevie, and I may have come from different worlds, but there’s no doubt that there was a common creative force—an energy that brought us together.

On that first weekend together in 1971, Stevie had just turned twenty-one. His Motown contract had expired, and he had just been paid the money that had previously been held in trust, having made music professionally since he was eleven years old. He was more than ready to leave ‘Little Stevie Wonder’ behind, shed the Motown hit-making formula, and unleash his true voice. Finally, he was his own boss, free to explore and expand his horizons. Like so many young people, then, Stevie was becoming more and more invested in the social and political struggles both he and many others faced. It was time for a new soundtrack that took on racism, poverty, love, and spirituality.

I was thirty-one, and I had just found my own voice with Malcolm and TONTO. I was living a life full of adventure. Hans Maeder and my experiences at the Stockbridge School had laid a foundation of acceptance and curiosity. Tanglewood had firmly fixed a musical mindset. The army taught me to look at the world through a camera lens, and my nights taking photos of dancers in Stuttgart showed me how to see beauty through that lens. Edie unveiled the tragic beauty of a lost soul. I had found myself and grown comfortable in my own skin on Fire Island. Malcolm helped pull all thse pieces together and gave me the structure I needed to channel all of that into a soundscape that truly felt like the universe unfolding.

Two creative forces were on a collision course: Stevie with a head and heart full of untapped creativity, looking for a voice, me with a new voice, full of sounds and sonic textures that had never previously existed. It was the perfect storm. We were about to step out, not only into the universe but into other dimensions. Malcolm, Stevie, and I were all seekers—a match made in musical heaven.

Toward the end of that first weekend, Stevie said to Malcolm and me, ‘I want you to be creative directors of my company.’ We took him seriously and immediately said yes. There was no thinking about it. That was it. Just like that, we were on Stevie Time, and it was magical.

In writing this book, putting together the pieces of our time with Stevie has proven to be trickier than expected. We didn’t work in a straight line. We never sat down to make a specific song or with an end goal in mind. It was open, free, creativity in its truest sense. We created a library of songs, parts of songs, and ideas that were then drawn on for the albums that came to be known as his ‘classic period.’ Once something was in that library, it could—and did—get taken out and end up on any of the albums. Over the three years from 1971 to 1974, we worked in four studios across two cities. We were there for Music Of My Mind, Talking Book, Innervisions, and Fulfillingness’ First Finale. The best way for me to share the stories from that time is to look first at the processes and the way we pushed musical boundaries together. After that, I’ll let my highlights from each of the records we created take center stage.

Working so directly with a recording artist was unheard of at that time. It was one of the first such relationships in the business of music. In those days, guys like us were always hired by the record company or the recording studio, but our allegiance was to Stevie and nobody else. It was just the three of us.

but the connection we made with Stevie was so strong that nothing else seemed to matter. We went with it on a handshake and trust. It felt good. It was the beginning of a wonderful, constantly evolving relationship. The start of an exciting journey. We were living in the moment, without any sense of the social effects our music would have n the world.

We would arrive at the studio around four o’clock in the afternoon. First, we would take care of all business and administrative work: logging sessions; figuring out how much money the recording tape, instruments, or other supplies we needed were going to cost; deciding which musicians we were going to work with, what time we were scheduled for recording, and so on. Then we’d listen to the tapes from the night before. I had a little book where I would keep track of all the songs we were working on. I’d make note of what worked well; where touch-ups or extra parts were needed. If we found an interesting sound or riff that didn’t have a home yet, I’d log it so that we could find it again when the right song presented itself.

Stevie, Malcolm, and I acted on impulse. Our work together became all-consuming. We were obsessed. We worked night and day, holidays and weekends. If we started recording at 7pm, we’d come out blinking into the morning light in the middle of New York at 7am. The music just kind of fell out of Stevie constantly, and we collected it and put it into a library to pull from when it came time to create an actual song.

In the beginning, there was no one else around. We had occasional visitors. Sometimes Stevie’s brother, Milton, or his cousin, John Wesley Harris, would stop by. Various lady friends or Stevie’s hippie lawyer, Johanan Vigoda, made appearances, but for the most part, the three of us were left alone, and we just went to work.

The music we made with Stevie was a fusion of electronic and live instruments. We created a new instrument with the synthesizer every time we played it. Because Stevie was blind, he couldn’t write down his arrangements, and with an unstable analog synthesizer, the sound changed over time—sometimes subtly, sometimes drastically—so there was an urgency once we got the sound we liked. That urgency added a lot of excitement to the process.

Malcolm and I worked hard to get the sounds as close as possible to what we all liked when we did the tracking. We wanted to avoid having to fix anything in the mix. Our masters were on sixteen-track tape, which necessitated more than one sound on each track. For example, the pre-mixed stereo tom-toms would share the same tracks with the overheads and ride cymbals.

I would go through and do the EQing before we started mixing. To me, music is like architecture. It’s like moving around inside a building, from room to room. That’s how I heard it. Malcolm was more formal and structural, less organic. He would tell me raise a vocal up two decibels in the chorus, always very precise and technical. But there would be an alivenessto the mix. It was a musical performance. We mixed with four hands in real time. We would rehearse all the moves and then do the mix in one pass. We had it all memorized. It was all about feel. It was about having a very physical and expressive connection to the music.

Musicians have often written music based on where the song was going to be performed, but Stevie’s music was conceived as a recording in the studio. He didn’t follow the convention most other artists used: write the songs, take them on the road or rehearse them with a band to perfect them, then come into the studio and record. He figured out how to perform it live later, not the other way around.

From the very beginning, the records we made were always about the songs first unfolding on loudspeakers in the studio and then continuing to unfold as we created them. We never thought about imitating live performance. The songs never intended to be an interpretation of a real-time event but rather were sonic soundscapes.

With an instrument like TONTO, you couldn’t write a line ahead of time. Until you get the sound set up, you don’t know how its going to react with the other sounds. We recorded one event after another, overdubbing single notes, never chords. Everything was done in a sort of improvised, jazz-like fashion. Sometimes we had arrangements, other times some of the lines would be suggested by Stevie. Sometimes there was nothing at all, and we simply followed our muse.

In the beginning, when it was just Stevie, Malcolm, and me, it felt so pure. Looking back, I wouldn’t change any of it. We caught the magic as a team, and we preserved what Stevie was trying to do. It was truly the music in his mind, and an important step in his evolution as a composer, musician, singer, and lyricist.

When we first met Stevie, he was free from his Motown contract, but he was only free for a couple of months. Although he was euphoric about guiding his own journey and traveling new sonic landscapes, I believe in his heart of hearts, he didn’t want to leave Motown. He had grown up with the label, and they were family. He had also grown past the Motown hit-making formula and wanted to assert his independence and have creative control over his work, but I do believe that his heart was still with Berry Gordy and his team. And, of course, Gordy was intent on Stevie remaining a part of the Motown family. Knowing that Stevie had already received a sizable offer from CBS became a very big motivator for him.

I first met Johanan Vigoda, Stevie’s lawyer, during the Ciao! Manhattan days. We both worked with Richie Havens, too, and our paths crossed once again when I recorded Havens at Mediasound. He had changed over the years since we first met. Back then, he looked and acted like a typical New York lawyer. Now, he had long hair, wore jeans and floral shirts with tails out, and had become a vegetarian. He looked the part of the brilliant eccentric working for the underdog recording artists. You always knew when he had been working on a document because the paperwork would be crumpled up, with coffee ring stains and dog-eared around the edges. But he knew the law inside and out, and he was a brilliant and aggressive negotiator.

In the short time we had been working together at Mediasound, Stevie, Malcolm, and I had recorded an incredible amount of music. With that music in hand, we flew to LA with Stevie and Vigoda to meet Motown at the label’s new offices at the corner of Sunset Boulevard and Argyle Avenue in West Hollywood.

Vigoda was there to negotiate a new contract for Stevie, but Malcolm and I knew that if they were able to work out a deal, we’d be relocating to Los Angeles too. Our aim was to scout out a studio that could handle us in LA. We brought our two trusty ARP 2600s with us and settled into the Hallmark Motor Hotel on Sunset.

14: - 1

[MUSIC CUE - Tonto’s Expanding Headband “Time Whys”]

On that first evening, Malcolm set up one ARP in his room and I set up the other in mine. We ran extension cords through the open pool-deck doors and got the ARPs to modulate each other’s sample and hold circuits, making them interact with one another. It was like they were having sex. It filled the courtyard with the most amazing random sounds, almost like the sounds of alien birds.

A day or two later, Malcolm and I drove Vigoda from the hotel to the Motown offices, as he didn’t drive. We found ourselves in the top-floor executive office of the label’s vice president of International Management, Ewart Abner. We’d met Ab once or twice with Stevie during our time at Mediasound. He was a small, lithe man, almost bird-like. He reminded me of Sammy Davis Jr. (Malcolm called him ‘Li’l Abner’).

Abner sat at his enormous desk in a high-backed, throne-like black leather chair on which he hung his sports jacket. There was a Motown lawyer in there with him. We were a bit late, and Vigoda was slightly disheveled, as usual, shirt tails out, clutching his paperwork in a single pile. He went into Ab’s office while we sat quietly outside with his secretary. It wasn’t long before we could hear the conversation in the other room becoming heated. The Motown lawyer was huffing and puffing.

‘Listen, schmuck,’ Vigoda asked, raising his voice, ‘where did you go to law school?’

And then: silence! Vigoda was the master of intimidation. Malcolm and I looked at each other in amazement. You could have heard a pin drop. Vigoda had done his job. On July 1, 1971, with Stevie’s recordings of the music that would become Music Of My Mind used as leverage, Stevie signed a groundbreaking new deal with Motown.

The label had met all of Stevie’s demands, granting him fifty percent ownership of his publishing and creative control of his recordings. From what I understand, the pact included an advance in excess of $900,000 (plus annual guarantees) in addition to a then-unheard-of fourteen percent royalty rate. This new deal was revolutionary, and it paved the way for other artists to demand (and receive) far more control over their work.

With his new deal in place, Stevie returned to the open, welcoming arms of Motown. He set up his own publishing company, which he named Black Bull Music after his astrological birth sign, Taurus. The deal was a perfect fit for Stevie. Everyone was excited and ready to get back to the business of making music. Malcolm and I thought that our futures were secured, too,

Alongside our work with Stevie, our lives were rapidly accelerating. In New York, I was doing my daily hike from the Ansonia to the studio and was still doing commercial sessions with the synthesizer for the likes of Trans Caribbean Airways, Ford Turino, Crazy Daisy Toilet Paper.

A month or so before we started working with Stevie, Malcolm’s wife, Poli, and their son, Milton, arrived from Toronto, Canada. The immigration process was insane, and Malcolm spent a lot of time sorting it out on the phone. He was very distracted by it, and I was, too. Once Poli and Milton settled in, Malcolm’s apartment next door to Mediasound, which was once roomy and more than enough for just him, turned into a crowded home for three.

Before we knew it, Stevie had become a full-time, twenty-four-seven project. It was evident that Malcolm and I had to give up our day jobs. When Malcolm resigned from his job at Mediasound, he was no longer entitled to the free apartment next door, so he had no choice but to move to a bigger apartment.

I was facing some of the same space issues. Although I loved living at the Ansonia, I was growing tired of sleeping on a mattress on the floor my living room, hippie-style. I wanted a new heated waterbed—they were the latest craze—and, frankly, I needed more living space. We needed room for a production office, too, as we no longer had access to the offices at Mediasound. We still worked there, but as clients instead of employees. We kept several of our own clients—mostly commercials and acts on Richie Havens’s record label—but we were employed mainly by Stevie.

By good fortune, Malcolm and I both found spacious, two-bedroom apartments in a lovely pre-World War II building at the corner of 72nd Street and Broadway. My second bedroom and living room became our new production offices. My Altec A7 loudspeakers and Steinway grand piano fit beautifully in the living room, and we were all under one roof. Finally, Malcolm and his family had enough room to spread out. e could share a taxi to the studio, and I got to slosh around on my new heated waterbed. It was perfect.

We spent a little over six intense months working with Stevie at Mediasound, and although we had tracked some of Stevie’s most memorable songs there and built an archive of many more, including some that were almost finished, we realized it wasn’t possible to consider mixing there. The studios were getting busier and busier, and there were other clients clamoring to use Studio B.

In those days, there was no such thing as recording console automation, and being able to leave mix up on the desk overnight was impossible. The fragile settings on TONTO (which was growing bigger and more complex) were also creating an impossible situation, as we were forced to break everything down and set it up over and over again. It became a huge waste of time and money for us, Stevie, and the studio. These disruptions were getting in the way of our flow. It was time to move.

Jimi Hendrix had opened Electric Lady Studios on August 26, 1970, naming it after his album Electric Ladyland. He sought to bend the boring institutional studio standard into something magical and lasting. It was a renegade concept inspired by his quest for creative innovation and independence, and the very first artist-inspired, artist-owned studio. Jimi conceived of Electric Lady with his own artistic needs and comfort in mind. He wanted it to be a place where he and his friends could, in Jimi’s own words, ‘he abstract universal language of music.’

The studio was designed by John Storyk, a twenty-two-year-old architect fresh out of Princeton. Electric Lady was his first recording studio commission. Hendrix took a chance on him because he’d been impressed by Cerebrum, the East Village nightclub Storyk had designed (and, coincidentally, where I had heard my first Moog synthesizer a few years earlier).

Hendrix, of course, barely got to use his magnificent studio. Just three weeks after Electric Lady Studios opened, he passed away in a London apartment on September 18, 1970, after overdosing on barbiturates. He was twenty-seven years old. What a tragedy.

In our search for a new home, we met with Jim Marron, the studio boss, and Eddie Kramer, Jimi’s producer and director of engineering. They had been Jimi’s confidantes and co-conspirators and were in charge of Electric Lady’s construction, operations, and engineering. In many ways, they had been to Jimi what Malcolm and I were to Stevie.

Upon entering the studio from 8th Street, we descended a steep staircase and emerged into an underground fantasy land with sophisticated theatrical lighting, Persian carpets, Chesterfield sofas, and fringed lamps. Everywhere you looked, there was art, musical instruments, and recording equipment. In the hall and in Studio A were incredible murals painted by the California artist Lance Jost depicting an intergalactic spacecraft with blond nymphs perched on huge windows looking out on a galactic universe floating by. It was like a New Orleans bordello that had somehow found its way on board a psychedelic spaceship.

Based on our experiences at Mediasound, we knew how important finding the right studio could be, and Electric Lady couldn’t have been better. We could come and go as we pleased, and the décor was much more creative and inspiring than any other contemporary studio I had ever seen. The studios were warm and fuzzy, touched by art and music, not ad agencies and union dates.

We played back some of our rough mixes in the control room, and what we heard blew us away. We immediately fell madly in love with Electric Lady. To this day, I believe that showing up there when we did was some kind of divine intervention. Once again, God must have had his hand on our shoulders. Stevie, Malcolm, and I filled Jimi’s shoes, and it was a perfect fit. It was good for all of us, and since Stevie Wonder, not Motown, employed us, we took care of business on his behalf. We had found a new home.

As fate would have it, Electric Lady was also in the heart of the gay community in Greenwich Village, which was known as Boys Town. These had been my stomping grounds even before the Ciao! Manhattan days. Bars, clubs, men cruising the streets, and the iconic Stonewall bar were just around the corner. The Stonewall riots were still a fresh memory.

We moved into Electric Lady in November and December of 1971 and immediately picked up where we had left off at Mediasound. There were two studios: Studio A, where we set up shop, and Studio B. They shared a lounge with a barber’s chair and a couch, some playback equipment and a pair of speakers, and a reception/coffee area, which was triangular and had a huge round column in the center, maybe eight feet in diameter, with a bench built around it.

Stevie quickly learned the studio layout and the hallways—where Studio B was, where the stairs were, the layout of Studio A, the control room doors, the lounge, the bathrooms, and so on. It wasn’t long before he was moving around happily and confidently on his own. Malcolm and I made sure that we gave him an auditory picture any time we made changes to the studio and that he knew where any microphone stands were. As always, we kept the mic high so that he wouldn’t walk into them and get clobbered by those or any other unpredictable obstructions. The studio’s architecture played a part. There were no sharp edges or corners. Even the windows in the doors were round. The whole place felt inviting, relaxed, and safe.

The control room was wide but not deep, so all the instruments and TONTO lived in the studio. We still utilized the always-live, open-mic technique that we’d employed at Mediasound. The studio glass disappeared, and I could see that Stevie felt right at home, as did Malcolm and I.

There were no restrictions at Electric Lady. I never felt tired. It didn’t matter if we needed to work three days in a row, leave a mix on the console, or stay set up in the studio; they bent over backwards to make it happen. Eddie Kramer had installed an off-brand Data Mix recording console, some great studio monitors, and a ton of custom equipment. It was a beautiful and inspiring place to work.

A few days after we arrived at the studio, a young lady, Joan De Cola, started dropping into our sessions, always standing in the back. She said she worked upstairs in the business office but wanted to become an engineer. There was always a second engineer to represent the studio and take care of our needs, set up gear, make coffee, and support the primary engineer. Joan stepped into the role. We liked her energy, and we could see that she was hands-on and driven. On top of all that, she could curse like a sailor.

It was a historic move. Back then, an apprenticeship was the only way you learned your craft, and in those days in the recording business, working in a studio was almost exclusively a male thing. Women were relegated to the front desk, business offices, and public relations roles. Joan became one of the first female engineers in the studio world and went on to have a very successful career long after her work with us.

The other blessing was that we never heard a word from Stevie about being over-budget or anything like that. He allowed us to concentrate on the music without worrying about whether or not we’d be able to pay the bills or put food on the table.

After we moved into the Lady and established ourselves there, John Storyk showed up and introduced himself. That night began a friendship that has endured for more than fifty years. We both share a passion for music and architecture. I think that if I had not into the world of music, I would have become an architect. To this day, I often think of music from an architectural perspective and compare creating a recording to designing a house.Each sound is like separate room with its own form, furnishing, and colors. Once you piece that together, the house itself takes shape. Then you need to think about how the recording fits with everything else. You’d ask, ‘Does the house fit into the neighborhood?’ ‘How does it look from the street?’ Many architects I’ve worked with over the years have been great musicians. John Storyk is an incredible pianist, for example, and I know that his musical ear was invaluable to his success designing studios.

We were impressed with John’s design of the studio. As our friendship grew, he told us how he was influenced by classical architects like Antonio Gaudi and Frederick Keisler, who, by coincidence, had designed the facade and auditorium of the theater that occupied the ground floor above Electric Lady. The studio was in the basement and took up three additional floors above the theater. John used a combination of organic architectural forms, curves, and theatrical mood lighting to help Jimi realize and control his creative inner visions.

At that point, TONTO was still a piecemeal construction that we rolled around on hospital gurneys. Inspired by John’s vision of Electric Lady, we asked him if he would be interested in designing a housing to hold the various synthesizer modules. He immediately and enthusiastically said yes.

At last, all of our thoughts and plans, dating back to our fever dreams at the Spanish/Chinese restaurant across the street from Mediasound, were about to be realized. We had many meetings with John. He measured our heights and reach, considered our synth modules present and future, noted where we wanted to have them, and conceptualized all the ergonomics of an ideal setup. Our intent was simple: to create a modern orchestra of synthesizers, featuring modules from Oberheim, Moog, ARP, EMS, Surge, and some proprietary modules designed by Malcolm.

John envisioned TONTO living inside a sphere, which was a stroke of genius. It allowed us to face each other and gave easy reach to all the controls without stretching or running from one end of the instrument to the other, as it was more than fourteen feet long. Before John, TONTO was almost impossible to program and play in real time as it was set up on several different racks and tables. It was like a body wrapped in wire. We gave John the go-ahead to get started, and TONTO was about to become the most advanced synthesizer in music. John said he knew a great cabinet maker in Woodstock, and with that began the construction of TONTO as we know it today.

Before John started work, we did our first live performances with TONTO. I had maintained my connection with Hans Maeder and Stockbridge, and he was well aware of TONTO. He invited Malcolm and me to come to the school for a concert. In December 1971, we packed up all the pieces and made the 140-mile drive north. We did some live improvisations. I remember one improv in particular. Malcolm named it, was called, “Don’t Play Marbles with Me Dad’s Glass Eye Because He’s Looking for a Job.” Tama Starr came with us and danced to some of the songs.

A couple of months later, while John’s plans for TONTO were coming together, Malcolm and I were invited to give a concert at the Trinity Church on Wall Street. How could we say no? It seemed to be the actualization of what we had been planning and hoping for since Malcolm and I first met at Mediasound. Unfortunately, TONTO was still in its old form. We spent a few days patching and programming the monster with the plan of taking it to Trinity Church, all set up.

On the afternoon of the gig, it was drizzling, so we covered the fully programmed and patched synthesizer (along with a full PA system) with blankets and transported it downtown. By showtime, it was still hot and humid, with a little rain and some rumbling thunder and lightning.

We thought we would play some songs from Zero Time, but TONTO had a mind of its own. It didn’t like to be touched, much less moved, and when we started to play, it went utterly bonkers. Our planned program promptly turned into a completely improvised electronica/jazz mashup. Malcolm, the jazz musician that he was, was in his element. And I was sweating bullets.

God’s gift was that the church was acoustically ideal, just like Mediasound Studio A. It was a huge, majestic, reverberant space. No matter what we did, it sounded like we were in touch with the voice of God. As lightning flashed outside, the stained-glass windows would light up, adding to a sense of majesty. All in all, the concert was a success, but the stress it put me under only reinforced my sense that I belonged in the studio, behind the scenes, not on stage.

Once Storyk delivered his phenomenal cabinet array, and we put it all together, TONTO became a musical instrument that you lived inside. It had a massive footprint (at least for a musical instrument) to hold all its synths, electronics, and wiring, and filled up a small room. It was twenty-five feet in diameter and weighed over a thousand pounds.

TONTO’s keyboards were monophonic, but with multiple keyboards, we could layer up to six voices at a time, creating an incredible range of complex, electronic events. Each sound had sonic content going on inside of it which was constantly changing and evolving. What sounded like a reed or brass sound was actually a combination of multiple waveforms working together to achieve a cohesive sound.

We were blazing new sonic and technical trails at every tune. Much of the technology needed to connect the multiple synthesizer modules that we used didn’t exist, and Malcolm, in his brilliance, created solutions, many of which are still in use today.

Malcolm invented a first-of-its-kind joystick controller, which was an analog pitch and filter modulator. The expression it added to the synths can’t be overstated.

He extracted a joystick from a model airplane radio controller and mounted it in a small metal box with a black plastic top plate. He attached some rubber bands underneath, so the stick always snapped back to the center when you let go of it. Inside the box were two voltage controllers. The one on the vertical axis was used for filter modulation, and the other on the horizontal axis was for pitch. Two small knobs controlled the amount of each effect.

The stick sat on the left-hand side of the keyboard. We were able to bend and control the pitch and tonality simultaneously. Its use wasanalogous to an electric guitar player bending a string and using a wah-wah pedal at the same time. It was totally Malcolm’s brilliant invention. We should’ve patented it! Now, joysticks are taken for granted on most electronic musical keyboard instruments.

Stevie got it immediately and jumped all over it. It inspired him to create his bass line on ‘Boogie On Reggae Woman.’ The joystick brought funk to the synthesizer.

3

[ CUE: Stevie Wonder "Boogie On Reggae Woman"]

On Annie Mac’s podcast Sidetrack, Stevie shared how that sound and the joystick enabled the laidback, bluesy bass that defined the song and is often cited as one of the best bass lines in pop music. He also said that to get an even more laidback vibe, he drank a beer to add the same slurring sound to his voice that we achieved with TONTO.

A Master Tuning Bus was another one of Malcom’s creations that made playing TONTO even more expressive. In audio, a bus combines multiple signals into one channel. Ours allowed us to perform global processing that affected all the parts. When you played a bass line, for example, the bus enabled us to make the entire instrument, including the sequencers, transpose into any key to match the line being played. That way, as long as I played only on the white notes, whatever I played would always be in the correct key. I couldn’t make any mistakes—it was brilliant.

TONTO’s brain, which Malcolm had conceived on the tablecloth in that Chinese/Spanish restaurant across the street from Mediasound, was very cranky and temperamental. Still, it allowed us to communicate electronically with modules from many different designers, including Moog, Arp, Serge, Malcolm, and others. Nothing was standardized.

Once the modules were installed in John Storyk’s custom console, TONTO was a breeze to work on and wire from the back because the curved cases splayed the rear of the modules outward, leaving plenty of space for wiring. We unified all the separate power supplies into one centralized, highly accurate power supply, with thick-gauge wire procured from surplus supplies made for the Apollo mission and Boeing 747 parts. It provided the required voltages to each of the modules and brought a marked improvement in TONTO’s stability.

Being all analog, TONTO was like a new instrument every time we played it. Every time we touched one knob, the sound changed. It was a question of stability and active repeatability, which was a part of the magic and the curse of early electronica. Today, of course, people can produce any sound, record the performance, save it digitally, and manipulate it in any way they want after the fact. When we were creating our records, we had to do everything in real time, remembering all the moves in case we needed to recreate the sounds. We were the memory, not the computer.

The idea of combining different sound generators into a modular synthesizer system, which TONTO heralded, is still cutting edge and lives on in modular synths like the Eurorack. These systems are based on modules that all share the same physical height with varying widths that conform to one another. One can acquire modules, racks, and cases from many manufacturers, knowing that they can all be assembled together into a single unit. It’s like TONTO had a grandchild, except the Eurorack is one that is far easier to control and manipulate.

After more than fifty years, people still ask me how they can get some of those great TONTO sounds, like the bass on ‘Boogie On Reggae Woman.’ I always say that you can get close, but those sounds only existed in that one specific time and place, and you just can’t recreate them perfectly. TONTO was just too perfect in its analog imperfection!

4

[MUSIC CUE: Stevie Wonder “Boogie On Reggae Woman”]
