Chapter 11

Go West: the Record Plant years

Once Stevie’s new Motown deal was in place, we continued to work in New York and tracked songs and parts of songs that would show up on future records. However, with Motown in Los Angeles, it wasn’t long before we started working there too, and for a short time we were flying back and forth—a true bi-coastal operation. Music Of My Mind exemplifies this nomadic period. We started out at Mediasound, then moved to Electric Lady, and eventually, after Stevie began negotiating with Motown, we continued at our newest discovery, Crystal Studios in Los Angeles. Talking Book started life in New York but ended up being mixed and mastered in LA.

In those days, passengers dressed up for flights, so we always looked a little out of place in our everyday hippie outfits. We usually flew on 747s, which had a piano bar and a lounge on the upper deck. It was a different time. We always carried Stevie's master tapes on our laps. We were religious about that. No way were those going in our luggage—we didn’t trust anyone else with them.

One vivid memory stands out. We were flying west at dusk on the Fourth of July, and as we traveled into the night, I could see fireworks lighting up the skies below—to the left and the right, bursts of color across the country—while above us, the stars and the moon shimmered in the quiet. It was unforgettable.

As usual, we stayed at what had become our regular haunt—the Hallmark Motel on Sunset, just down the street from Motown’s new offices. But it wasn’t long before plans were made to relocate permanently to Los Angeles. Over time, we moved our entire kit and kaboodle. Our tribe consisted of me, Malcolm and his family, and Dennis Pelletier, a former boyfriend of mine who by proximity had become our very competent live-in bookkeeper and had taken over Bobby Anderson’s role as my majordomo.

We put TONTO and all our earthly belongings in the hands of my boyfriend, Michael Korhonen. Michael headed west in a large U-Haul van with everything we owned and my new puppy, Tako—a gift from my old mate Raka Weisman—and we followed. Once we arrived, we settled back into the Hallmark. Malcolm’s wife Poli and Dennis immediately started looking for a place to call home, and we got to work with Stevie.

We had done some studio scouting on our first trip west and had found Crystal Studios on Vine Street and Santa Monica Boulevard, close to Motown’s offices. We did a couple of trial sessions there with Stevie and were really happy with the results.

Crystal was built in an old post office and was on the cutting edge of the industry at the time. It had three rooms. Studio A was the big room, Studio B was a smaller mixing room, and there was a mastering room with a highly modified Neuman cutting lathe. It was a one-stop shop. You could record, mix, and master your album all in one place. Studio A had a huge control room. It was like both a studio and an electronic design laboratory. Much of the equipment was either one-off designs or hot-rodded gear created by the gifted Andrew Berliner, who had also built the magnificent, straightforward recording/mixing console. Its unique six-band EQ Playskool-sized knobs and its sonic transparency were extraordinary. To this day, I have never heard a better-sounding recording console.

Malcolm and I loved the Crystal control room and its king-sized monitors. It was big enough to accommodate Stevie’s keyboard rig with ease, while the main recording room was so big it could hold an orchestra and housed a nine-foot Yamaha grand piano. Best of all, there was plenty of room for TONTO.

Dennis and Poli found a wonderful house for all of us on Sumac Lane in Santa Monica Canyon. It was in the Spanish colonial revival style, covered in flowering bougainvillea, with a gabled red tile roof and casement windows. The house was L-shaped. One part of the L featured a huge, open kitchen space with an island that separated it from the living room. The kitchen faced west, with sliding glass doors opening onto a patio with a spectacular view of the Pacific Ocean. The other side of the L had two stories containing bedrooms, washrooms, and other spaces that could be used as offices. Windows on the side looked out onto a large kidney-shaped swimming pool with a purple underwater light and a hammock stretched across one end of the pool.

The hammock was the perfect place to relax and smoke a joint while you dunked your butt in the warm water. Mature palm trees surrounded the pool. It was blissful.

I got the master bedroom and bath. It had a fireplace and a private entrance, which I shared with my boyfriend, Michael, and my dog, Tako. Malcolm, Poli, Milton, and Dennis shared the rest of the place. It was crowded, but it worked. After all, we were a ‘hippie tribe with a mission!’

Stevie came by for a visit, and Poli made a great dinner. Malcolm offered him a puff on a joint. sat quietly for a few minutes and said, ‘It’s making my head feel tight.’ He didn’t dig it at all. It was the only time in all the years I’ve known him that I’ve seen Stevie use anything.

When we first arrived in LA, we bought a very used dark blue Renault sedan to go to and from the studio and make house calls to other clients with our ARP 2600 synthesizers. Happily, these house calls were becoming more and more frequent. We were at it night and day, and I didn’t have much time to think about my own needs or emotional issues. I remember our voyages to Amigo Studios in North Hollywood to work with Ted Templeman on The Doobie Brothers’ album The Captain & Me. We added synth parts to the songs ‘Natural Thing,’ ‘Clear As The Driven Snow,’ ‘South City Midnight Lady,’ and ‘Ukiah.’

Even though we were a hippie tribe on a mission, our communal lifestyle soon grew stressful. It wasn’t a bed of roses. In New York, we each had our own apartments, and I had my space and privacy. With all of us under one roof, I was no longer the king of my castle, and privacy issues came to the fore. It felt like I had become a guest in Malcolm and Poli’s family home, and then there were emerging conflicts between my boyfriend, Michael, and my ex, Dennis. Three gay men living together with a hetero family created an understandable degree of stress.

After a few months, Michael said he’d had enough. Much to my dismay, he decided to move on. He was a wonderful man, an acrobat and gifted photographer, and I have often regretted the loss of him. Sadly, I let my emotional issues slide and kept my resentments to myself. I blamed it on the business.

We stayed at the Sumac Lane house for about a year. When our lease expired, Poli found a big ranch house near Point Dume in Malibu. It had a pool and a huge living room that would eventually become TONTO’s studio. It was an additional twenty miles from the studio, north on the Pacific Coast Highway, which was often clogged with Malibu commuters and summertime beach traffic, adding another forty-five minutes to our commute to our eventual home base, at the Record Plant Los Angeles. I moved in and lived there until Malcolm and I had firmly settled into our work routine, then I decided to move on. The commute and the overall living arrangement had become intolerable. I found a beautiful beach house, much closer to Hollywood, on a narrow, rocky stretch of the beach. The house was built on pilings, thirty feet from the edge of the Pacific Coast Highway, at a place called Big Rock. I had finally found somewhere I could be myself.

Crystal Studios was a great fit for us, but our days there were numbered from the start. It was already one of the hot LA studios. Furthermore, it catered to acoustic musicians like Jackson Browne, James Taylor, Joni Mitchell, and all the folkies from Laurel Canyon. With Stevie block-booking the place, they couldn’t accommodate anyone else, and their old clientele was raising a ruckus. We were asked, politely, to move on. So, off we went in search of a new place to drop anchor.

In another stroke of seemingly divine intervention, Gary Kellgren—who along with Chris Stone had opened the Record Plant in New York back in 1968—heard through the grapevine that we were looking for a new studio for Stevie and TONTO. He and Chris had expanded their operations in 1970 with a second Record Plant in Los Angeles, and he wanted to pitch us on working there.

Though I was familiar with the Record Plant in New York, I had never actually worked there. But the recording business was small and personal—everybody knew everybody. My comrade Tom Flye from Lothar & The Hand People had become one of the Plant’s star recording engineers and mixers, and Malcolm had briefly worked there before landing at Mediasound. So, Gary and Chris’s invitation felt right. And our first meeting was seismic—literally.

We met up at Gary’s house, an old Tudor mansion on Camino Palmero Street in Hollywood. It was dusk. The place was surrounded by overgrown pine trees, and it looked dark and haunted. I remember Gary saying that it was the former Canadian consulate. We sat across from one another in the dining room. The room was huge, with dark wood paneling, a high-beamed ceiling, and a big chandelier. The four of us sat at a table long enough to serve dinner for twelve. It was impressive.

Gary and Chris’s personalities were so different. Gary was talented, animated, sensitive, and inspiring, and he did most of the talking. He had a glint in his eye, and he reminded me of a naughty, mischievous elf. Chris, on the other hand, was always the businessman, restrained, quiet, and serious.

We got down to business and had a lengthy, detailed conversation about our needs. They offered to rebuild Studio B to our specifications, including a space for TONTO and a separate, secure tape library for Stevie’s masters—if we booked the room for a year. The one condition was that they reserved the right to let other musicians book Studio B when we werent using it.

This was exactly what we were looking for. We reached an agreement in principle. Malcolm and I were delighted. We shook hands, and Gary brought out a bottle of Courvoisier brandy. He poured a snifter for each of us. We stood and lifted our glasses in a toast. And then, at the very moment our glasses clinked, there was an earthquake. The chandelier started swinging back and forth, and I could hear the pots and pans in the kitchen clanging around. It was my first experience of an earthquake, and it gave new meaning to the term rock’n’roll. higher power had put his hand on our shoulders once again. It was an omen, and it changed everything.

At our meeting, Gary and Chris gave us the go-ahead to get John Storyk on board. Later that week, we all visited the studio with Tom Hidley—the chief engineer/acoustician, soon to become a world-famous studio designer—and made plans to get started. This was a historical confluence of energies. It was obvious that we shared the same philosophy, design concepts, and influences. The Record Plant in New York was where Jimi Hendrix had been inspired to build his own studio, which Eddie Kramer and John Storyk had made a reality. Now, John, Tom, Gary, Chris, Malcolm, and I would be bringing the new Record Plant LA Studio online, and it was inevitable that it would have the same warm fuzzy feeling. What a brain trust—a match made in heaven.

The Record Plant LA was a psychedelic knockout and a tour de force in acoustic design and high technology. Gary set the mood through his very creative interior design and theatrical lighting. It was all about the environment and ensuring a level of relaxation for the creatives and their ideas. The studios’ interiors were continually evolving masterworks of design. I believe that it was the only studio with a full-time carpenter/cabinet maker on the staff. Gary made sure it was comfortable for the musicians and engineers alike.

As you pulled into the parking lot, you knew from the cars who was in charge. Chris drove a big slate gray Mercedes sedan and always parked in the last space on the left. His vanity license plate fittingly read DEDUCT. He took care of all the business with a steady hand, managed the logistics and politics, and made sure that everybody got paid. His office door was always open, and he often played the role of father confessor. He was wheeler-dealer and the financial wizard behind it all.

Gary, on the other hand, drove a two-tone purple-and-silver 1965 Rolls-Royce. He rarely parked it himself. Instead, when he could get away with it, he’d leave the motor running and the door open and step out with a cooler filled with ice cubes, champagne, and orange juice, and sometimes a lady or two on his arm. He expected one of the studio’s ‘gofers’ to come out and park the Rolls for him. Gary was a visionary, eccentric free spirit, and a spectacular recording engineer. His license plate read GREED. It was his running joke about the record business.

The studio’s front desk and office space opened onto the parking lot through a sliding glass door. You could see who was coming and going. It was like a fishbowl. In those days, Fran Hughes ran the show. She decided who got buzzed in and who didn’t. She was like the studio mother. Everybody had to pass her inspection on the way in or out. She kept a huge paper calendar on a rolling easel in front of her with all the studio booking info. She was the one who adjusted the schedule, tracked who was on time and who was running over, assigned second engineers, and kept the wheels in motion. She had a fat lead pencil and a rubber eraser. It was always a juggling act, and she ruled the roost with an iron hand.

There was a big glass jug on the front desk, and in it were these big lapel buttons that read ‘JFYJ’ (‘Jump For Your Job’). The interns who were the studio gofers, janitors, and doers of a hundred other jobs each had to wear one. It was their rite of passage—the only way to earn their stripes.

When Fran saw someone heading for the door from the parking lot, she usually had some kind of snarky comment ready. She would not allow any groupie behavior when someone famous walked in the door. She made sure that every visitor belonged to a session and was expected—no riff raff. She ran a tight ship. The Record Plant was never closed, but somebody from the crew was always at that desk twenty-four-seven. Security was very important.

After a few months of settling in, I realized that the Record Plant was way more than a recording studio business. It was a musical collective and creative destination.

You could go out in the hall and pass well-known musicians playing pinball in the canteen or just walking around at any time, day or night. Being in a place like that was like being in a collaborative Petri dish. Even if they were not in a session, major recording artists would come by just to hang out. If you were on the A-list, you made it through Fran’s security. It was a hive of creativity. I took it for granted, and I thought it would go on forever.

Stevie, Malcolm, and I were permanent fixtures in the studio in those days. Stevie mastered air hockey in the canteen, making some employees question if he was really blind. There was this guy named Mal who worked for either John Lennon or Phil Spector—I can’t remember which. One day, he shot off a pistol in the men’s room.

You could hear the bullets zinging off the tile walls. Chris rightly lost his mind and banished him from the studio for life.

The canteen was just down the hall from Studio A.

It had a huge tropical fish tank built into the wall at eye level. You could look through it from the hall or the canteen and see angel fish, betas, and neon tetras swimming. Musicians, engineers, and occasionally record company executives in their sports letter jackets, with their hangers-on, would gather and schmooze. Sometimes they’d be visiting each other’s sessions, sometimes they’d be in the bedrooms and lounges across the back parking lot.

Beer was twenty-five cents a bottle from a well-stocked, repurposed soda vending machine.

Past the canteen was the most talked-about, self-indulgent pleasure zone, the infamous Jacuzzi Room. If you mentioned to anyone that you worked at the Record Plant, the first question that was always asked, with a wink and a nod, was about that room.

The Jacuzzi itself had blue tiles and sat under a skylight, so you could look up and see the stars at night. There was a lock on the door and a big waterbed in the corner. It could be relaxing or erotic, depending on what you had in mind, and clothing was optional. It defined sex, drugs, and rock’n’roll, and I had my share.

The Jacuzzi Room was directly across the hall from Studio B. At the end of the hall, around a sharp right turn, was Chris’s office. His door was always open, and you were always welcome. His desk faced the door, so he could keep an eye on who and what was going on. Behind him was his bookkeeper’s office and a private door to the parking lot.

Behind Studio B was a huge old soundstage, which later became Studio C while we were working on Fulfillingness’ First Finale. It had a huge control room, and the studio was big enough for major string and horn dates.

Across the back parking lot and into another building were the bedrooms. There were four in total, each one built in all its decadent glory by Gary and his various collaborators. ach had a theme, from light and airy to dark and dangerous. It was both a fantasy and a lifestyle. You could book a bedroom when you booked your studio. It was a place to take a shower or a nap, relax, have some privacy, or dive headfirst into rock’n’roll excess. If you can imagine it, it very likely happened in those bedrooms.

The first room was the Sissy Room. The ceilings were at least fourteen feet high. It had a huge wicker king-size bed with a canopy draped in wispy white voile and a skylight, and the walls were all airbrushed to look like you were in the blue sky with fluffy white clouds. There was an ensuite bathroom, .

The Rack Room was next, built for rock stars who like sleeping arrangements with a twist. It had a double bed hanging from ropes with ceiling pulleys and a winch at its head. There were sheepskin cuffs and restraints and a mirror on the ceiling, and the bathroom door had bars on the window.

The Boat Room was a ship’s cabin with brass portholes, a couch, a desk, and a double bunk bed up a ladder, close to the enclosed ceiling. The bed had a mirror four feet above it. It was very cozy, and the only room without an ensuite bathroom.

Finally, a tiny secret room called the ‘Anne Frank Room’ was hidden inside the ‘Boat Room.’ You entered it through a door under the loft bed. There was a small bed and a telephone—perfect if you wanted to disappear. As far as I know, there are no pictures of that room.

Our first major project at the Record Plant involved mixing the recordings we had done at Mediasound and Electric Lady in New York. This was also my first experience with the power of immersive, surround-sound audio. First up was ‘Superstition.’ We mixed it, and later tracked Stevie’s sessions, using a four-channel quadraphonic system. This gave not only the left and right channels of a stereo mix but also a three-dimensional depth we hadn’t heard before. I began to think about how my brain knew where a sound was coming from, and I learned that head-related transfer function (HRTF) is as powerful as pitch and duration. The unique, highly asymmetric construction of our outer ear and the fact that we have two of them—plus our very efficient brain to process the input—allows for a rapid and precise determination of where a sound is generated. The difference in distances (and therefore arrival times) and slight sound coloration by the ears and the head is HRTF, and it varies slightly from person to person due to the ear and head shape.

Monitoring in quad in the studio was compelling and magical. It made all the difference. At the time, quadraphonic was becoming a significant innovation in the audio industry. It was being embraced by several major music labels, such as EMI, Decca, and RCA, as well as recording artists like Pink Floyd, The Beatles, and The Who. None of us had any experience with it, but the Record Plant always had to have the latest and greatest of everything, so we dove in happily, headlong into the unknown.

It was relatively easy to set up by adding two speakers in the back of the room, just like the ones in the front. The room already had a compression sloped ceiling, which was part of Hidley’s original design and created a focused listening environment. We constructed a bass trap over the back half of the control room ceiling was cantilevered over the hallway behind the back wall. The new rear speaker soffits stuck out into the hall. We had to put padding on them because they were at ear level.

The additional space invisibly doubled the cubic volume of the control room. The good news was that the sloped ceiling and the additional trapping gave us an impressively powerful, phase-coherent, clean bottom end. The bad news was that when the sound waves from the sloped ceiling and the parallel sloped console surface combined, they created a subtle ‘comb filter’ effect when you were seated at the mixing position in front of the console. This effect occurs when the same sound wave arrives at the listener’s ears at slightly different times. It only takes a few milliseconds’ difference to make certain frequencies sound a little hollow. Malcolm and I only noticed this several months later, when we started mixing. It wasn’t apparent initially, and thankfully it never became a significant issue. We solved the problem by gaffer-taping a blanket over our mixing position at the console, which controlled the reflections.

The control room was small, but we had enough space to place Stevie’s instrument stack in the center of the room, just behind the console: a Fender Rhodes 88, a Hohner Clavinet with a special bender bar on top, sometimes an ARP 2600, and his collection of effects boxes, notably a Mu-Tron Bi-Phase. We pushed things right to the edge when we brought Stevie into the control room to perform while he listened to everything in quad. Now we were doing something different, beyond just making quad recordings. Quad monitoring at a high volume on those Hidley monitors proved to be an amazing tool to get the best possible performances out of . You could feel the sonic energy pounding your chest and moving through your body. It was like being onstage. Having the artist in the control room during the recordings was a gift. It enabled Stevie, Malcolm, and me to occupy the same sonic space within the music. We could stay focused, hear what we got on tape, and make EQ and effects changes on the fly, without having to go back and forth between the studio and the control room between takes. That saved considerable time and energy, and we were getting great results. This became the way we worked on all Stevie’s recordings.

For me, tracking in stereo gave me a sound picture of the band standing on a stage in front of me inside a proscenium arch, making the music objective, which was fine recording a symphony orchestra. However, we were no longer listening from a distance, sitting in the audience, with a third-person point-of-view and a soundstage in front of us. With a quadraphonic system, we were hearing the music all around us, with a first-person point of view: lead vocal in front of us, background vocals behind us, the guitar on the left, the drums in the front, the congas behind you, doubled clavinet parts opposed at 180 degrees. We were able to create a sense of space without resorting to echo reverb. We placed the music around Steve in real time and refined the directionality of each sound, which brought a different kind of emotional awareness to the music.

Unfortunately, our quad mixes didn’t translate to the average person’s vinyl record system. You needed to purchase a special system to play this music back as we heard it in the studio. In 1972, you simply could not store enough information on a vinyl record, so we ditched it when it came to the final commercial album. But monitoring in quad was an invaluable tool. It brought a new sonic awareness, and is one of the reasons Stevie’s recordings sound so present, even in stereo.

These days, more than fifty years later, with object-oriented audio and digital processes like Dolby Atmos, we can put a sound object anywhere inside a 360-degree bubble and store it there digitally. This is changing the way we compose and perform music. Today’s digital recordings are truly native performances, and storing spatial information digitally is a big game-changer. The deeper dimensions that spatial audio adds when listening with headphones is a powerful and emotional part of the listening experience. We live with our music in a digital universe.

Music is no longer a a mix of live and electronica that lives solely in the medium itself. I began that ride over fifty years ago with our first attempt at mixing ‘Superstition’ in quadraphonic sound, and it continues through Dolby Atmos today. What a ride.

Outside of the studio, my life was going great. I had settled into my house at Big Rock in Malibu. The Big Rock itself was directly across Pacific Coast Highway. It loomed high over the highway, and sometimes, when it rained, mud and rocks would tumble down the cliff and onto the street in front of house. It always felt a little dangerous. I think that is why I got such a good deal on the rent. The house had two floors. The first included a living room, a kitchen, a small guest room, a bath, and a big deck facing the ocean. The second floor, reached by an exterior stairway, led to master bed and bath, a walk-in closet, and floor-to-ceiling windows with an ocean view.

The crashing waves under the house at high tide brought a welcome rhythm to my life. There was a rickety stairway down to a very rocky beach. My dog, Tako, never missed an opportunity to run on the beach. I loved the place.

I invited my mother out for a visit. I wanted her to see what I’d accomplished, considering what I had put her through with Ciao! Manhattan and all my other shenanigans years before. I took her to a Stevie session at the Record Plant, which was a first for her. I hadn’t realized until then that she didn’t understand what I did to make a living. I remember her sitting peacefully on the deck at my house in a chaise lounge, facing the Pacific Ocean. It is an unforgettable memory. Looking back at all my adventures and misadventures, she’s the one who stood beside me through thick and thin. She was the eternal optimist. But I realize now that without their love and faith in me, I wouldn’t be here telling my story.

By now, I had started to make a good living. I bought a used 1967 Jaguar E-type roadster convertible. Driving it made me feel like I was flying a World War II fighter plane. It smelled of worn leather and hot motor oil. I loved driving it home from our all-nighters at the Record Plant with dawn breaking, the top down, doing a hundred miles per hour on the empty freeway, a joint clenched in my teeth and Tako in the right-hand seat, ears blowing back in the wind. And my sex life? I was handsome, fit, and horny, I lived in a house right on the beach in Malibu, and I drove a fast car. I was living the Hollywood dream.

But being alone in a two-bedroom house on the beach had its downsides. Keeping it clean, cooking, doing laundry and shopping, and taking care of Tako during my frequent trips to New York had me looking, once again, for a majordomo to take charge of it all.

Studio One was the most popular gay disco and bar in Hollywood, and I was somewhat of a regular. The club was like the West Coast version of New York’s Studio 54. It was loud, sweaty, and erotic. I could stop by after a session on my way home, an hour or so before closing, and usually find some company for the night. It was on one of those late nights that I met Creed. He was a young, intelligent, handsome, and swarthy Eastern European with a mustache and long straight black hair pulled back into a ponytail. He always wore a bandana and looked like a gypsy. He was a real beauty.

A few weeks after Creed and I got to know each other, I offered him a gig taking care of the house in exchange for free rent. He moved into the small downstairs guest room.

My stay at the beach house lasted until the winter rains came and brought the rocks and mud tumbling down from Big Rock, with some rolling across the Pacific Coast Highway and into my front yard. (Tragically, that house and many others along PCH were destroyed in the Palisades fire 2025.) The other issue was the bumper-to-bumper summer beach traffic, with its noise and exhaust fumes, which made my commute to the studio a trial. Creed eventually found a new love and decided to move to New York. And so, in the winter of 1973, I knew it was time to move on.

Many of my music industry colleagues lived in Topanga Canyon. It was inland, halfway between the San Fernando Valley and the Pacific Coast Highway—a faster run to Hollywood—and a hippie’s paradise. I drove around to check out the little Topanga town center and then cruised along the Old Topanga Canyon Road. I saw a ‘For Rent’ sign posted on the side of the road, so I pulled into a small parking lot. On the right was a barn and corral with some horses in it. I found myself standing at the base of a very rutted and steep mountain path, so steep that unless you had a four-wheel drive jeep or a horse, you had to walk. I could see a house through canopy of overgrown trees. Clinging to the hill was a wood-frame house with a glass wall and a wooden deck facing the canyon. I trudged up the hill and walked around back.

‘Hello,’ I remember calling out, ‘anybody home?’ I heard a voice from way further up the hill, which looked to be coming from another house, with only a narrow footpath leading up to it. I went further up the path and met Mimi and Henry Smith, the owners.

They called the biggest house that I saw from the street the A house. The entire property consisted of separate little houses, all built on different platforms clinging to the side of the steep hill, connected by narrow walkways. Each platform was at a different level and a different size hey were all built mostly from recycled lumber. The A house had a living room and kitchen with big sliding glass doors that opened onto a deck that overlooked the entire canyon. Another had a bathhouse with a Victorian tub and big windows, and an outhouse with an old tank-type toilet; another one was just basically a bedroom. The handrails were made from tree limbs, and the doors and windows came from old homes that had been demolished. They all looked weather worn. Mimi and Henry had built the whole thing by hand. It was beautiful, but it ignored building permits, code, or zoning regulations. It was true folk art and a rustic paradise.

The A house became my home. It consisted of three attached but separate structures. The central part was a big chalet with a glass wall and a deck overlooking the canyon. The opposite wall had a freestanding, big brick fireplace, and behind it was a kitchen and dining room with a clerestory window looking out into the backyard. There was a hitching post just under the window. To the side, a smaller attached bedroom, a peaked ceiling, and a stone fireplace were more than enough for my needs. I was enthusiastic about the whole thing. We made our financial arrangements, and, with Creed’s help, I moved in.

Henry was a Native American and the chief of the local Chumash Indian band. Henry’s house, called the B house, was also the tribe’s headquarters. The Chumash lived up and down the coastline, from Malibu to Pasa Robles. Mimi was white and had flaming red hair. There were always Native folk coming and going, and sometimes Mimi, a registered nurse, would help them with their medical needs. Henry was also a big supplier of ‘medicinal’ marijuana, which still wasn’t legal, and supplied most of the folks in Topanga, me included.

Shortly after I moved in, Creed started planning his move to New York. He brought his friend Tony Randall to the ranch to take his place and help me get settled in. Tony was an art student at Cal State Northridge and worked nights at the coat check in Studio One to pay his tuition. We more than hit it off and ended up living together from the beginning of 1973 until 1976. It wasn’t platonic, and we remain friends to this day.

I soon realized that the A house served as cover and camouflage for the B house. Mimi and Henry could tell if anybody, like the law, was trekking up the hill, giving them time to hide any of the more recreational medicines that might be on hand. I recognized the discrimination that the Native Americans suffered at the hands of white people. With all my liberal high-school education, my exposure to human rights throughout the 50s and 60s and through working with Stevie, and the universal social justice issues we faced, I rapidly connected with them and became a part of it.

Then there were the horses. Henry had four. He and Mimi used to ride and used them occasionally to bring building supplies up the hill. The horses lived in the corral at the foot of the property, just off Old Topanga Canyon Road. I connected with a big, older mustang gelding named Iron General. He was a gentle grey giant with a very wide neck. He looked like he was out of a storybook. I learned to ride him with a bosal, which was basically a loop around his muzzle and one lead in my hand, and never with a bit or a harness. He was a forgiving and mellow old boy. Sometimes I rode with a saddle, but most of the time with just a pad. Henry was a good teacher. Tony was a good rider too, and he took on Natasha, Henry’s smaller Arab mare. I looked after the financial responsibilities for the General and Natasha and took care of their food, the farrier, and the regular vet bills.

We settled into a routine. Tony would make dinner for us every night, then I would run off to the Record Plant to record with Stevie. Tony would head to Studio One for work by 8pm, return home by 3am, then get up at 7am for his studies at Cal State Northridge. Sometimes, we would bring the horses up the hill and tie them to the hitching post in front of the dining room window, and they would stare at us as we ate breakfast. The ranch abutted all the mountain fire trails, so we would ride off with Tako in the lead. We could ride forever. Somehow, we always had time to ride our horses. I had become a Jewish cowboy. Often, I’d arrive at the studio to work with Stevie and Malcolm smelling like hay and horses.

Chapter 12

The magic we created

00:00 - 00:1

[MUSIC CUE: Stevie Wonder “Living for the City”]

Music Of My Mind was the first and most self-contained of the records Stevie, Malcolm, and I made together. Aside from some splendid guitar work on ‘Super Woman’ by Howard ‘Buzzy’ Feiten (formerly of the Paul Butterfield Blues Band and The Rascals), Stevie played all the instruments. You never knew where a live instrument would end and an electronic instrument would begin. There were no rules or regulations. We would put in whatever Stevie felt was right for the song.

‘Man,’ Stevie kept saying, ‘this is how my music sounds in my mind.’ That’s how the album got its title. It just tumbled out.

Music Of My Mind was his first real adventure with synthesizers, and it kicked off what would become known as his classic period. He’d already come a long way from the Motown sound of his teen years. He was exploring themes of love, loss, and social justice, and his emerging maturity was obvious to us.

When Stevie wanted something, he would explain what he heard in his head, and we would attempt to create it as closely as possible. He would say something like, ‘Hey, man, give me that John Lennon slap echo thing.’ We knew exactly what he meant, and we had the technical skillset to do it.

Everything was happening so quickly that Stevie often couldn’t remember all the words to the songs. Some of the lyrics were even written in the moment, so Malcolm would read them back to him through his headphones, prompting him line by line. I swear that if you listen really carefully, every now and then, because of the headphone leakage, you can hear Malcolm’s clipped British accent feeding Stevie the lyrics a bar or two before he sings them.

Stevie was very receptive to our feedback. We had a live mic in the control room, so he could always hear us in his headphones. We never turned that mic off. It was like the glass between the control room and the studio disappeared. We were always connected.

Malcolm’s observations of our time with Stevie were loving, astute, and insightful. In a 2019 interview for the website okayplayer.com, he told the writer Chris Williams, ‘The thing about Stevie was that he had such an incredible range of emotion in his voice. He could go from a real whisper to a roaring shout and holler . . . [he could take] one syllable and play three hundred notes to it and twist it around. He was also capable of doing really simple things, and we explored a lot, like in the lead on “Keep On Running,” where he was doing half-spoken, half-sung, almost like the church thing where members from the congregation sing or yell out a line while the pastor is preaching. It’s a common thing in the Black Baptist church. It’s a gospel thing, and it’s spontaneous, fun, and done with a lot of energy.’

When it came to putting the album together, we stepped back and took a look at what we already had in the can. I remember the three of us sitting on the green shag rug in Johanan Vigoda’s very grungy office on Broadway, keeping company with his dead potted palm plants. We went down the list and played every song, listening for the tracks that would become Music Of My Mind. We had probably fifty to pick from. The record was never conceived as an album from the start. It was just Stevie pouring out all these songs out of his head, and they were all in different states of completion.

The beautiful difficulty we had with Stevie was pinning down which songs were going to be on the album. We’d play stuff back, song after song after song, again and again, and Stevie kept saying that almost every song had to go on the album. We ended up with more than enough for a double album. We had our little book of songs, some finished ready to mix, others not. We made list after list, putting songs together in different order, and finally nailed down which ones would make the cut.

When the time came to put the album out, we needed to find someone to create the cover artwork. Danny Blumenau, the night manager at Jimi Hendrix’s new Electric Lady Studios, was also an artist. He used the quiet time at night to create some of his designs on the studio’s bathroom walls using a technique called decoupage, where paper cutouts are glued onto surfaces in combination with special paint effects like gold leaf and other decorative elements. I think Stevie loved Danny’s art because he could feel the outlines of the cutouts on the walls. Danny combined a portrait of Stevie with other graphics, reflecting a whole different world in the sunglasses Stevie wore.

Music Of My Mind was released March 3, 1972, and peaked at number 21 on Billboard’s pop chart and number 6 on the R&B chart. Though it wasn’t a huge commercial success, it began a new stage in Stevie’s artistic development, proving that he had staying power and that he was capable of much more than what he had achieved during his prodigious youth.

Our use of the synthesizer long before it became a staple instrument in popular music opened Stevie’s mind to all kinds of exciting sonic possibilities that added a new dimension to his music while preserving its inherent warmth and emotion. With Music Of My Mind, we had created a new, fully integrated signature sound, style, and personal expression that would forever change the face of R&B and popular music.

Unfortunately, it didn’t stay that way. Once Music of My Mind had opened the door and shown that Stevie’s creativity knew no bounds, we slowly became surrounded by the self-important big shots and hangers-on that go hand in hand with fame and fortune in the music business. I often wonder what would have happened if we had been left alone in our creative bubble for a few more years. However, the journey was far from over, and there was much more incredible music to make and places to go together.

In June, with Music Of My Mind climbing up the charts, Stevie was invited to open for The Rolling Stones on the very debauched American leg of their Exile On Main St. tour—a tour still remembered for hard drugs, bombings, and arrests. Opening for the Stones, as rough as it was, provided Stevie with very important access to rock audiences, which were new to him.

Jeff Beck was in New York that summer, having just wrapped up his East Coast tour. Malcolm already knew him, as they had played together at Ronnie Scott’s. Jeff’s management reached out to us, and we arranged to produce and record with Jeff and his band at Electric Lady a few days before Stevie got back from the Stones tour at the end of July. Jeff’s band on that tour included Cozy Powell on drums, Max Middleton on keyboards, and Clive Chaman on bass.

On the day Stevie returned to Electric Lady, we were working with Beck in the studio. We introduced them to one another. Stevie asked Jeff to play guitar on his song ‘Looking For Another Pure Love.’ In return, Jeff asked Stevie if he could have ‘Maybe Your Baby’ for his new album.

‘No, you can’t use it,’ Stevie kept saying. ‘No, I’m going to put it out, no, you can’t have it.’ Jeff kept on and on. Finally, Stevie relented. ‘Okay, I’ll give you a new song I’m working on.’ That song was ‘Superstition.’

When we finished tracking ‘Superstition,’ Stevie realized what he had created, and after intense lobbying from Motown’s executives, he wisely decided to release ‘Superstition’ himself. He gave Beck another track, ‘Because We’ve Ended As Lovers,’ which he had written with his ex-wife, Syreeta Wright. That song ended up on Beck’s 1974 album Blow By Blow. It was an emotional song and a highlight of the record, and it helped solidify Beck’s reputation as an elite guitarist. (After was a hit for Stevie, Beck released his own version of ‘Superstition’ in March 1973 with his power trio Beck, Bogart & Appice.)

‘Superstition’ reestablished Stevie as a chart-topping artist and proved that he had found his voice and was truly capable of captaining his creative ship. He told Malcolm and me that he had first imagined the song in a flash while on tour with the Stones. ‘I just started singing the melody,’ he said. ‘Probably the first thing, the only thing I can remember keeping, was the line Wash your face and hands. I think that was from when I was really little boy. I remember hearing this song saying, Get out of that bed, wash your face and hands, from the song “Shake, Rattle & Roll.”’

I remember Stevie being at the mic, writing and singing at the same time, working out the song. Malcolm and I were running around the studio with a pencil and paper, asking everybody about things that they considered superstitious, like walking under a ladder or looking in a mirror with a crack in the glass, which supposedly brought seven years of bad luck. He was saying that things appear in a certain way only because we don’t understand them, and that you should try to understand yourself, rather than just blindly believing them. In his words, as he put it to Rolling Stone in 1988, ‘The lyrics came from this sense that people believe in things that they don’t understand, and they suffer. If you’re feeling low and you come to a conclusion based on whatever someone else says, it gets hairy. Basically, I was just saying superstition ain’t the way.’

Usually, Stevie would play keyboards and sing a guide track simultaneously, then build the rest of the song around that. For ‘Superstition,’ he walked into the studio, sat down at the drums, put on his headphones, and just started playing, without relying on a click track or anything else to play against. He played the entire drum track for over five minutes, not singing at all, with nothing else to guide him.

11:10 - 1

[MUSIC CUE - Stevie Wonder “Superstition”]

He heard the entire finished song in his head. It was astounding. His performances were played freely and had very subtle tempo changes. He knew exactly where it was going. It was one of the things that made it all so magical.

The defining instrument on ‘Superstition’ is the Hohner clavinet. We tracked it in three different passes, and rather than taking a direct output from the clavinet and sending it straight into the console, we experimented with the sound. We hooked it up to all kinds of funny guitar boxes, like a Mu-Tron Bi-Phaser, a wah-wah pedal, and various distortion, delay, and echo boxes. I think was a first. These were sounds that reminded people of other sounds; they had a unique color and brought a new light to the record.

The clavinet itself was unusual as it had a pitch-bending bar, much like the one you would find on an electric guitar. No one else, before or since, plays the clavinet like Stevie Wonder. What we created was a sound that people had never heard before. Malcolm liked to joke with Stevie and say he was a prejudiced pianist because he played the black keys far more often than he played the white keys. he majority of the songs we recorded were in keys that used a lot of black keys, like E flat minor, D flat, or G flat. I think the reason he liked the black keys was because of the way he played. He navigated his way around the keyboard by running his thumbs along the front of the white keys. That also allowed him to play with flat-handed technique, almost patting on the keys, which is how he got that percussive, funky sound. Nowhere is that sound and technique more evident than on ‘Superstition’ (which is in E flat minor).

Once the immediately recognizable riff was on tape, Stevie said, ‘Let’s get a funky bass sound.’ We did that with TONTO’s Moog modules. By now, Stevie was well-versed in using TONTO to create unique bass lines. Trevor Lawrence added tenor sax, and Steve Madaio laid down some trumpet. The rest was TONTO. We mixed the song at the Record Plant in LA.

‘Superstition’ was only one of the many memorable songs on Talking Book. ‘Blame It On The Sun’ was another. It was a song about heartbreak. I don’t remember exactly how we pulled it all together, but it followed the more traditional routine we had established. The lyrics were very heavy. You must blame yourself, Stevie sings, not others, for love lost. It was all small instruments, not a lot of layers. If you listen to any of these records, especially this one, you will hear that the arrangement tend to be very spare. TONTO was a monophonic instrument and could only execute two, three, or four voices at a time, depending on how many keyboard controllers we were using. We were playing acoustic/electronic chamber music a quartet.

Stevie was always interested in other people’s work, and not just music. I remember Malcolm reading him excerpts from George Orwell’s 1984 and Animal Farm. One day, Stevie arrived at the studio and announced that he’d written a new song.

‘Oh no,’ Malcolm said, ‘not another love song, Stevie.’

‘o, no, listen,’ Stevie replied. He went in, sat at the piano, and started singing ‘Big Brother.’

‘Big Brother’ would become one of the most impactful and poignant songs on Talking Book, and I’m sure it was inspired by Malcolm reading 1984 to him a few days earlier. Stevie was mulling the story over in his head. The song’s narrative mirrors the book’s main theme: a society where your every move is being monitored, and if you step out of line, Big Brother steps in and your life becomes very unpleasant. Stevie was very affected by the book and saw many parallels to the world we were living in. I loved that Malcolm’s reading focused his attention on some of the social issues of the time.

Much like Martin Luther King Jr., who used his gift for oration to inspire people to rise in protest, Stevie was using his art to change the world for the better. I understood and shared his vision. I was ten years older than Stevie, and I had already had my own experiences with racism, segregation, and the fight for civil rights. I’d already seen a world in conflict, and I’d had the opportunity to learn from people who were doing something about it.

As with Music Of My Mind, we had so many songs that we had difficulty getting Stevie to choose which songs to put on the album. And, as usual, he always wanted to include everything.

‘Stevie,’ Malcolm said, ‘you know, this is an album; it’s not a talking book.’ Then he added, ‘Oh, I think you should call this album Talking Book.’

Stevie liked it, and that is how Talking Book got its name.

By now, Stevie, Malcolm, and I had grown very comfortable with one another. We were involved with almost every aspect of the album, including the cover photography. I was the one who took the pictures.

It was 5am, and the sun was just coming up. We buttoned up our night’s work, mixing the album at the Record Plant Studio B, and were all hanging around in the parking lot with our hands in our pockets. I didn’t know exactly what to expect. We already knew that the album was going to be called Talking Book, and I was aware there had been some detailed preplanning for the cover shoot on Stevie’s part. His hair had been styled the day before, all braids and beads. He looked stunning.

I had brought my camera gear to the session the night before: a Nikon F and a selection of lenses. Using the method I had perfected as an army combat photographer, I planned to keep it simple and fill the frame.

That morning, I met costume designer extraordinaire Ola Hudson for the first time. She was a rising designer to the stars and had created Stevie’s incredible African robe. Ola had a great understanding of musicians. I later learned she worked with Ringo Starr, John Lennon, The Pointer Sisters, and David Bowie. Her son Saul, who I met as a little boy that day, grew up to become one of the world’s greatest guitar players, Slash from Guns N’ Roses.

The only thing left unplanned was a location for the shoot. I suggested we go high up into the Hollywood Hills, just off Mulholland Drive. So, we headed off to catch the early morning sun. As I recollect, Ola rode along with Stevie, bringing the robe she designed, and she dressed him for the shoot once we arrived.

The cover photography and the album art looked biblical. It underlined African history, nobility, equality, and social justice. It was a statement about the fabric of our society, especially the Black experience in the South.

The whole shoot was over in around a half-hour. The pictures we got that morning speak for themselves. I showed our results to Motown’s soon-to-be president, Ewart Abner. He loved them and sent me a check for $300. Once again, I was oblivious to the profundity of what we had done that day.

The album art also contained Braille lettering of Stevie’s name and the title, embossed on the upper right side of the album cover, which was put together by the art department at Motown. The design and the typeface emphasized the Biblical tone of the story narrative. It was brilliant.

Talking Book was released on October 28, 1972, with ‘Superstition’ as the first single. It became Stevie’s first Billboard chart-topping single since 1963’s ‘Fingertips, Pt. 2.’ Five decades later, it was listed at number 12 in Rolling Stone magazine’s ‘Top 100 Greatest Songs Of All Time’. We must have done something right. Everything we touched seemed to turn to gold.

The album turned out to be Stevie’s breakthrough work, defining him as the artist he envisioned and truly wanted to be. It earned him a career-first: a spot atop the Billboard R&B Album charts. It finished the year at the number 3 spot on the Billboard album chart. ‘Superstition’ and ‘You Are The Sunshine Of My Life’ both hit number 1 on the Top 100 earned Stevie his first Grammys: ‘You Are The Sunshine Of My Life’ won Best Male Pop Vocal and ‘Superstition’ won Best Male R&B Performance and Best R&B Song. The album itself sold 1.6 million copies. We were on top of the world.

Once Talking Book was behind us, we turned our attention to recording the songs that would become Innervisions. For me, Innervisions marked the peak of our efforts with Stevie. We all pushed each other to be better. It was a healthy competition. We believed that we were building something special together. Stevie’s songs went from the tough realities of ghetto streets to the transcendent joy of spiritual acceptance, each rendered with an original, unique musical palette. The feel of the album is a little more jazz than funk, perhaps because of Malcolm’s subtle jazz influences. The result was simply an incredible uplifting sound and message.

Stevie’s writing pushed the songs in a more consciously political direction, tackling systemic racism and incarceration in ‘Living For The City’ and in ‘He’s Misstra Know-It-All,’ in which he rails on the incumbent president, Richard Nixon, who was making headlines and destroying our faith in the American presidency. We did everything we could to encourage Stevie and his sense of social justice and racial equality. Stevie’s songs were about the social condition and the moral fabric of our society, justice, politics, and the Black experience. These songs became a big part of my own social consciousness. The thing about Steve’s music is that it’s not just about heartbreak and love or our loves lost. Many of the songs were about our social condition and the fabric of our society. The profound social relevance of the lyrics to ‘Living For The City’ is a shining example.

I was a child in the 50s, and I had also suffered my share of discrimination, both as a Jew and for being gay, but it was my exposure to civil rights and humanism at Stockbridge that helped me empathize with what Stevie was standing up for. I had found my liberal, humanistic identity years before as a teenager—a worldview that Malcolm also shared. Between Stevie and me, I thought, we had all the bases covered: Black, blind, Jewish, and gay. I felt like we belonged together.

Innervisions epitomized the maxim that ‘All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights.’ Malcolm was also a progressive, growing up in London and living in world of jazz, whose roots came directly from African American culture. Malcolm, too, was color-blind and empathetic.

Although ‘Living For The City’ was released on Innervisions, we had recorded it in our early days at Mediasound. It was one of the first R&B hits to include both a political message and the sounds of the streets—voices, buses, and traffic—mixed with the tracks we were recording in the studio. It was like a radio drama, and its effect on us was vivid and emotional. In a strange way, it was a precursor of rap and the social commentaries embedded in that genre. For me, it was and still is one of the most relevant and important recordings of my career, and it is still as important today as we still struggle with racism, white nationalism, voter suppression, LGBTQ backlash, the overturning of Roe v. Wade, affirmative action, and more.

The song’s story begins far from New York. It is about a young Black man born into a poor family in the Deep South who experiences poverty and discrimination in ‘hard time Mississippi.’ He seeks to escape the oppression in the South and heads to New York City, hoping to find a new life.

Just past the four-minute mark, the song’s sparse instrumental bed gave us the room to create a rich soundscape. The story: a young man travels to New York by bus. We hear the bus engine rev, a driver announcing that it is bound for New York, and, upon his arrival in the big city, the innocent and hopeful young man is promptly framed for a crime, arrested, convicted, and sentenced to ten years in prison.

2 - 27:50

[MUSIC CUE: Stevie Wonder “Living for the City”]

It was like an old-time radio soap opera. As a kid, I had listened to Captain Midnight, The Lone Ranger, Gangbusters, and other early radio dramas that created these very real, gritty sound pictures that always set the scene. ‘Living For The City’ was a simple, homemade version of the same thing.

‘New York City. Just like I pictured it, skyscrapers and everything’ was voiced by Calvin Hardaway, Stevie’s older brother, while Johanan Vigoda delivered the judge’s sentence, ‘Ten Years!’ The bus was actually the diesel engine of a heating oil truck making a delivery on 57th Street in front of the studio at 4am, captured by me on my Nagra portable recorder.

With Stevie being blind, this sonic picture helped us focus on his point of view, the story, and the social consciousness he brought to his music. ‘Living For The City’ is key to understanding the many intersecting beliefs upon which the greatness of our relationship with Stevie rested. It was the push and pull between the three of us, the empathy, the technical innovations, the conceptual drama, and the bond between man and machine.

According to Stevie, it was late on a rainy night when we started tracking the song. We usually started with the keys and a guide vocal. This time, we used a Fender Rhodes keyboard with a highly processed output that panned the sound very hard from side to side in time with the song’s tempo. The drums come in with the vocal at the beginning of the first verse. The synth bass line was all TONTO.

As usual, we used an Electro-Voice RE20 for the vocal recording. It is a dynamic mic, and Stevie sang two inches in front of it. This produced a proximity effect, which we loved, giving the vocals a close, dry, intimate sound. It felt like you were standing next to him, his voice unfolding in your head.

Another thing we liked about the RE20 was that Stevie could put his hand on it to check his position without creating any hand noise from touching it. It became our go-to microphone for most of Stevie’s vocals throughout our time with him.

Once the keyboards, drums, and vocals were recorded, it was TONTO time. The bass sound was first. We spent a lot of time with Stevie getting it just right, and then Malcolm and I massaged the synthesizer while we were recording. The sound was constantly evolving. With us, it was never a matter of ‘set it and forget it.’ For the TONTO tracks, we usually recorded two voices at a time, one on each keyboard, each with its own sound—woodwind or horn-like—with its own space and character. Each sound was monophonic. The only actual polyphonic instrument on the track was the Rhodes piano.

We thought Stevie’s vocal performance should feel gruff and angry, so we deliberately provoked Stevie when we tracked it. This is where my education as a singer brought me insight into getting a great vocal performance out of Stevie. When it was time to record the vocal for the last verse, we did it again and again and kept on doing stuff that would get him angry. One of the things he hated was stopping in the middle of a take, so we kept stopping in the middle of takes, saying, ‘C’mon Stevie, you can do better than that!’ His voice got hoarse. He was getting upset with us, and it showed, but we got a great track out of him.

3 - 32:08

[MUSIC CUE: Stevie Wonder “Living for the City”]

Innervisions was released on August 3, 1973, and today is considered to be one of Stevie Wonder’s finest works and one of the greatest albums ever made. In 2003, it was ranked number 23 on Rolling Stone magazine’s ‘500 Greatest Albums Of All Time.’ Just three days after it was released, however, disaster struck. Stevie had just played a concert in Greenville, South Carolina, and was on his way to Durham, North Carolina, to do a benefit show at America’s first public, community-based Black radio station, WAFR. His cousin John Wesley Harris was driving a rented Mercury Monarch sedan. Reports vary, but most say that Stevie was asleep when a stray log dislodged from a lumber truck driving in front of them, went through the front windshield, and Stevie him in the head. He was in a coma for five days and spent two weeks in the Winston-Salem hospital.

Long before the accident, while we were still in New York, Stevie, Malcolm, and I had been sitting in the lounge of Electric Lady Studio A. Stevie was sitting in old barber chair that dominated the room. With eerie foreshadowing, he made it clear to Malcolm and me that if anything ever happened to him, we were not to allow any master tapes to be released to anybody. Now, while Stevie was in the hospital, Berry Gordy summoned Malcolm and me to ask about the master tapes. We knew Stevie’s contract gave him ownership of those tapes, and we respectfully informed Gordy of Stevie’s explicit instructions that they were not to be turned over to anyone. He said that he understood, but it was clear that Motown had a lot riding on Stevie.

It was several months before Stevie made a full recovery. The accident changed him. He came out of it with the higher consciousness that comes with a near-death experience. When he returned to the studio, he told us that he was definitely in a much better spiritual place and that it had made him reexamine and reevaluate his life and future. He seemed more somber and introspective. I think that showed on his next album, Fulfillingness’ First Finale, which, as it turned out, was to be our finale with him.

Chapter 13

Our non-stop studio

While we were with Stevie, besides working on his own albums, we worked alongside him, continuing as a team, on two albums he produced, one for his ex-wife Syreeta Wright, the other for Minnie Riperton.

Syreeta Wright was a receptionist at the Motown offices in Detroit when Edward Holland of the Holland-Dozier-Holland songwriting team heard her singing and was impressed enough that he had her sing on some demos. She met Stevie in 1968, and they began dating about a year later. Their relationship was both personal and professional. Stevie encouraged her to write her own songs, and the two collaborated on his hit ‘Signed, Sealed, Delivered (I’m Yours).’ They married on September 14, 1970, and although they divorced only eighteen months later, they remained good friends and collaborators.

We worked on Syreeta’s debut solo album, Syreeta, released on June 20, 1972. As I recollect, part of the divorce settlement was that Stevie would produce an album for her. They worked amazingly well together, and she remained an important part of his team, providing lyrics, background vocals, and compositions for several years.

Yvonne Wright (no relation to Syreeta) was another important collaborator. She wrote the lyrics to ‘Girl Blue’ and ‘Evil.’ She would sit next to him at the piano while he was recording and whisper a line to him a few bars ahead as he was playing and singing. He was very tight with her.

Music Of My Mind ends with ‘Evil,’ a simple and incisive piece of social commentary that opened the door to our collective consciousness. ‘Evil,’ Stevie asks, exasperated, ‘why have you engulfed so many hearts? you destroyed so many minds?’ The lyrics ring true to this day, and musically, it has a fantastic opening, TONTO front and center with an oboe sound and a foreboding bass. The sound is simple, singular, sad, and compelling.

We first met Minnie Riperton when she came to the Fufillingness sessions to add background vocals. We were so impressed even though we were in the thick of it with our own project, Malcolm, Stevie, and I all jumped at the chance to work with her when her husband and songwriting partner, Dick Rudolf, asked us to help make her second record, Perfect Angel. I remember putting bird sounds on her song ‘Loving You,’ which of the sound effects we used with Stevie on ‘Living For The City.’

Minnie had an extraordinary voice, especially in her falsetto register. It was totally under her control, and its clarity was astounding.

‘Loving You’ became Riperton’s biggest hit and topped the Billboard Hot 100 chart on April 5, 1975. It went on to sell over a million copies. We recorded most of the album at the Record Plant LA and mixed it at Westlake Audio, Tom Hidley’s new place. We went there because it had the first API console with fader automation, and we tracked many of Minnie’s vocals in the control room with all of us on headphones.

Tom had built Westlake as a showroom to sell the latest studio gear. He used the control room in the back to demo his studio design concepts, the latest in recording consoles, and all the new gear coming to market. It wasn’t really a studio, just a control room. The store in front was a showroom, and it gave us access to all the latest outboard gear in the industry. It became our lounge during our nighttime sessions. Sometimes we would sit on the floor up front, leaning against all these new racks of gear, with its pistachio and brown shag carpeting, eating pizza or a bucket of Kentucky Fried Chicken.

The API console only remembered fader levels; the rest was done in real time, as usual. The memory was stored on the first and last tracks of the master tape, so every time we made an update, we would have to jockey back and forth, printing the update on the alternate track, which caused the fader moves to come back later and later and was overly complicated. So, Malcolm and I would make as many moves as possible and use the automation as little as possible. In the end, we were happy with the record. Once again, using the latest and greatest technology fed our creative spirit.

While we were working with Stevie on his projects, there was little room or desire to work with anyone else. There were down times, though, especially when Stevie was on the road. That’s when Malcolm and I kept busy working on albums with other artists.

We recorded The Isley Brothers’ 3+3 in 1973 while Stevie was touring and promoting Innervisions. The Isleys were a true family affair. The original Isley Brothers consisted of O’Kelly Isley Jr., Rudolph Isley, and Ronald Isley. 3+3 would be the first album to formally include younger brothers Ernie on guitar and Marvin on bass, and brother-in-law Chris Jasper on keyboards. The original trio officially became a sextet, hence the album title. It was both old-school and new-school R&B, with Ronald’s incomparable smooth lead vocals and Ernie’s hard-edged guitar leads. Chris and Marvin brought new funk/rock energy to the band, and the combination proved to be a significant artistic advance for them.

Our first meeting came when they walked in on us while we were mixing ‘Don’t You Worry ’Bout A Thing’ with Stevie. We already knew about them and figured out pretty quickly what they had in mind, which was precisely what we had on our table. As usual, we had a lockout in Studio B to ensure our setup remained intact. We would arrive early in the afternoon and prepare the room for work. Once again, we tracked the basics with quad monitoring and the players in the control room. The guys would usually arrive an hour or so after us and get started right away. There was no dawdling around in the canteen. They were always very single-minded and down to business. They came in well-prepared, having played and perfected all the songs in rehearsal or on the road.

Ronald’s lead vocals were terrific. He was at the top of his form, and we could feel his gospel roots. We used an RE20 mic and the same close, confessional approach we deployed for Stevie’s vocals.

Working with Ernie on guitars was a sonic adventure. He regaled us with tales of how, as a little boy, he played guitar with Jimi Hendrix in the family’s kitchen. Jimi was there because the senior Isleys had recruited Jimi to play in their backup band in the early 60s. We developed a very original sound for Ernie’s lead guitar parts. We recorded his amp through a Dolby 361 noise reduction unit but didn’t decode the signal. We left it raw and then compressed it with a Flickinger tube limiter. You can hear the results of our labor on the guitar leads on ‘Who’s That Lady’ and ‘Summer Breeze,’ among others on the record.

The rest of the tracking was very straightforward, with our traditional approach being tight and close with the drums recorded from the drummer’s point of view. Marvin’s bass was recorded direct to the board, as were all of Chris’s electronic synths and keys. There was improv, experimenting with percussion, and figuring out the background vocal arrangements in the studio.

O’Kelly and Rudolf, along with the rest of the guys, sang the background vocal parts. They also oversaw all of the business, financials, and logistics. O’Kelly’s one eccentricity was that he had very long fingernails, at least two or three inches long, on his left hand. Rudy ran the show both on and off the road. He made sure that everybody got to the studio on time. If any of the brothers were late, they would hear from him, and it wouldn’t be pleasant. They were a very professional, well-oiled machine.

We never connected outside of the studio. It was all strictly business. Malcolm and I had the feeling that, in their eyes, we were like the studio itself—simply tools to be used. They wanted what Malcolm and I could do to enhance their sound by bringing in new techniques and technologies to the album. We were there strictly to serve those needs, which was totally fine with us. When we finished, O’Kelly brought in a briefcase with a stack of hundred-dollar bills and paid our $12,000 fee in cash.

A year later, when Stevie was on the road, we recorded a second album with The Isley Brothers. The Heat Is On continued where 3+3 left off, with the same lineup. By now, the Isleys had found their pocket and perfected their soul/funk sound. Malcolm and I felt like we had recorded 3+3 just the day before.

The big difference was that we tracked the record at Kendun Recorders in the San Fernando Valley, on the other side of the Santa Monica mountains from Hollywood and the Record Plant. It was another Tom Hidley design, and the console was an API with custom Dean Jensen transformers. We felt right at home. We mixed the album at Tom’s Westlake Audio.

The Heat Is On was unusual because all the up-tempo funk was on side one, and the smooth ballads were on side two, which helped it set a certain mood. It first charted on June 14, 1975, and spent forty weeks on the Billboard 200. It was the Isley Brothers’ first album to hit number 1. Once again, when we were done, O’Kelly showed up with his briefcase and paid us in cash. We never saw or heard from them again.
