Chapter 14

All good things must end

00:03-00:10

[ARCHIVAL CLIP - Stevie Wonder winning Grammy]

And the winner is “You Are the Sunshine of My Life,” Stevie Wonder

The sixteenth annual Grammy Awards were held at the Hollywood Palladium in Los Angeles on March 2, 1974. Stevie took home the awards for Album Of The Year Innervisions, Best Pop Vocal Performance (‘You Are The Sunshine Of My Life’), Best R&B Vocal Performance, and Best R&B Song (both for ‘Superstition’). Malcolm and I received the award for Best Engineered Recording (Innervisions) in a non-televised award ceremony earlier that afternoon.

Curiously, each time Stevie went up to accept an award during the televised evening event, we were left out of his thank-you list. After the event, in a half-page ad in Billboard magazine thanked his Motown team, Wonderlove (his touring band), and others. Malcolm and I were listed at the bottom of the ad, just above the staff of the 5th Avenue Hotel. Malcolm found that particularly demeaning.

The lack of recognition stung. We had devoted years of our lives up to this point, and we were integral collaborators. We took Stevie’s invitation back in 1971 to become the creative directors of his company seriously, even though we never formalized it with a contract.

With these tensions mounting, it became apparent to Wonderlove member Michael Sembello that Berry Gordy and the rest of Stevie’s entourage were actively disregarding and diminishing our contributions because they wanted to convey the all-powerful image of Stevie Wonder that his community craved. In Mark Ribowsky’s biography of Stevie, Signed, Sealed, And Delivered: The Soulful Journey Of Stevie Wonder, Sembello recalls, ‘What it is, is experimentation to the extreme and under the extreme violation of certainty. You had this . . . thing called TONTO from an alien station. It’s extraterrestrial . . . and if you ask me, those two guys up there on the bridge of the mothership were really the ones who made it possible for Stevie to go to the ends of the universe.’

However, it seemed like other members of Stevie’s entourage were encouraging him to keep credit away from Margouleff and Cecil. From Motown’s perspective, this made sense—the label’s strategy was to make Stevie seem more like a solitary, all-powerful, highly capable genius. For Motown, including us in the public perception of the album interfered with that view.

No one, least of all Malcolm or I, would ever deny Stevie’s incomparable genius, but we were responsible for many of the textures and sounds that became forever associated with Stevie Wonder.

We were disappointed, embarrassed, angry, and a little broken in spirit, sitting quietly waiting for some public recognition—and more tangibly, royalty payments. Malcolm and I discussed walking away, but, hoping that things would change, we readied ourselves and the studio for the next spate of sessions, which would become Fulfillingness’ First Finale.

As usual, Malcolm and I did everything we could to encourage Stevie to write about social justice and racial equality. ‘You Haven’t Done Nothin’’ became one of his angriest and most politically aware statements. The song was about someone who talked a big game about uniting a country and then did everything he could to destroy the people he saw as his enemies. The lyrics were aimed squarely at President Richard Nixon, although they never mentioned him by name. (Nixon resigned a few days after the record’s release.)

Stevie invited The Jackson 5 to the studio to sing background vocals on the song. Malcolm and I knew nothing about the visit. They arrived together in the early evening, as I remember, and the session was very warm, low-key, and unassuming. I met Michael and his brothers in the canteen, just a few minutes before the session. I think he was around fifteen years old and struck me as a childlike, sensitive, gentle soul who showed enormous respect for Stevie. Over the following years, Michael and I occasionally ran into one another at events. He remembered my name and was always ready with a hello and a smile.

4:51 - 6:15

[MUSIC CUE: Stevie Wonder "Boogie On Reggae Woman"]

The Caribbean flavor, irresistible beat, and unforgettable melody of ‘Boogie On Reggae Woman’ still make me feel happy whenever I hear it. Stevie played almost all the instruments on this track, one after the other. The drums were recorded dry and earthy. The bass line was huge and just strutting along, twisting, pulsating, and bending as it went. It brought new meaning to the word funky. The acoustic piano was recorded in stereo from the player’s point of view—the left hand on the left and the right hand on the right. You can feel the motion as Stevie moves from low to high. We controlled the width of all the images with pan pots and added very little reverb or echo. Stevie played a traditional blues harmonica, not his usual chromatic one. I love his very long, non-traditional solo as the song fades out. The vocal was sassy and happy. Stevie sang it, as usual, two inches from the mic, with his hand touching it to keep his position, and, as usual, we used an RE20. There was only one other musician on the song: Rocky Dijon, who played conga.

We actually started work on the song long before things started to fall apart. Even though it was associated with the tensions of Fulfillingness’, it lived in one form or another in Stevie’s song archive from happier times for all of us. As we did with all the songs we recorded at the Record Plant, we monitored our recording sessions in quad, which allowed us to occupy the same space as the music, building the song a layer at a time—drums in the front and congas in the back, for example. We could hear the parts talking to each other.

‘Creepin’’ is classic Stevie. It has it all: deep and sweetly haunting, a dreamy atmosphere, a lush melody, and profoundly romantic lyrics. It was very hypnotic and peaceful. The instrumentation features a Fender Rhodes, a chromatic harmonica, drums clean and present, laid back and steady (no click track), and then TONTO with its warm, sophisticated bass line and haunting orchestration. The incredible Minnie Riperton performed the stellar background vocals on the track. It was a perfect song in every way.

The last song we worked on with Stevie was ‘They Won’t Go When I Go.’ It was the only song on Fulfillingness’ to be co-written by Stevie and Yvonne Wright. The instrumentation is stripped-down, vulnerable, and moving, with just acoustic piano, vocals, background vocals, and TONTO to carry the song. The flute and organ-like ARP sounds floating from TONTO give it a feeling of fragility, a profound sense of death, and significant loneliness. In a way, this song speaks to me as a eulogy for the TONTO years with Stevie. Just as the final synth notes ring and die out, so too did our relationship with Stevie, floundering further under the immense pressure from Motown and Stevie’s industry big-shots and hangers-on.

The straw that broke the camel’s back and led to our leaving Stevie occurred while we were recording a vocal with him in Studio B. That night, there were all these hangers-on in the studio, all talking and voicing their opinions. They were smoking weed, bringing it into the control room, and saying things like, ‘Oh, here, have a joint. Can I sit in the back?’

It finally got to a place where things were getting out of hand. The noise was interfering with our ability to communicate with Stevie. Malcolm turned around and said, in his best British accent, ‘Excuse me, but do you think you could keep it down to a dull roar?’

Stevie said into the mic, ‘Hey, man, don’t talk to my friends that way.’

I realized then how our relationship had changed. It used to be just us in the studio making music. Now, there were all these people partying, having nothing to do with the music, distracting Malcolm and me, distracting Stevie. At that moment, it seemed to me that the entourage had become more important to Stevie than we were.

Finally, Malcolm stood up from the console and said, ‘Then perhaps they can help you make your album.’ He walked out and didn’t return.

That was it. It was over. I spent the next week or two trying to make peace, but the angels had left the building.

The tensions and frustrations were not really about the music. They were about the old business still lurking in the background. After Stevie asked us to be a part of his production company back at Mediasound in 1971, we felt we should have received royalties from his records. He did not feel the same way. So, our relationship ended.

All these years later, I wonder what would have happened if we had stuck around.

Fulfillingness’ topped the Billboard 200 thanks largely to the massive commercial success of ‘Boogie On Reggae Woman.’ Though Stevie’s trajectory was still climbing, we felt the deepening distance between him and us, and I think that’s sonically evident on the record, too. Stevie was slowly moving back toward live playing, choosing to record the full Wonderlove band more and using TONTO less and less. Still, that album contains some of our best work together, and I remain proud of what we accomplished.

Looking back on our work with Stevie, there are no two ways about it: what we did was remarkable, and it will never be repeated. Something like that only happens once in your life. You just don’t know it’s happening when you’re there.

Time heals most wounds. And while the way our relationship ended stung at the time, the music lives on. We created something infinitely larger than ourselves, and I’d be foolish not to feel happy, honored, and immensely proud of what Stevie, Malcolm, and I accomplished.

Chapter 15

Life after Wonder

My professional life can be neatly divided into two parts: before Stevie and after. Before our collaboration with Stevie, Malcom and I were largely unknown, working behind the scenes, creating and adding our voices to projects mostly in anonymity. The success of our four albums with Stevie, the Grammy on our shelf, and the connections we made working in world-class studios with world-class artists, opened doors and paved the way for new projects.

One of the first things Malcolm and I did was put the finishing touches on our second Tonto’s Expanding Head Band album, which was released by Polydor in 1974. We shortened the band’s name to TONTO, reworked and remastered some of our earlier music, added a couple of new songs, and brought in some live musicians for a couple of tracks: Reggie McBride on bass, Steve Gadd and Armand Habdurian on drums and percussion, and Marlo Henderson and Michael Sembello on guitar. We titled the album It’s About Time. I can’t speak for Malcolm, but for me, my heart just wasn’t into it, and the album didn’t have anywhere near the impact that Zero Time did.

In early 1975, we got a call from Billy Preston’s manager, Bob Ellis, who told us that Billy was interested in having Malcolm and me work on his next album for A&M Records. Billy was at the peak of his career and riding high with his biggest hit, ‘Nothing From Nothing,’ so it was an easy decision to head over to Sunset Boulevard meet with him and his manager.

Bob’s office was up one flight of stairs in a two-story brick building on the south side of Sunset, just across the street from the Continental Hyatt House Hotel. The hotel, sometimes referred to as the Continental Riot House, was famous for the nonstop partying from touring rock bands staying there. It wasn’t uncommon to see TVs fly out of a window and crash into the driveway below.

Bob’s office felt warm but slightly over the top, with crown moldings, high-end furniture, and gold records covering the walls. He also managed his then-wife, Diana Ross, as well as Ronnie Wood, Rufus, and Chaka Khan, to name a few. We were impressed.

The first thing I noticed were the frogs: frog figurines on his desk and more on the tables around the office. I couldn’t help but ask about them.

‘Frogs bring good luck,’ he said.

Ever since then, I too have kept a small brass frog on my desk for good luck.

The first thing pretty much everyone noticed about Billy at the time was his enormous afro. The second thing was his gregarious and open yet humble personality. He welcomed us into the office with a big smile, revealing a big gap between his front teeth, just like mine.

We had done our homework. Billy’s contributions to The Beatles (also known as ‘The White Album’) and Let It Be had earned him the title of the Fifth Beatle, while his collaborations with Eric Clapton and Sly & The Family Stone—plus sessions with Little Richard, Sam Cooke, Ray Charles, The Everly Brothers, the Reverend James Cleveland, others—had solidified his A-list status. His playing, whether on Hammond B3 organ, Fender Rhodes, Clavinet, or piano, was instantly recognizable. His gospel roots were never far away, bringing a spiritual touch to his playing and making him completely unique. There wasn’t another musician who could claim to have been coveted by both The Beatles and The Rolling Stones at the same time, while both bands were in their prime, or to have been touted as Ray Charles’s successor by Ray Charles himself.

Billy already knew a lot about us, too, and being the keyboard master, he was interested in what we were into, especially TONTO. The meeting had not lasted more than a few minutes before Malcolm and I happily said yes to recording his next album, which would become It’s My Pleasure. It was also the start of a friendship that lasted right through to his unfortunate passing in 2006 at the age of fifty-nine.

With recording about to get underway, Malcolm and I decided to move on from the Record Plant, so we took TONTO and our gear back to Point Dume and set up our electronic music center in Malcolm’s house. Billy lived in Topanga, just off Topanga Canyon Boulevard. Tony Randall and I were basically his neighbors, and Malcolm lived only twenty short minutes away. We all had a lot in common, and it didn’t take us very long to bond.

Billy’s property was more of a ranch than a house. He had a rehearsal studio with a keyboard rig and an excellent PA system so we could review the songs that he had in mind to record. It gave us a roadmap of how to approach tracking the album.

ran ace mastering engineer wed previously worked with during mastering of Minnie Riperton’s Perfect Angel. His studio was located at the end of a dead-end street in Burbank. e had just built a second studio behind his mastering room. It was state of the art: upgraded Tom Hidley studio design with signature compression ceiling, Hidley quad monitors, highly modified API console with Dean Jensen transformers, and two sizable, isolated studio spaces. There was also an excellent grand piano. It felt like the Record Plant revisited, right down to the green-and-brown shag carpeting.

Peter Chaikin, our second engineer at the Record Plant during our days with Stevie, followed us to Kendun and picked up right where he left off. We all felt at home and got to work right away.

One of the essential elements of Billy’s rig was the Hammond B3 and its Leslie cabinet. A single Leslie cabinet has two speakers, a treble horn and a bass speaker, which rotate, creating a swirly, tremolo-type sound. Usually, B3s only one Leslie cabinet. However, Malcolm and I decided to use two and mic them in stereo, one panned to the left and the other to the right. We spent a lot of time setting up the treble horns in stereo with a pair of AKG C414 EB condenser microphones. We recorded the bass speaker on the bottom in mono with an Electro-Voice RE20. The sound was huge, spacious, and simply astounding. We put the cabinets in back studio with lively walls for lots of natural reverb and ran the connecting cables under the door to the front studio, with Billy at the keyboard and the drums facing each other.

The drums had their own multi-microphone setup, which took hours to perfect. As usual, we mixed from the drummer’s point of view. We used a pair of AKG C414s overhead, Sennheiser 421s on the toms, an RE20 on the kick, a Neumann KM 84 on the hi-hat, and two Shure SM57s top and bottom on the snare. e added a pair of Sony C37s. That was pretty much the standard approach for Malcolm and me.

We used a pair of AKG C414 EBs on the acoustic piano and recorded it as an overdub to take advantage of the studio’s acoustics. We placed the mics at the bottom and top of the keyboard so that the sonic image was left-to-right, reflecting Billy’s point of view. You could hear the motion of the notes as his hands moved up and down the keyboard. For us, the movement itself was musical. Billy’s electronic keyboard stack included a Fender Rhodes, a Clavinet, and a few other keyboards, all set up in the control room. The bass and guitars were set up with direct inputs in the control room. Everybody had good eye contact.

For our first session at Kendun, Billy pulled into the parking lot in the car he drove every day. It was a showstopper: an early 60s Bentley sedan with right-hand drive, bone white with a Chrysler V8 under the hood. The car was seriously hot-rodded, and it was rumored that George Harrison had previously owned it. Everybody at the studio went outside for a gawk.

A few days later, Malcolm and I got to ride in it. The car was overpowered and totally out of balance because of the weight of the retrofitted engine on its front end. Its shock absorbers were nonexistent, and the whitewall tires were the wrong size. It lunged and floated over the road. The brakes were pretty much nonexistent, too. I only rode in it once or twice, and I felt like I was taking my life into my hands each time. But although it was terrifying to ride in, it really a beautiful old jalopy. Billy loved it.

Billy didn’t have a touring band, so we got on the phones and reached out to all the great players we’d worked with in the past. It’s My Pleasure became a star-studded ensemble of musicians, and the results brought new meaning to the word funky. The group we assembled had all worked with Malcolm and me, or each other, on various productions. Everybody knew everybody. It immediately felt like a working band that had been together for years. We were family.

Stevie Wonder played harmonica on two tracks. George Harrison (under the pseudonym Hari Georgeson) played guitar on two tracks. Rufus, Tony Maiden, and Shuggie Otis added guitars. The great Ollie Brown played drums. Rocky Dijon joined in on percussion. Reggie McBride and Kenny Burke played bass. The incredible Waters family—Lorna, Maxine, Julia, Oren, and Luther—performed all the background vocals, harmonizing and arranging the parts with Billy on the fly. Syreeta Wright sang on one song, ‘Fancy Lady.’ (After our time with Billy, Syreeta would collaborate again with him on the classic duet ‘With You, I’m Born Again.’) The sessions with Stevie and ‘Hari’ happened at night, with just Billy, Malcolm, and me.

There were no rehearsals with the other players, no sheet music. Billy had all the songs in his head, and he taught each player their parts from scratch. Sometimes a chord chart would be scrawled during the session, but mostly everybody just brought their ideas to the table, and it came together. Once the changes and the rhythms were locked down, Billy added his distinctive mixture of different harmonic ideas, using Clavinet, piano, organ, bass and synth textures that defined the music.

You could spot his playing from a mile away. He would be standing behind his stack of keyboards in the middle of the control room with his omnipresent gap-toothed grin, showing off his dazzling virtuosity and ability to combine soul and rock while never leaving his blues and gospel roots behind.

As usual, Malcolm and I always kept an open mic between the studio and the control room so that the wall between the two disappeared. We monitored the sessions in quad, as was our custom. It was loud, and we all lived inside the music in an immersive sonic bubble.

When the time came to bring in TONTO, we took the twenty-four-track master tapes under our arms, and Billy and I drove (he in his Bentley, me in my own car) to Malcolm’s house. There were two broad steps down into a sunken living room, which was rectangular and had an enormous, rough, floor-to-ceiling stone wall with a fireplace at one end, where we placed our Hidley monitors at ear level. On the wall across from the stairs, facing the monitors, was TONTO. We used a Tascam twenty-four-channel console to control the monitoring setup between the speakers and the synthesizer. Our tape machines were on the opposite wall. It was an ideal size, and it sounded great.

Elsewhere at Malcolm’s, the garage was his labworkshop, and the former dining room was our production/business office. It was a home studio in the truest sense of the word. His wife, Poli, kept us and the place organized, and, in keeping with the English tradition, she kept the teapot hot and the food flowing.

We cranked up TONTO, and Billy jumped in with both feet. The synth sounds became a key part of the sonic fabric of It’s My Pleasure. We made many trips back and forth between Malcolm’s and Kendun. The final mixing happened in real time, without automation, and Kent mastered the final product. There were lots of laughs and good hearts, and I think the results speak for themselves.

I was definitely in a time of transition when we made It’s My Pleasure. I had started making house calls to various studios to let people know that I was available. One of them was Richard Kaplan, who owned a studio in Malibu called Indigo Ranch. He asked me to come there to track and mix with Michael Pinder, the keyboard player in The Moody Blues and part-owner of the studio. He was recording with a Mellotron, an early version of a sampler that utilized pre-recorded tape loops of string orchestras, horns, and other sounds. (An excellent example of one can be heard in the Moody Blues song ‘Nights In White Satin.’)

The Mellotron was a cranky electro-mechanical beast, hard to use and record. Fortunately for me, during our Mediasound days, Malcolm had spent time repairing Felix Pappalardi’s Mellotron. It sat in Malcolm’s living room for a few months, so I had the chance to mess around with it, which meant I wasn’t intimidated about dealing with Pinder’s.

Around this time, I had begun working with a new assistant, Howard Siegel, having been introduced to him by Tony one night at Studio One. I was attracted to him instantly, and not surprisingly, considering where and how we met, our relationship started out very physical. And though our physical attraction for one another soon waned, our friendship evolved into an intellectual, spiritual, and soulful bond. Howard was knowledgeable, persistent, and curious. There were no barriers or secrets between us. It was truly a meeting of like minds.

The sessions at Indigo were my first with Howard at my side. Driving home, he’d pepper me with technical questions. He was relentless. I knew then that he was the right guy for the road ahead.

Indigo was ten miles north of Topanga. You’d head up the Pacific Coast Highway and then turn right onto Carbon Canyon Road, which thankfully was paved, and wind upward for at least fifteen hundred feet. Another sharp turn put you at the studio’s driveway. From there, things got even trickier. The studio was tucked away in Solstice Canyon, at the end of a treacherous one-lane dirt road with hairpin turns and sheer drop-offs. It could be terrifying, especially at night. Then, finally, the road dead-ended at the studio door.

The property was gorgeous. Avocado and citrus orchards gave way to hiking trails that wound down into the canyon at the southern end of the property. The studio was surrounded by craggy rock formations and overlooked the flat blue expanse of the Pacific Ocean. The canyon was named for the winter and summer solstice ceremonies performed there for hundreds of years by the local Chumash Indians.

On the outside, the studio looked like a rustic ranch house. On the inside, it was a recording studio with all the latest gear: a custom-built thirty-channel Aengus/API mixing console, highly modified by the respected and eccentric audio guru Deane Jensen. The console was his baby, hot-rodded with his stellar audio transformers. Its sonic clarity and transient response were outstanding. It was a perfect balance of technology and tranquility.

After those initial sessions with Pinder, I fell in love with Indigo Ranch. Unlike the Record Plant, where I remained ensconced, Indigo was quiet and isolated. It felt separated from the rest of the world.

Thanks to my track record with Stevie, I was now a hot commodity, warmly welcomed in all the A&R offices in town. I took on a few indie bands and recorded demos for them to offer to the labels. Studio time at Indigo was charged loosely by the day (unlike at the Plant, which was hourly) so I could stay focused and productive on a very tight budget.

Indigo had a guest house attached to the studio with a sleeping loft and a few bedrooms, so Howard, Tako, and I would stick around for a few days at a time. Michael Hoffman, Richard’s partner, kept the fridge full while we worked. It was all about working, eating, and sleeping—a time for concentration and solitude.

Living in Topanga Canyon, so close to Billy, led us to naturally becoming friends. He was always warm, fun-loving, and hospitable; his friends constantly came and went. He loved his ranch, his horses, and cowboy hats. We both blended effortlessly into the Topanga scene with all the other latter-day hippies and long-time eccentrics that made up the very free and easy community. Like many other musicians and creatives, nonconformists, and eccentrics living in that very laidback place, we were weary from the rigors of work in Hollywood, constant touring, and other demands on our time and energy.

Billy’s ranch sat at the end of Colina Drive. It had hedges and a high fence with an electric gate. It was secluded and rustic, not the kind of place that you would stumble across unless you were looking for it. The main house sat on a hill. The dining room and the chef’s kitchen next to it had sliding glass doors that opened onto a wooden deck overlooking the grounds. The living room was a two-story knotty pine-paneled space that went the width of the house. It turned out to be a great room to record drums. A set of stairs at one end led to a second-floor balcony that opened into the bedrooms.

Next door to the main house was a rehearsal studio (formerly a horse barn), a garage, and a new shelter with a corral Billy had built for his horses. Finally, there was a small one-car garage, which was where Billy’s Bentley lived. The place was big and sprawling. We spent time hanging out there together and occasionally rode our horses on the Topanga fire trails, me on Iron General, Tony on Natasha, and Billy on one of his. When it came time for Natasha to give birth to the foal she was pregnant with when we took her on, we set her up in Billy’s barn. She delivered a beautiful little filly whom Billy named Fancy Lady.

About a year after completing It’s My Pleasure, Billy asked me to co-produce his next album for A&M. I said yes without a moment’s hesitation. In the interim, Malcolm and I had begun to drift apart, working together sometimes but other times apart. With Howard officially installed as my right-hand man, I took on Billy’s album without Malcolm, and it became my first major solo producer’s gig.

Perhaps because we were totally wrapped up in Topanga Canyon culture, or maybe because we were tired of the Hollywood studio scene, Billy and I decided to forgo the craziness of the LA music industry and record the album at his house. I remember standing with our elbows on the railing of the deck at the front of his house, sharing a joint. (Even now, the aroma of the pot smoke mixed with the smell of horses wafting in the air is a lovely memory.)

Off in the distance, we could hear the Record Plant remote recording truck, in low gear, laboring up Colina Drive. The truck pulled up to the ranch, Billy opened the gate, and we were underway.

The truck was a thirty-five-foot-long converted box truck. It pulled up and backed onto the parking pad next to the kitchen. There was a slight hill, so we jacked up the front wheels about a foot to level it out. Standing in front of it, you had to look up to see the top of the radiator. It looked massive. We hooked up the power from the main fuse box. The truck was our control room, and Billy’s house was transformed into our recording studio. We dragged all the microphone cables through the kitchen and into the living room and set up a pair of JBL 4320 monitors and a TV camera so we could see everybody from the truck.

Billy had a white Yamaha grand piano in the living room, so we built his electric keyboard stack next to it. We set up the drums on the opposite side, where the living room and dining room joined. The B3 sat in the dining room with its two Leslie cabinets. We overdubbed the acoustic piano and the B3 and tracked the basics with the electric instruments. The guitars and bass were recorded directly to the board, with the players sitting next to Billy. We had an open mic between the truck and the living room. When we started overdubbing, we recorded the guitars (with amps), the piano, and the organ. I was running back and forth between the truck and the house, adjusting mic placements and fine-tuning setups. The rehearsal studio became a combination writing room and hangout space for the players. It was soundproofed and very comfortable. The whole thing was perfect.

In the mornings, it was just the three of us: Billy, Howard, and me. Sometimes I rode Iron General over to the sessions. I remember one day, arriving to find Billy standing on the balcony outside his bedroom, sleepy-eyed, dressed in a dark red, hooded, terrycloth bathrobe. He looked like an ancient monk. Sitting at the B3 in his robe with a coffee mug at his side, he was a sight to behold.

There were some old faces and some new faces in the band, but once again, they were all-star session players and friends of ours. Steve Beckmeier played guitars, Ollie Brown and Alvin Taylor shared drum duties, Kenny Burke and George Johnson played bass, and Tony Maiden added rhythm guitar. Malcolm was part of the recording, too. Although we were no longer partners, we recorded him playing TONTO at his place on Point Dume. The only superstars on the record were the Tower Of Power horn section and Jeff Beck, who played lead guitar on ‘Bad Case Of Ego.’ It was all very relaxed, laid-back, and idyllic. The guys would show up, play their parts, sit on the lawn in the sun, play with the horses, lounge, and jam in the rehearsal studio. The final vocals and mixing happened over in Malibu at Indigo Ranch.

The album Billy Preston (sometimes called Do What You Want) was released in November 1976 and featured two songs, ‘I’ve Got The Spirit’ and ‘Girl,’ that charted on Billboard’s soul chart. Overall, however, it wasn’t as successful as his previous releases.

While we were cutting those songs, we recorded another full album’s worth of pure gospel material. Billy was tapping into his gospel roots, which was inspiring to us all. omehow, that album was never released, I suspect because it didn’t fit with A&M’s expectations. No doubt they wanted another ‘Nothin’ From Nothin’’ or ‘Outa-Space.’ Gospel was not what they had in mind, so I fear they buried it. What became of those recordings, to this day, I don’t know.

Billy’s sexual orientation was known and accepted by his close friends in the music world but not by his fans or the public. Sadly, he struggled throughout his life with being gay. It conflicted with his public image, his deeply held religious beliefs, and his lifelong association with the church. Billy was raised in the African American gospel tradition and was a committed Christian throughout his life. His gospel roots lived in his music. Sadly, the church did not look favorably on homosexuality, and Billy faced being locked in the closet forever. In the end, I think that suppressing himself was part of what drove his well-documented and tragic drug addiction. He did not publicly come out as gay until just before he passed away in 2006.

At his secluded ranch in Topanga, however, Billy could be who he really was. Living in the same community, he, Howard, and I felt accepted and safe. There was a sense of brotherhood, belonging, and relief. It was our sanctuary. That played a big role in our decision to bring the Record Plant remote truck to Billy’s ranch instead of going into Hollywood. The added benefits of no commute time, traffic jams, or studio hangers-on were a welcome bonus.

The poet Charles Bukowski’s poem ‘Bluebird’ reminds me of those days with Billy. It begins, ‘There’s a bluebird in my heart that / wants to get out / but I’m too tough for him, / I say, stay in there, I’m not going / to let anybody see / you.’

Working with Billy at his house in Topanga, we let his bluebird take flight. Looking back, it was the same for Howard and me. Rest in peace, Billy, my dear friend.

A couple of weeks after we wrapped up the record, Billy left to tour with The Rolling Stones, and Howard and I moved on to other projects. Malcolm and I continued to drift apart. We each had our own projects. Sometimes, as with Billy’s album, we’d come back together and do some work with TONTO, but more and more it became clear that we were moving in different directions.

For me, the moment of truth came when Billy asked if we wanted to play TONTO live with him on the television show The Midnight Special. The show was to air on May 2, 1975. Billy was hosting, and it was a huge deal. Malcolm, being the performer he was, having spent years playing the finest jazz clubs, was over the moon about it. I was reluctant, because I never really saw myself as a live performer. We said, ‘yes,’ and got to work programming TONTO for the show. Being all analog and connected with patch cords, TONTO made our task intricate, sensitive, and time-consuming.

We brought it to the stage all pre-patched and ready to go—or so we thought. The setup went well until they turned on the stage lights. The temperature went up ten degrees, and TONTO went completely bonkers. So did I. For me, it was a trial by fire, and we got burned. You would have thought we’d learned our lesson at the Trinity Church concert.

Fortunately for us, we also had TONTO’s parts recorded on a trusty sixteen-track tape as a backup, and we got through the show using the tape machine, with Billy playing one or two lines over the track. But the Midnight Special debacle sealed the deal for me. I realized, once again, that I was at my best being the man behind the curtain. Playing live in front of an audience was not for me.

Malcolm and I had very different views about where to go next. I wanted to continue recording and producing records. Malcolm wanted to follow his dream of live performance with TONTO. He wanted to buy an industrial building we found in Venice, two blocks from the beach, and create a recording studio and a home for TONTO. I just felt that we would have to go too deep into debt to do it. There never any bad vibes about our parting ways. In the end, I simply gave Malcolm my share of TONTO and all our recording gear and walked forward, becoming an independent recording engineer and producer.

Poli, who always called TONTO and me ‘Malcolm’s other wives,’ was happy about the split. At the time, I didn’t feel too kindly toward her either, so I was too.

Over the years, there was talk of a reunion with TONTO, but it never materialized. Eventually, Malcolm and his family moved back to the East Coast, and the prospect of us working together again faded away. I will always regret that lost opportunity.

Malcolm went on to have an incredible career and continued to influence the synthesizer world till his passing in 2021 at the age of eighty-four. I think a lot about him these days. He will always live on in my memory. May all the Gods bless him. Rest in peace, my dear friend.

Chapter 16

My journeyman years

By the end of 1976, I was keeping busy working at various studios in and around Los Angeles. Indigo Ranch, Crystal Sound, Sound City, and Amigo were all on my résumé, but I stayed closest to the Record Plant. The place may have become infamous, but it held a special place in my heart. So, when Chris and Gary offered me the job of ‘Chief Consulting Engineer,’ I couldn’t refuse.

My new position gave me two different duties. The first was to reorganize the internal operations and upgrade the studios. With my track record—and a Grammy—I acted as a security blanket and attended to the needs of famous clients. I became a sort of sonic maître d’.

The other part was psychological and political. Gary and Chris were increasingly at odds with one another. Gary’s partying was becoming all-consuming, and he was sinking deeper into drug addiction. His behavior was more and more unpredictable. Chris, on the other hand, was very predictable. He was still strait-laced, never did anything to excess, and was always the consummate businessman. However, he didn’t know anything about the technical operations of the studios themselves. That was Gary’s wheelhouse. So, I acted as a buffer between them.

I moved out of Topanga and into town to be closer to the studio. I rented a two-bedroom house with Howard on Davis Way in Laurel Canyon that included a guest house, which I turned into an office. The house was at the top of Lookout Mountain, halfway up the Canyon, and had a spectacular south-facing view that stretched from Downtown LA to Catalina. At night, you could see the running lights from the planes all lined up on their descent to LAX. It was the Hollywood dream.

I leased a silver-and-black 1976 Cadillac Seville from Executive Car Leasing, which was where the Plant leased all of its cars and trucks, so there were no questions asked or deposits needed, and they delivered the car right to me at the studio. I got a custom license plate that said WHANK, which put me right up there with Gary and Chris and their plates, GREED and DEDUCT. We all laughed a lot when we saw our three cars in a row in the parking lot. Truthfully, that was our attitude, and those three little words summed up our feelings about the music industry.

I missed the rural life in Topanga—the serenity, the isolation, the horses, and my friends there—when I moved back into town. But I realized I had missed the action the Record Plant and the scene in town even more. Back at the Plant, I remained visible, connected, and in the flow of the music scene. The studio was fully booked, night and day. There was also a new facility in Sausalito that needed attention. There was always a lot going on, and, with Howard at my side, we got to work.

Living back in town also reintroduced me to the feverish LA nightlife. All the music clubs in Hollywood were pumping and jumping with live bands until 2 and beyond. The Starwood was the premier place to perform, mainly because of their talent booker, the very avuncular David Knight, a former A&R man and radio disc jockey with a reputation for having the ears and eyes for talent. David had a high batting average for getting bands signed to major labels. He’s credited with discovering DEVO, and he booked their first show at the Starwood I was in the audience.

The club had a private balcony and a green room constantly awash with platters of food, drink, and other libations. It was reserved for record company executives, band members, and industry folk. David knew every manager, agent, A&R man, and lawyer in the business. They were all friends, and he always ensured they were ‘taken care of.’

Some bands got signed solely based on their performances at the Starwood. I would often stand at the side of the balcony and watch the record company folk respond, either positively or negatively, to the band onstage. I got to know many of them, and because of my Record Plant connection, if I liked what I saw, I could always offer them some studio time at a low rate, or even for free if the buzz around the band was big enough.

And then there were all the other clubs in town: the Troubadour, the Whisky, the Roxy, the Country Club, FM Station, the Central, the Coconut Teaser, and on and on. here was the Rainbow Grill’s very druggy private upstairs bar. You had to get the nod from Mario, who was at the front door, to be allowed to walk up the stairs. The clubs were hot, drunken, drugged, and loud—the epitome of sex, drugs, and rock’n’roll—but they were also the wellspring of new talent.

In 1977, I travelled to New York with Howard to record Chris DeMarco’s band, Riff Raff at my old haunt, Mediasound on 57th Street. The band consisted of Chris, Ned, David Lanik, Ray Hennigan, and Jimmie Lowell. They were signed to Chris Blackwell’s label, Island Records, and were the talk of the town. I saw them play a few times in the Village at Copperfield’s and CBGB’s. They were a flat-out rock’n’roll band, and I liked what I heard.

Sid Bernstein was their manager and was famous in his own right for bringing The Beatles to America. I thought he was a huge deal. y meeting with him at his office in the Carnegie Hall building was a little weird. First, he asked if I would like to sit on his knee, which I awkwardly declined. The next minute, he was hiring me to produce their record. That was the first and last time I saw him.

We rehearsed for a month in a house we rented on Long Island. Once that wrapped, we returned to New York to record at Mediasound. the recording was done, we booked Ranch Studios near Nederland, Colorado, to do the mixing. Even though it’s only about twenty miles west of Boulder, it felt like the middle of nowhere. It didn’t help that it was the dead of winter and we were eighty-four hundred feet above sea level in the middle of the Rocky Mountains. The temperature was consistently below zero degrees Fahrenheit, and the elevation often left us out of breath. In spite of all that, the five of us—Chris, Ned and his girlfriend April, and Howard and I—had a great time putting the music together. The whole experience was unforgettable.

Unfortunately, Island Records wasn’t impressed with the results, and the album failed to see the light of day until it was finally released by Dreamco Music in 2010 as Chris DeMarco The 70s—Riff Raff. Still, the music is good, and it’s a great snapshot of the late-70s New York rock scene.

Chapter 17

Let’s whip it good

It started with a phone call from my accountant, Neal Levin, who took care of the Record Plant and so, by default, me. Neal’s office was in Beverly Hills, very upscale and impressive. He was tall, thin, buttoned-down, and always softly spoken. He showed little emotion. It seemed like nothing could rattle him. His staff was all female—maybe ten women in total. He was the only male in the office. He had an electric foot massager under his desk.

DEVO were one of Neal’s clients. He said they were interested in meeting me and wanted me to work on their upcoming third album for Warner Bros.

Neal set up a meeting for a few days later at the Record Plant. He told me that they were facing a do-or-die situation with Warners: their first two albums, 1978’s Q: Are We Not Men? We Are DEVO! and 1979’s Duty Now For The Future, had met with limited commercial success, and label wasn’t happy. A lot of money had been invested in both promotion and personnel. Brian Eno produced Are We Not Men?, and fellow David Bowie alumnus Ken Scott produced the follow-up.

Other than seeing DEVO perform at the Starwood, I didn’t know much about them. I knew they were from Akron, Ohio, and that they featured two sets of brothers: Mark and Bob Mothersbaugh and Gerald and Bob Casale, along with a drummer, Alan Myers. Neal told me that they knew all about me, though, and were eager to meet.

I would come to learn that not only were DEVO from America’s heartland, Akron, Ohio, but they were also art students at Kent State who had been inspired to form the band after witnessing the bloody campus massacre on May 4, 1970. Through their music, they were dealing with fears about the environment and the ongoing civil rights issues that were gripping the nation. They had a sense that we were amid a new civil war. I knew where they were coming from and what they had to say was necessary.

The day came for our meeting. It was in the early afternoon, and I was expecting to meet DEVO and take them on a studio tour. Howard and I were sitting in the front office with a view of the parking lot when a small black car with tinted windows pulled in. There was no mistaking them. It was DEVO.

Seeing them getting out of the car was a sight to behold. They were all dressed exactly alike, in red worker’s coveralls, rubber boots that came up just over their calves, and red hard hats, each with a small box attached with clear plastic tubes that went straight up their noses. Their faces were covered in what looked like grease and dirt.

Howard and I were used to seeing eccentric artists’ arrivals and departures at the Plant, but this was on another level. Everyone’s chin fell to the floor, mine included. Howard and I took them on tour of the studio, and they turned the place upside down. It was a hilarious, quintessentially DEVO moment, though I later learned that the reason the band in costume wasn’t to impress me or the Record Plant staff. t was because they were performing in Neil Young’s film Human Highway, which was shooting on a soundstage down the street at Raleigh Studios. They were on their lunch break and had decided to stay in costume because it would take too long to get changed, clean up, and then put their costumes back on for the afternoon’s filming.

Human Highway was about a world full of nuclear pollution. Its premise aligned with the Three Mile Island nuclear radiation leak, which was front-page news at the time. DEVO played nuclear waste workers who didn’t like their jobs and were themselves radioactive. The film fit perfectly into the band’s viewpoint that our world was de-evolving and that an apocalypse was looming over all of us.

Feeling good about the meeting, the boys invited us to their rehearsal space on Wilcox Avenue, just off the Sunset Strip. Coincidentally, it was just across the street from Motown’s offices at 6464 Sunset, at the corner of Sunset and Wilcox. It was in a row of old storefronts that had seen better days. The front windows had been frosted over when they were converted to rehearsal studios, and the place itself was small, crowded, sweltering, and filled with gear.

Howard and I showed up for a few sessions. The band’s main visionaries, instigators, and songsmiths were Jerry Casale and Mark Mothersbaugh. Although all the DEVOs had their hats in the ring, Jerry and Mark were the ones whose ideology drove the band. The rehearsals gave me a chance to learn what they were thinking, listen to the songs, and gain some insight into who they were, and for the band to get to know Howard and me. I wanted to establish a bond with them.

We started talking about what they had in mind for the record, and these downhome midwestern boys from Akron kept returning to their love of R&B, which they listened to growing up. They were fans of Motown and early roots music. They loved Stevie and those early Prince records. They liked the sound of the Moog bass and electronica that Malcolm and I had cooked up with TONTO. It was a shock to see that these white alien robot spud boys from Ohio wanted to put an alien head on an R&B body. It was a hybrid of sorts and a brilliant idea. I loved the concept, and I immediately understood why they wanted to invite me to their party.

By the time Howard and I joined the band for rehearsals, they already had a good idea of what they wanted to do. The songs we heard had been composed, arranged, and meticulously rehearsed soon after their move to LA a year earlier. My main goal was to help select which songs to record, envision how to integrate the synth sounds into the songs, plan our approach for the upcoming recording sessions, and ensure that no technical issues would stand in the way. I had to remove any barriers that would stop them from performing to the limits of their potential and do it all on a reasonable budget. After my experience with them in rehearsal, I understood that DEVO knew precisely who they were. It was my job as a producer to enhance their singular vision, not to change them.

Even though the Plant was a beehive of activity twenty-four-seven, we were able to lock out the studio, which meant we could take our time and work without disruption. My first strategy was to change the band’s expectations of how to work in the studio. The boys expected to set up all their instruments, put on headphones, record, and then, between takes, trudge back and forth between the control room and the studio to listen to what they’d done. I was the big disrupter. I arranged the sessions so the entire band could play together in the control room at high volume, just like playing live onstage in front of an audience. The only exception was the drummer, Alan, who played the band’s only purely acoustic instrument. Howard and I put his drums in the middle of the . They were perfectly isolated and benefited from Studio A’s splendid acoustics.

I had long been recording live drum kits from the drummer’s point of view and treating the kit as a collection of individual instruments, and DEVO’s drum tracks were no exception. Each drum had its own sound and its own microphone. The hi-hat, tom-toms, and cymbals panned naturally across the mix. The panning motion is musical and emotional and as essential as meter, pitch, and duration. The drummer is the only player naturally immersed inside their instrument, and Alan was the only one using stereo headphones for the basic recordings.

Alan had a great sound. His playing had precision and power while still being sensitive to the feel of the songs. He wasn’t a drum machine, locked into a strict tempo; I could feel the subtle, rubato movement in his playing. Great recordings always start with a solid rhythm track, and I felt confident he would deliver the goods.

Howard and I employed a pair of newly developed PZMs (pressure zone microphones) to record the drums. They were mounted on four-by-four plywood rolling stands and positioned approximately ten feet away from the kit to the left and right at ear level. They served as our ambient room mics, and by placing them on a barrier surface, they were immune to the phase cancellations or coloration typical in conventional microphones that pick up sound reflections from all directions. The PZMs provided a great sense of presence in the recording, especially at the top end. A good example of the effect can be heard in the solo drum part at the opening of ‘Whip It.’ I think we were the first to use them at the Record Plant.

As always, I had set up an open mic in the control room so that Alan could hear precisely what was going on without anybody having to push the talkback button. In the control room, we could mostly do away with headphones. This improved communication, especially when it came to reading eye contact and body language. We could always hear exactly what was happening in the loudspeakers as the music went directly to the tape. DEVO were at their best when they were all playing at the same time, and we were able to recreate that in the studio.

Studio A’s control room didn’t have any rear , so we brought in a set of JBL 4320s, put them on stands in the back, and set up the control room for discrete quad monitoring. It was basically the same setup Malcolm and I had created years before, moving the audio from an objective, stereo interpretation to a subjective quad one. The listener occupied the same space as the music, and we placed the instruments around us in the speakers and listened to them talk to each other.

Quad monitoring at a high volume on big speakers proved to be an effective tool for getting the best possible performances out of the band. You could feel the sonic energy pounding your chest and moving through your body. It was like being onstage, and it brought more emotion to their music.

Once we had the basic in the can, we used the studio to overdub acoustic instruments, drums, guitars, vocals, and various other instruments in the usual way. I had long since learned that the studio itself is a musical instrument—a place to perform together in the same space as the music, a musical instrument that you inhabit. It was exciting to see DEVO realize this too.

Mark and Jerry both had powerful alpha personalities. They often had very different ideas, and they were very passionate about those ideas. Sometimes, I would work on a sound when Jerry or Mark were out of the control room. When they came back, I’d be expecting some praise, only for one of them to say something like, ‘What’s that wimpy sound?’ Crestfallen, I would take it back to the drawing board.

There were other times when I would offer something they hadn’t considered, like having three isolated guitar amplifiers, which was met with surprised enthusiasm. We used the isolation booth and room traps for the amps, giving each a different sound—clean, crunchy, and distorted, mono or stereo. I could then mix and match at the console and season to taste, depending on what we were into, or if there was some other engineering issue. I paid particular attention to the bottom end, especially the relationship between the bass and the drums, because that’s where R&B energy lives.

Freedom Of Choice was released in May 1980 and catapulted the band to mainstream success. The album peaked at number 22 on the Billboard charts, and its biggest hit, ‘Whip It,’ became an iconic sonic symbol and the definition of the 80s new wave sound.

The main sonic hooks in ‘Whip It’ alternate between a five-note ascent and a three-note descent. It’s a melodic question and answer. It was played with a synthesizer, electric guitar, and synth bass. Each part was unique and had to stand on its own from beginning to end. The arrangement was straightforward, with four equal voices and drums each having their own place on the stereo sound stage. There was no additional production—no smarmy string pads or tons of reverb or effects. The chorus featured two synthesizer notes that go back and forth a semitone apart and have a cheesy, almost Playskool-like sound that created a kind of robotic tension.

The lyrics work so well because they function on two different levels. One interpretation held that they were about all-American capitalist, can-do clichés—a tongue-in-cheek pep talk satirizing hollow American optimism. A kind of Horatio Alger thing: ‘Move ahead, try to detect it, it’s not too late, you’re number one, there’s nobody else like you, you can whip it.’ But perhaps the more popular take was that the song was really about sexual innuendos, S&M, and masturbation. ‘I really whipped it good last night.’ I think it was about both. Regardless, the double entendre served the song well.

Jerry and Mark trade vocal lines back and forth in a call-and-response style. Mark sings in a kind of nasal drawl while Jerry employs a more cartoonish, authoritarian voice. The vocals were sung very close to the mic. I wanted it to feel like you were standing next to them.

For the all-important crack of the whip, we recorded a real whip, live, in the long, narrow alleyway between the administration and studio buildings. It was about four feet wide and around seventy-five feet long and very reverberant. Early one morning, when the place was quiet, we put a U87 condenser mic close to the door of Studio A and then placed a KM 84 condenser mic way down the alleyway, just past the entrance to Studio B.

I tried to ‘crack that whip’ and ended up snapping myself in the face. As I recollect, it was Jerry who did it on the record, standing the doorway of Studio A with the bullwhip in his hand. Howard was running back and forth, setting and resetting microphones, then making adjustments at the console and tape machines. We got a few good cracks and then added a synth sound on top of it. There was a lot of laughing. The sound was epic.

‘Whip It’ was certainly twisted and original. It turned out to be a great sing-along, rallying cry for the new wave movement. It had all the fingerprints of DEVO that you would expect—fun, different, and a little off. I think it was Mark who said that it came from a good place—a pure, creative, open collaboration—and to me that’s when all the best stuff happens.

I worked to keep the recordings sounding raw, edgy, and close, especially the vocals. What was important with DEVO was keeping it in your face, with each part being one singular voice. It was very much like the approach Malcolm and I had used recording the vocals and the isolated mono synth parts on Stevie Wonder’s songs years before. This tight, immersive, slightly twisted, first-person approach was rich and full frequency, with plenty of kick drum and bass.

Freedom Of Choice was, for the most part, fun to produce and record. We all ended up happily working together, and it all fell into place. I know that the production and engineering approach Howard and I followed significantly affected the way DEVO performed in the studio and contributed to the unique sound of the recording itself, even when it was mixed down into stereo.

DEVO’s theory of de-evolution offered a predictive glimpse of humanity’s destructive nature and formed the conceptual groundwork for the band, offering a strange and subversive warning about the regression of humankind in a single word. Looking back, they were right in their view that our society was unwinding, as we see our current leaders ignoring the advice of scientists and the incomprehensible upside-down surreal behavior of some of our politicians. Environmental pollution and global warming are bringing about our own undoing. De-evolution is a reality, and Booji Boy (the masked character Mark played in early DEVO shows) saw it all in his crystal ball.

Freedom Of Choice was mastered by Ken Perry at Capitol Studios. Unfortunately, we could not deliver the gift of the quad experience we had in the studio to our audiences because you couldn’t store spatial information electrically in those days. In 2022, I remixed ‘Whip It’ from the original multitrack analog masters in Dolby Atmos for Rhino Records with engineer Bret Mazur. It only took forty-two years to offer DEVO fans what we were hearing in the control room at the Record Plant.

The core message of both the album and its title track is that while people have free will, there are many who don’t want it and instead want to be told what to do. We need to recognize that. If we don’t, we will have our ‘freedom of choice’ taken from us. DEVO’s message has stood the test of time. Their songs were and still are politically subversive yet pop-friendly at the same time. ‘Whip It’ was their only gold-certified single, and it has continued to influence our culture to this day.

Mark and Jerry were the creators of the iconic music video for ‘Whip It,’ which is where the S&M vibe comes into focus. In classic DEVO style, they took the double entendre of ‘whipping it’ to the absurd.

The red ‘energy dome’ hats featured on the album cover and in the ‘Whip It’ video were Mark and Jerry’s invention. They were to ‘help contain and recycle your karma,’ which was supposedly flowing from the top of your head. It was a brilliant marketing concept that has endured in our pop culture.

Freedom Of Choice gave DEVO the momentum and credibility Warner Bros demanded. Thanks to the album’s success, the label let Mark and Jerry follow through with their original desires to do everything themselves, for better or worse. They self-produced their next album, The New Traditionalists. Warners might have been expecting another ‘Whip It,’ but the band took things in a darker, less pop-oriented direction. They took their de-evolution further, and the new record did not perform commercially like Freedom Of Choice. I wonder what it would have been like if Howard and I had stuck around.

Chapter 18

It was the 80s, after all

DEVO and ‘Whip It’ broke open the early 80s synth-driven, new wave-meets-punk sound that would come to dominate radio, record stores, and the emerging new world of music videos. Culturally, those days were all about new technology, the future, bold colors, and new sounds. The synthesizer had moved centerstage, and Howard and I were right there with it. Once again, I found myself at the forefront of technology and innovation. I was also the main guy behind the board, and with Howard my side, we had our pick of projects.

I first met David Sanborn at Mediasound. He was in the Paul Butterfield Blues Band with Trevor Lawrence, Steve Madaio, and Howard ‘Buzzy’ Feiten. Butterfield was there to record, and Malcolm was the house engineer for the sessions. It was around the time Malcolm and I were just getting started with Stevie. It wasn’t long before every connected, and the band ended up playing on many of our sessions. David played alto sax on ‘Tuesday Heartbreak’ and both Steve Madaio and Trevor played on ‘Superstition.’ The three of them became our go-to horn section. Guitarist Feiten also became a regular on our dates with Stevie and on many other projects. So, we weren’t exactly strangers when, in early 1980, David asked me to produce his next album for Warner Bros.

I was stoked. The Warners A&R people suggested I check out the studio they owned in Burbank, Amigo Studios, which I remembered from some synthesizer sessions I had done with The Doobie Brothers and others a few years before. It was the label’s home base for producers and recording artists, and it was apparent that money had not been an issue in building the place. The studio complex was behind electric gates and very spread out. The main building was one story with Spanish colonial red tile roofs, plus a two-story warehouse in the back. There were three studios, two mastering rooms, a vault where they kept the Warner Bros tape archive, and a large, empty, echoey warehouse that was perfect to record drums.

Howard and I went over to check it out. Studio E was a beautifully designed acoustical room. It had a Harrison console and Massenburg Monitors, and I could sense Tom Hidley’s influence in its acoustic design. There was a vast collection of excellent microphones and outboard gear, and a beautiful Yamaha C7 grand piano. It was the right fit.

I always had a penchant for the latest and greatest in technology, and I’d heard through the grapevine that Amigo was one of the very first studios to have a 3M Digital Audio Mastering System, which had hit the street at the end of 1978. It was one of the first digital audio recording and mastering systems and the first to offer thirty-two-track, sixteen-bit capability at fifty kilohertz, running at forty-five inches per second. It was genuinely pushing the technology of the time to the limit.

I wanted to get my hands on this system, and there it was, staring me in the face. After some demonstrations and tests, I felt confident about recording David’s album digitally. It would be one of the first albums, if not the first, to be recorded digitally for Warners.

On the plus side, there was no analog tape hiss, compression, coloration, or any need for Dolby noise reduction. The transient response was excellent and very evident in the percussion recordings. The machine also gave us another eight tracks. In 1982, there was no such thing as a digital audio workstation (DAW) with virtually unlimited tracks, so having eight more tracks was a blessing.

On the minus side, the machine tended to overheat and shut down. We solved that problem by removing the power supply from the cabinet and putting it on the floor with a fan blowing cool air on it. I also couldn’t edit and splice tape like I was used to, so any editing had to wait until we had a mix laid onto the two-track Ampex ATR analog machine.

The band David assembled consisted of some incredibly talented musicians: Omar Hakim on drums, Marcus Miller on bass, Paulinho Da Costa on percussion, Michael Sembello on guitar, and Don Freeman on keyboards. With such a remarkable rhythm section, we moved away from pure jazz and ventured into a fusion of jazz, R&B, and pop.

I had already spent a considerable amount of time in the studio with Michael, initially as part of Stevie’s band, Wonderlove. I had also produced some demo recordings with him at Indigo Ranch. He possessed an incredible voice with a sweet tenor tone. During those Indigo sessions, we had recorded a version of Michael’s song ‘Maniac,’ which he co-wrote with Dennis Matkosky. The song later went on to become a major hit after being featured in the film Flashdance, propelling Michael into the spotlight as a solo artist.

Everyone in the band was already familiar with each other, having played together on numerous sessions over the years. was the cream of the crop of LA’s studio players. We all gathered at Michael’s place to rehearse and collect our ideas on the eight-track recorder in his home studio. Then, as a group, we selected the best tracks. The lineup of musicians was truly extraordinary, and the band’s chemistry was so tight that I wished we could have captured those rehearsal sessions in their entirety, from the very first downbeat.

During this process, we surprised David by suggesting two songs featuring Michael’s vocals, which deviated from the original plan of an all-instrumental record. It was a significant departure for Sanborn to have vocals on his record, but he was thrilled with the concept. The resulting album, As We Speak, includes ‘Back Again,’ written by Don Freeman and Dennis Belfield, as the closing track on side A, and ‘Love Will Come Someday,’ a collaboration between Sembello and David Batteau, as the final track on side B.

Once we arrived at Amigo, the setup was very straightforward, with everybody playing in the studio except for David, who we put in the isolation room to record his guide sax tracks. We took our time getting the drums just right, using Amigo’s extensive mic collection. After recording and listening to a few tests with Omar, we were delighted with the digital recordings’ clarity, resolution, and dynamic range. As usual for me, the drums and percussion were recorded and mixed from the player’s point of view. The bass and electric keyboards were recorded direct. The guitar was tracked with an isolated amplifier, and later, once we had the track, we brought Michael and the others into the control room, as required, for doubles, fills, and solos.

Paulinho’s percussion tracks were all recorded separately, in an all-day double session. Since he’d attended the rehearsals at Sembello’s, he knew what to expect. His gear arrived in a truck with two roadies to set it up. It filled four or five large road cases and the entire room when it was all unpacked. He had a collection of some of the most esoteric percussion instruments on the planet. We set them up, creating several different mic stations. Paulinho went from one to another, checking what worked and what didn’t.

With our combined backgrounds in R&B, what came out of the recording sessions was much funkier than you’d expect on a smooth jazz album. It was still on the fusion end of funk, particularly the songs ‘Port Of Call’ and ‘Rush Hour,’ which grooved hard primarily due to Marcus and Omar. We all connected very well and had a sense of serenity. We had happy hearts and did a lot of laughing. David was the great instigator, and it was smooth sailing.

Once we’d finished recording the basics at Amigo, we began planning David’s overdubs. Since David lived in New York, Howard and I decided that we would travel east for a change. I checked with 3M, and they assured me they would make a digital machine available for us in New York. So, we booked some time in my old stomping ground, Mediasound, where David and I had first met. I thought recording his sax solos in Studio A, formerly a church, with its excellent acoustics, would be terrific.

I arrived a few hours before the session with the digital masters under my arm and was met with a huge problem. The tape machine 3M sent had a totally different alignment, and the masters would not play back on it. Since it was new technology, nobody knew how to fix it. We were unable to retrieve the recordings we’d made in LA. It was a heart-stopper—one of the very few times in my entire career that I had to cancel a session because of technical issues.

3M came to the rescue a day or two later and flew in a tech who could fix it, but by that time, Mediasound was no longer available. I booked time at Soundworks, which was located in the basement of the very trendy nightclub Studio 54. The club was in an old Broadway theater was closed during the day. The studio in the basement had lines running up to the club’s stage, so we recorded some of David’s solos there during the day, when the club was empty.

Throughout the process, the digital machine proved to be both a blessing and a curse. I was naïve to think that this new technology would not have its share of growing pains. I felt a huge wave of relief when I heard the chirp of the plane’s wheels touching down back in LA with the masters safely recorded.

To complete the record, Howard and I moved to George Massenberg’s studio, the Complex, in Culver City. George was respected for designing and manufacturing high-end audio gear, especially equalizers. The console in the studio was one of two he built for himself. The board sounded incredible and was ideally suited to our new digital recordings. We finished a few more overdubs and then moved on to mixing. We were using the latest in digital recording, which sounded great, but didn’t have fader automation, so we mixed in real time.

Warner Bros said that I had gone over budget and would have to wait until the album recouped before Id get my royalty, but I didn’t care. For me, it was all about serving the music. As We Speak reached number 70 on the Billboard 200, number 32 on the R&B chart, and number 1 on the jazz chart. I received my first royalty check in 2025, but it was worth it.

After delivering David’s album, my plan was to produce a Michael Sembello album for Warners, which the label had already expressed an interest in. However, while I was in New York overdubbing Sanborn’s record, the label engaged in some rather brash shenanigans, signing Michael behind my back and hiring Phil Ramone to produce his debut, Bossa Nova Hotel. Needless to say, I felt betrayed by both Michael and Warners. His project had been set to be a keystone in my progression as a producer, but now it wasn’t to be. That did some serious damage to my future plans.

A few years later, Michael told me that Ramone had spent most of the sessions sleeping on the couch in the back of the studio. Thankfully, Michael had strong ideas on his own. Released in September 1983, Bossa Nova Hotel reached only number 80 on Billboard, but the first single, ‘Maniac,’ was a huge hit, due in no small part to its inclusion in Flashdance. It spent two weeks atop the Billboard Hot 100 and was nominated for seven Grammy Awards, including Record Of The Year, Song Of The Year, and Best Male Pop Vocal Performance. As part of the Flashdance soundtrack, he shared the Grammy for Best Album Of Original Score Written For A Motion Picture Or A Television Special. I couldn’t have been happier for Michael’s success, but I also couldn’t help but feel disappointment that I had been excluded from it.

In the late 70s and early 80s, as I was finishing work on the DEVO album, cocaine came upon us like a tsunami. It seemed like everybody was ‘tooting and snorting’ or asking, ‘Do you want to do a bump?’ Artists, engineers, record company executives, lawyers, staff at the live venues, agents—everybody seemed to be taking part. In many cases, the record companies were adding drugs as expense line items into their recording budgets. Some studios even had their own resident drug dealers. This was a big change from the early days of my career, and it was much more sinister. It was totally out of control.

Since everybody around me was using it, I thought, What could be wrong with it? After a few months, I found myself eagerly looking forward to my next line. All it took was seventy-five dollars for a bindle of coke, a razor, and a rolled-up ten-dollar bill. I was always ready to have a toot in the Rainbow’s upstairs private bar, the Starwood’s green room, the men’s room at Studio One; in people’s offices, during business meetings, at the Record Plant, or on a mirror on the top of my dresser in my bedroom.

I remember my classic prank in Studio A. I’d take a white grease pencil and the one-sided razor we used for editing tape, draw two white lines on the black Formica desk, and leave a rolled-up ten-dollar bill with the razor next to it. In the low light, it looked real. It was good for a laugh when people came into the control room and tried to snort it behind my back. The look on their faces was priceless. But it wasn’t long before I was finding myself in my bedroom at one in the morning, half awake, alone, chopping out a line in my underwear. I looked in the mirror and saw my haggard, hungover face. I realized that I was well on my way to becoming an addict, and I knew right then that I had to stop. I stopped cold turkey, which caused me some suffering and anxiety over the next few months, but I discovered that I could leave it, which is precisely what I did.

Cocaine was a scourge that took its toll on so many of us.

The shadow of drug use still lurks over the entire recording industry and now our entire society. DEVO were one of the few bands I worked with around that time who had never touched it. They were able to hang onto their clean-cut, Midwestern upbringing.

Looking back on those times leaves me with a feeling of bittersweet melancholia, knowing that I too was a part of that world. We all lived, metaphorically, at the same house at the end of the block. Most of us, me included, did not realize at the time that drugs were slowly destroying our lives. And many exceptionally creative people who should be standing with us today met their untimely ends because of drugs and alcohol.
