Chapter 19

What’s a nice gay man doing marrying a sweet woman like this?

When I told my friend David Knight—the talent booker from my Starwood days who later became my dear friend and housemate—that I had gotten married, there was a look of total disbelief on his face.

‘Bob, Bob, that’s ridiculous!’ he replied. He was incredulous, almost yelling. ‘Have you lost your mind?’

It only took me three years to realize how right he was.

In August 1986, I moved back to Hollywood from Topanga. I was already on the downside of being more or less famous. I was a little hungry, and I was looking to climb my next mountain. I chanced upon a one-bedroom apartment that allowed dogs on the corner of Franklin Ave and Camino Palmero Street. It was a typical wood-framed two-story stucco complex with a center courtyard, swimming pool, and underground parking—basically a filing cabinet for people. Coincidentally, it was on the same street where Gary Kellgren’s old mansion once stood, and where, years before, Malcolm and I had met Gary and Chris for the first time and made the deal we sealed with an earthquake to move Stevie Wonder and TONTO to the Record Plant.

Barbara Ruth Shelley lived there, too. We met one sunny afternoon, walking our dogs on the lawn in front of the building, me with my faithful Tako and she with her little Pomeranian, Dom Perignon. Babsie, as I soon began to call her, was a lovely lady—full of caring, intuitive, and inspirational, with a great sense of humor. At least, she always laughed at my corny jokes. We started spending more and more time together, and our friendship blossomed. Both of us were from nice, Jewish, middle-class homes. Her real last name was Applebaum. Like me, she had a genuine love for Motown, R&B, and the music business.

I loved the passion Babsie brought to everything she did. When we met, she ran an independent public-relations business, where her clients included Aretha Franklin, Ray Parker Jr., and Stevie Ray Vaughan. Her past accolades included working with Clive Davis, heading the PR departments first at Arista Records and later at Rhino Entertainment and Casablanca Records. We had a lot of common experiences and mutual friends in the music business. She cut a mighty bow wave, and she was a force to be reckoned with. I was impressed.

As our friendship grew, Babsie and I shared a good deal about our pasts, including my sexuality. She said she didn’t care about it and had many gay friends. Then, one afternoon, driving back into Hollywood from the Valley, I light-heartedly said that maybe we should get married.

‘Yes!’ she replied immediately. I guess it was a proposal.

A few weeks later we got married, unfortunately, and with only a little hindsight, for all the wrong reasons.

I’d convinced myself that I was bisexual, and that relationships were about the person who lived inside. That was what was important, not our ‘plumbing.’ Naïvely, I never considered the emotional implications for either of us.

Babsie and I got married before a justice of the peace at City Hall. The marriage was witnessed by Howard Siegel for me and Sylvia Cunliffe for Babsie. A late luncheon followed the ceremony in a quiet, upscale restaurant. It was just the four of us, and I had no idea what was going to happen next.

So, why did we get married?

At that moment, it all sounded great. Part of me thought I would be better accepted in the music business. Back then, it was ‘don’t ask, don’t tell’ at best, and besides, what a power couple we would be! Another part of me thought that it was finally time to grow up—something I still haven’t done. Perhaps it was a stereotypical mid-life crisis.

We moved just down the block into an upscale late-50s apartment on the top floor of a building at the corner of Franklin Avenue and La Brea called the Franklin Towers. The apartment faced south and had a built-in wet bar in the living room, plus concierge, underground parking, heated pool, and a spectacular view of downtown LA.

I started trying to live a heterosexual existence, all the while becoming more and more unconsciously uncomfortable with all of it. I thought I would grow into being more hetero and monogamous if I gave it some time. Still, my marriage soon left me feeling emotionally suppressed and depressed.

Because Babsie was in the public-relations business, there were always parties, shows, and green rooms with lots to eat, drink, and get high. I met many people who didn’t know me and had no idea about my sexuality. They would say disgusting things about the gays inDescript the room behind their backs, which made me angry and resentful.

Babsie had become my ‘beard.’ I began to think that she owned my body and that I had betrayed myself. I had gone back into the closet after decades of being liberated and out. I don’t think she was having any better time of it, either. We were both trying to make it work. We settled down into a routine of humdrum co-existence. There was sex, but more out of duty than passion.

It was early 1987 when Stockbridge school’s founder and headmaster, Hans Maeder, came by on what turned out to be his last visit to California. In my youth, Hans was my mentor, guiding light, and surrogate father. He and Stockbridge had profoundly changed my world and set me on my life’s path. I had maintained a friendship with him over the years, and now, here he was, all these years later, sitting alone in the middle of the couch in my living room.

I sat across from Hans in an easy chair and introduced my new wife, and we had a polite afternoon visit, sharing stories about our past and what we were doing now and looking off into the future. e told me that he had prostate cancer and would be getting treatment for it upon returning to New York. I didn’t realize it at the time, but that visit was his way of saying goodbye.

As the afternoon drew to a close, Hans handed me a package wrapped in plain brown paper. He looked directly into my eyes, and with his slight German accent still present, he said, ‘I know you will enjoy these.’ It was a small collection of gay photography magazines. They looked dog-eared and well-read.

Without him ever saying a word, Hans’s gesture told me that he knew all about my sexuality, right back to my Stockbridge days. He had never said anything or indicated in any way that he knew until that moment when he handed me his parting gift. In the next instant, like a bolt of lightning, I realized that he shared those same feelings of living a life closeted from his true desires. It brought back memories of my liberated days on Fire Island. Hans, my life’s greatest teacher and mentor, had taught me one last lesson without saying a word: accept my sexuality. Be who I am.

That was the last time I saw him. He died in New York City in 1988.

About a year after Hans’s visit, Babsie and I had to move from the Franklin Towers. The building was to be converted into condos, and they wanted $50,000 from us to stay. We didn’t have that, so we moved around the corner, back into a one-bedroom apartment on the roof of my old building on Fuller Avenue that passed as penthouse. I called it Tar Beach.

While I was struggling through my relationship with Babsie, I continued taking on projects and trying to push the envelope both musically and technologically.

It was then that I got a call from my friend Russ Regan at Motown, asking if I was interested in producing Wilson Pickett’s next album. How could I say no?

Wilson Pickett was born in Alabama. He started singing in church as a young boy and gradually developed the raspy, passionate gospel delivery that would define him for his entire career. When we started working together, he had a long history of hit records under his belt, but unfortunately his drinking and drugging were taking their toll on him, and as far as hit records were concerned, he hadn’t rung that bell in quite some time. Long gone were the days of ‘Mustang Sally’ and ‘In The Midnight Hour,’ which helped give birth to soul music at Stax Studio in Memphis.

Russ hired me to produce Pickett’s 1987 album American Soul Man. We recorded and mixed it in Studio D at Westlake Audio in Hollywood—another classic Hidley control room. After all, Tom had been the designer, founder, and part-owner of Westlake. It was a beautiful recording space. If I recall correctly, Studio D had been built for top engineer Bruce Swedien to record Michael Jackson’s Thriller. The big downside was the console, a Harrison Series 10. It was one of the first generation of automated consoles, and while it sounded good it was constantly overheating and proved to be very unreliable.

We set up the band in the control room, as was my usual approach. The players were plucked from my standard crew, too, with Buzzy Feiten on guitar, Reggie McBride on bass, and Ollie Brown on drums, all Wonderlove alums. Much to my surprise, Ollie showed up with an early sort of drum machine and a programmer, and although it had good sounds and was rhythmicallyDescript correct, to this day I think it compromised the feel of the record.

There were some new additions to the group. uitarist Gary Myrick and percussionist Steve Klong, with whom I had recently worked on Gary’s Epic Records solo album, Language. Bobby Martin took on keyboards and sax and worked as our arranger. He had played with Etta James and the Frank Zappa band as a keyboard player and vocalist. For the horn dates, we had Stevie Wonder alum Steve Madaio on trumpet and Bobby Martin and Brad Silwood on saxophones. The background vocals, as usual, were sung by Julia and Maxine Waters.

Pickett (who we rarely called by his first name) usually came to the studio a little intoxicated or high, which was okay during the tracking dates as he only sang guide vocals. When it came time to do the actual lead vocals, however, I had to have a talk with him. It went something like this:

ME: ‘Pickett, you can’t come to the studio drunk and loaded. You have a choice. You can party and stay away or come to the studio sober.’

PICKETT: ‘Okay, okay, I’ll do it. All I really want in the studio is a tureen of Chablis.’

It was an unforgettable moment.

Pickett got serious after that, and when he was sober, he could sing circles around anybody. That’s precisely what he did when it came to tracking his lead vocals. But he remained strictly a client. We never developed the kind of friendship I had with Billy Preston, for example. It was all business.

I remember hearing about Pickett making unreasonable demands with Motown’s management and A&R people—how he threw a fit and turned over somebody’s desk. It became a love/hate relationship, and once I delivered the record, the label dropped him like a hot potato. They never really promoted the record with any enthusiasm. I think they were happy to see him gone, and I never saw him again either.

Both Wilson Pickett and Billy Preston were from the South, Pickett from Alabama and Preston from Texas, and they both grew up steeped in the gospel tradition. But Wilson’s life was destroyed by his heavy cocaine addiction and alcoholism, and I heard that he later became increasingly aggressive toward his bandmates, friends, and family. His story was, in ways, similar to Preston’s, with the drugs and the alienation from the record industry. The big difference was that Billy was kind and loving. I never saw him be angry or abusive toward anybody. Both men were brought to their knees by their cocaine and alcohol addictions, and while both were also inducted posthumously into the Rock And Roll Hall Of Fame, it was tragic that neither of them lived to see it.

American Soul Man was also the last project I worked with Howard Siegel. He and I had lived and worked together since 1975, starting with the sessions for Michael Pinder at Indigo Ranch and ending in 1989, when he fell ill with HIV/AIDS. We both knew that his HIV was a death sentence. As fate would have it, our physical relationship had ended years before he got sick. Had our physical attraction continued, I might not be here to tell my tale.

Howard passed away on December 3, 1991. It’s been nearly thirty-five years since his passing. I still celebrate the high holy days with his family. He will forever live on in my heart. RIP, my dear friend.

Chapter 20

Flying down to Rio

Brant Biles was twenty-one years old when we first met at Mates Rehearsal Studios in 1987. I was asked to produce and record an album with a female heavy-metal rocker, Lydia Van Houston. Brant played lead guitar in the band, and that’s where our friendship and our soon-to-be partnership as the Dynamic Duo began.

During the rehearsals, I realized that most of the songs the band planned to record were nothing to write home about, so I asked each of the band members to come in with some new ideas or a new finished song. The next day, Brant showed up with the only song I thought had something going for it.

I block-booked an inexpensive, rather unremarkable place in the Valley called Chameleon Studios for the project and brought along my own second engineer, Matt Pakuko. Chameleon supplied the studio, a gofer, and the coffee. It was a big step down from my days at the Record Plant, but it would do.

Just after we had set everything up and were getting started, Matt called to say that there had been a death in his family and he couldn’t make it. I turned to Brant, who was standing behind me in the control room, and said, ‘Do you know how to plug in a limiter?’

‘Sure,’ he said. ‘I just graduated from audio engineering school.’

Brant was a quintessential rocker, and he looked the part. He was tall and thin, with hair down to his shoulders. I knew after seeing him play live that he shined when he was in front of an audience. All the ladies loved him. He fit perfectly into Hollywood’s rock scene. First and foremost, however, he was a first-class musician who could write, sing, jump around stage, and shred on electric guitar. He had a lot going for him. He also wasn’t afraid to get his hands dirty—something I respected and could relate to.

Brant drove a beat-up, dark green, thirteen-passenger Ford van filled with motorcycle and car parts, bedding, instrument cases, spare tires, and tools. I thought it was a rolling junkyard, but I soon realized that everything had a purpose. Brant was capable of fixing an engine or doing a brake job, and then, in the next minute, he’d be sodering a circuit board, aligning a tape machine, or troubleshooting a patch bay. I saw a lot of Malcolm’s mix of technical and musical attributes in Brant, but I didn’t have a clue yet that I would end up trudging on the long road of destiny with him for nearly twenty-six years.

The first gig we did was for a band called The Flood. I offered him fifty dollars per day.

‘Really?’ he said. ‘You’re gonna pay me to do this?’

This was to be our first of many road trips together. We went to Phoenix, Arizona, to work with the band. They put us in a posh hotel with a room that overlooked a golf course on Camelback Mountain.

After that there were a series of guitar-heavy projects through the late 80s and early 90s, including Robin Trower’s Passion and Bobby Sexton’s Facing Destiny. We worked with a number of bands who didn’t make much of a splash, like The Innocents, The Wild, The Zeros, Gary Myrick’s Language, and The Bus Boys. In 1990, we recorded an excellent funk/metal band from San Francisco called Fungo Mungo, and in 1992, we did Tony McAlpine’s stunning album Freedom To Fly.

Prior to that, back in 1989, Brant and I had gotten the call to record The Odd Get Even

, the eighth studio album by the band Shadowfax, who were named after Gandalf’s horse from The Lord Of The Rings. Going into these sessions, I already knew that Shadowfax were one of the few new age/jazz fusion bands to cross over to a pop audience. They had a strong sense of ensemble and an unparalleled ability to marry the elements of rock, world, jazz, and folk music. The entire band played with virtuosity and complexity, and they consistently displayed their creativity and craftsmanship. In my humble opinion, they were some of the best jazz musicians on the planet.

The essential sound of the group primarily revolved around the band’s founders, Chuck Greenberg and G.E. Stinson. Greenberg played a variety of woodwind instruments, and one in particular, the Lyricon—an analog electronic wind synthesizer that was like a electric oboe—took centerstage. G.E. Stinson played electric guitar with a vast assortment of effects boxes and pedals.

We recorded all the basic tracks at Alpha Studios, which was almost direct copy of Studio E at Amigo. It had an octagon-shaped floating cloud in the control room instead of a compression ceiling and approximately the same geometry as a typical Hidley room, and I recollect the monitors were by Augspurger.

The studio’s owner, Denny Shaw, had been the head technician at Amigo for many years, so it was no surprise that he seemed to have copied the blueprints from Amigo. The room’s acoustics were terrific, and it looked great too. It was finished intricately in redwood and had great lighting. It felt warm and safe. We loved it.

We rented a thirty-two-track 3M digital machine for recording, and just like with David Sanborn, the recorder’s power supply had to live outside the cabinet to stay cool. However, with the machine’s 56kHz sampling and converters, the sound was incomparable. Denny knew how to run the machine intimately from his days at Amigo, and he kept it rolling flawlessly.

The recording console was a Calrec, British-built and straightforward. The layout was easy, the feel precise. It had a great-sounding EQ and excellent transient response. It was very well suited to working in the digital realm. The control room was huge and suitable for what Brant and I had in mind for tracking the band.

Everyone was set up in the control room, except drummer Stuart Nevitt who played in the studio directly in front of the console. To the left of us was Charlie Bisharat playing the electric violin. In the middle was Chuck and his Lyricon. G.E. had his electric guitar rig over on the right side. Behind us was bass player Phil Maggini and keyboard player Doug Maluchnik was to the left. These gifted musicians were capturing it all live, and we were right in the middle of it. Brant and I were really getting the goods.

However, one of the major issues we faced during the sessions was constant bickering and arguing between Chuck and G.E. about parts and arrangements. They were both very emotional about what they liked or didn’t like and had strong ideas about the direction the recording should take. On occasion, one or the other would storm out of the studio. It turned out that part of my gig was to be a peacemaker and keep the project on track. Once they started playing, though, it all came together, and it was perfect.

We overdubbed and mixed at Amigo Studio B, which had an SSL console and a pair of Westlake Audio monitors. We also had access to Studio A’s recording space and its mic snake. The room itself was huge and had good acoustics. It was ideal for our percussion and instrumental overdubs.

We had just started mixing when sound engineer and inventor John Bedini asked us to consider checking out his new audio processor, the Bedini Audio Spatial Environment (BASE). Shadowfax’s music seemed perfect for trying out this new technology, and since we had just started mixing, it seemed ideal to see what it could do. After doing some listening tests on a few of Shadowfax’s digital multitracks, I concluded that Bedini’s processor would take us further down the road toward immersive audio.

The BASE unit was an analog processor that analyzed and separated a stereo signal into separate mono and stereo channels. We could then move the mono information forward, backward, and side to side without losing the stereo ambient space. The stereo space could be increased or decreased, while the mono signal could be placed anywhere we desired.

The result was an enhanced signal that went beyond stereo while creating an extended sound field, making the stereo image more coherent and seamless.

Once again, I put on my pioneering hat and put the processor to the test. We had a stack of five Bedini processors, and I was especially impressed with what they did with the reverb, delay, percussion, and smooth synth tracks. You could feel an almost holographic sonic effect on the reverb and echo busses. Some percussion parts sounded like they were simply hanging out in space. You can hear the results when you listen to the record. The Odd Get Even is still one of the best-sounding recordings I’ve ever made.

The success of The Odd Get Even led to more smooth and progressive jazz projects. I got a call from Russ Freeman, guitarist and founder of The Rippingtons, who asked if Brant and I would be interested in remixing a five-song EP hed co-written and recorded with Ando San, the leader of the Japanese smooth jazz/fusion band T-Square. T-Square had risen to prominence in Japan in the late 70s and are still going strong as I write this.

We remixed the five sides with Russ at Amigo. I thought that the original recordings were overproduced, so I took it upon myself to ditch many of the overdubs and simplify the arrangements, which made the record sound tighter and bigger. Russ and Ando were very surprised and pleased with our results and released our mixes as the ‘American’ version of T-Square. And Russ was so impressed that he asked us to mix the new Rippingtons album, Welcome To The Saint James Club. We mixed the album in May 1990, and it was released in August. Brant played shakers on a tune or two. Working on the album felt effortless, and we had a lot of fun mixing it. It was the band’s first CD to debut at number 1 on the Billboard jazz charts, so we must have been doing something right.

It was just after Christmas when I got a call from David Knight, who was now working at Radio Vision on the East Coast. Hed become a world-renowned talent booker. Everybody in the business knew him. He asked if we would be interested in recording the Rock In Rio II festival in Brazil for broadcast in the States. The first Rock In Rio had taken place in 1985, drawing an audience of about one-and-a-half million people over ten days and featuring legendary performances by Queen, Iron Maiden, Rod Stewart, George Benson, Ozzy Osbourne, and many others.

I considered David’s offer very carefully for about one second, then said yes.

Time was short, as the concerts were going to start on January 18 and would run ten consecutive days at the Maracanã stadium in Rio de Janeiro. With just three weeks to prepare, Brant and I started planning immediately. David hooked us up with TV Globo, Brazil’s premier TV network, which would broadcast the show and provide logistical support. It seemed like no time before we were on a flight to Rio.

I couldn’t help but think of the song ‘Flying Down To Rio’ by Fred Astaire as we sat on the plane. I remember hearing it as a child on a scratchy old 78.

When we were coming in to land, looking out of the window, I could see Sugarloaf Mountain and the looming statue of Christ The Redeemer, arms outstretched above the city.

From above, Rio looked like the gem that I’d expected to see, but it didn’t take long for that expectation to fall flat. It was hot and humid when we landed. As we drove in from the airport, I could see that many of the buildings along the road were discolored with black mold. The hills around the city’s center were inhabited by overcrowded Favelas, the city’s infamous shantytowns, with no running water and raw sewage running from them into the bay.

Our hotel was on the beach, right between Copacabana and Ipanema. It was a multistory architectural gem from the 50s, but it looked a little tattered. A few of its plate-glass entrance doors were broken and covered with plywood. The beaches looked inviting and beautiful, but we were told not to go into the water because it was polluted. That wasn’t a problem, though, as we never had time to set foot on the beach. We dropped our bags, went to the stadium, and immediately got to work.

Usually, we would eat an early dinner and then get picked up by our driver, Armondo, who was permanently assigned to us. Armondo’s car was an Audi sedan that ran on alcohol derived from cane sugar. When we went through the tunnel heading north to Bahia Tijuca and Maracanã, it smelled sweet, like we were inside a bag of sugar.

Armondo had a bushy mustache that kind of exploded under his nose, and he was always happy, animated, and talkative. One night, while stopped at a light, we saw what looked like a group of gorgeous ladies standing on the curb. Brant was ogling them and commenting on how beautiful they were. As we pulled away, Armondo turned his head toward us, laughing.

‘Transvestitas!’ he said.

The whole Rio experience had a timeworn feel about it, like the movie Blade Runner. The Maracanã stadium was huge, with an official capacity of nearly 175,000 people, but unofficially, they packed in nearly 200,000 for the festival. The energy, spirit, and undeniable Brazilian love for the music was always overwhelming.

The festival’s headliners that year included Prince, INXS, Guns N’ Roses, Billy Idol, Carlos Santana, New Kids On The Block, Judas Priest, Queensrÿche, Megadeath, George Michael, A-Ha, and Run-DMC. In total, fifty bands performed. We would arrive at the stadium around 5pm, set up for the first acts, test and align the twenty-four-track tape recorders, and then assign mic inputs to channels, which were different for each act. Then it was showtime. We’d only record the acts that were designated for broadcast, but we were still burning through tape like crazy.

The stage, lighting, sound rigs, and backstage areas were in the center of the field and included several very large truck trailers from the American company Showco. Diesel-electric generators powered the sound and lighting. This backstage area housed the bands and their roadies, the stage sets, the dressing rooms, bathrooms, a cafeteria and green rooms, offices, a medical clinic, and a huge stage and lighting crew. It was like a modern self-sustaining city full of rock stars.

The local crew knew what they were doing, but most of them only spoke Portuguese. Brant and I worked closely with a tech named Zorro who spoke no English, so there was lots of hand waving, grunting, and laughing.

TV Globo had constructed a control room for us very close to the stage, equipped with two Soundcraft twenty-four-input consoles with multitrack busses tied together, making it a forty-eight by twenty-four track board. We used one console at a time—one for the first act and then the second preset for the next act. While the second act played, we’d reset the first console for the next act, and so forth. It was nuts. We tied into the stage sound with a mic splitter, so we shared the microphones and mic choices with the house. We recorded to a pair of Otari model 100 tape machines running at fifteen inches per second with Dolby SR noise reduction. We brought along the Dolby units for the recordings, which, unfortunately, were dropped off the back of the truck when they were delivered. When we opened the flight cases, the racks were bent, and all the sound cards fell out in a pile. It was a huge setback, but Brant and Zorro spent a long night unbending the rack, then rewiring and reinstalling the sound cards, and got them up and running in time.

We also supplied a pallet of Ampex 467 two-inch recording tape. We recorded for hours every day for ten days. Recording and mixing the shows was very hard and detailed, non-stop work, and there was never a take two. You got it the first time or you didn’t get it at all.

For me, the festival’s eighth night was the highlight, in particular a stellar performance of ‘Take On Me’ by the Norwegian pop trio A-Ha. Hearing the crowd of nearly two hundred thousand singing along, a cappella, was inspiring, emotional, and overwhelming.

We would finish recording the shows around 11pm, and then head to the mixing studio, Impresario Digital, in a small outlying suburb on the other side of Rio. Armondo would drive us across town at high speed with a security detail. After our drive in from the airport, we were worried at first about what we would find out in the suburbs, but the building was newly constructed, and inside it we found a very modern recording studio.

Looking out from the studio’s front door, there were weeds, a few abandoned buildings, and a herd of grazing goats. Turning one’s head and looking in, you’d see yet another brand new, Hidley-designed studio. We couldn’t believe it. It reminded us of Studio D at Westlake, and it gave us the feeling that we had never left LA. We mixed onto a Studer A80 and a seventy-two-input SSL G-series console. We brought several BASE spatialization units and used our own KRK speakers for mixing.

We had to mix three songs from each band’s performance that evening, and they needed to be up and ready for broadcast the next day. The band members would come by to select and fix their three songs, sometimes re-singing a lead vocal because the vocals didn’t sound very good, perhaps because the singer was breathing heavily from leaping around the stage. This proved very time-consuming, so we took turns fixing, mixing, and sleeping on a couple of moving blankets under the piano in the studio until it was our turn at the console. We’d mix through the night and into the morning light.

When the songs were ready, a runner from TV Globo would pick up the two-track stereo masters and transmit them via satellite to New York. Armondo would drive us back to the hotel. We were completely wasted and got a few hours of sleep before we up and repeated the previous day’s routine.

For those days in Rio, we were living in a bubble. I didn’t see or experience the city, its culture, music, or the land. Once the final mixing was done and the party was over, everybody made a mad dash the airport. We couldn’t wait to get home.

Before leaving, I stopped at the duty-free shop and bought a beautiful ruby pendant for Babsie, my soon-to-be former wife. When I gave her the pendant, Babsie stuck her nose in the air and told me she didn’t like red. We divorced a few weeks later. There was no fighting or bad vibes. We had simply fallen out of love. It was mutual and merciful.

Babsie moved to a one-bedroom near Melrose, and I moved into a small house on Lamer Street in Burbank. Sadly, she passed away on October 4, 2015, at her home in Scottsdale, Arizona. She was sixty-four years old.

Chapter 21

A producer at the crossroads

Shortly after we returned from Rio, I left town with Brant and headed to Tokyo. T-Square had invited us to Tokyo to mix their new album at Shinano Machi Studios. The two of us walked into the control room, and once again, it felt like we never left LA—it was yet another Tom Hidley control room. Brant remembers that the studio manager’s nickname was ‘Eighty-Eighty,’ which was the same day and date as my birthday, August 8.

On our return, we were running on all eight cylinders. We got to work on The Rippingtons’ Curves Ahead, which I believe was the highlight of what the band had to offer. Russ brought together an ensemble of amazingly talented musicians, including David Benoit, Jeff Kashiwa, Kim Stone, and Steve Reid. Each of them shone. The music had a big band feel from back in the day, but it was still very much a smooth jazz journey. We recorded most of it at the Slam Shack, the studio behind the garage at Russ’s house on Lamer Street in Burbank. Brant and I came on board just in time for some additional recording and mixing at Alpha. The album reached number one on Billboard’s contemporary jazz chart.

Next, it was back to Japan, T-Square had asked us to record as well as mix their new album. The sessions didn’t go as smoothly as we would have liked. Recording them proved difficult for us because of the language and cultural differences, which for the guys in the band, too. We could only get so far with waving and grunting. It was easier when we got to the mixing, because then we were communicating with Andoh, the band’s leader, who spoke English, and with whom we already had a close working relationship. With that album in the can, we left Tokyo and headed for home.

Shortly after my divorce from Babsie was finalized, I was living alone on Fuller Avenue when, entirely out of the blue, I got a phone call from Bob Bednarski.

‘Remember me?’ he said.

I didn’t until he reminded me that we had met on Fire Island.

‘I’m moving to LA,’ he said, ‘and I need a place to stay for a couple of weeks while I get settled in.’

I hadn’t seen Bob since 1968, but since I had an empty nest, I said, ‘Sure.’

I picked Bob up a few days later at the Greyhound bus station in Hollywood. When we’d talked on the phone, in my mind’s eye, he was still the willowy young boy from our days on Fire Island. Now, more than twenty years later, I saw that he was a grown man, tall, thin, with shoulder-length bleached blonde hair and an effeminate manner. He didn’t look like he had aged too well, and as we talked, he smoked one cigarette after another. He’d also changed his name to the more gender-neutral Brookie Rendell.

Around this same time, Russ Freeman told me he was building a new house in Colorado with a recording studio on the ground floor and a spectacular view of the Rocky Mountains. He was ready to flee Los Angeles. I liked his old house on Lamer Street in Burbank, so I arranged to rent the place as soon as he made his escape. Brookie arrived just in time to help me with the move, and he became what Bobby Anderson had been during my days living in the East Village during the Ciao! Manhattan saga—my majordomo.

Brookie lived with me for more than ten years. Our relationship was platonic, and he never got a real job, although he claimed he was trying to find one for the first year or so. He ended up taking care of everything: grocery shopping, the dog, furnishing the place, airport runs, and holding down the fort when I was on the road. It was perfect timing.

Russ’s old place was a workingman’s house built in the 50s to house the blue-collar workers building airplanes at Lockheed Martin at what is now Burbank Airport. It had two bedrooms that I used as my office and living room, one bathroom, and a family room with a fireplace that I turned into my bedroom. Out back, attached to the garage, was a room Russ had used as a studio. Brookie set up that space as his quarters, and I had the house. It was ideal.

It was then that I met Janel Silverstein, my very pregnant next-door neighbor. She was so big that I thought she might topple over. ‘I’m ready to take you to the hospital if you need to go!’ I told her. To this day, Janel is still a dear friend, consiglieri, and part of my family. She still looks after my business and personal affairs and is truly my guardian angel.

In July 1992, Brant and I went on a road trip in my little four-cylinder grey Mercedes 190 sedan to Russ’s new house in Colorado, to help with his new album. As usual, Russ had already done most of the recording and wanted us to work on overdubs and mixing.

The house was high in the Rocky Mountains. When we arrived, Russ played us the basic tracks he’d already recorded and said he didn’t understand why the horn and sax parts sounded weak. When Brant and I soloed them, we could hear the players breathing very heavily in the pauses. We realized that they were out of breath because of the altitude.

I didn’t like working up there either. I felt alone, isolated, and out of breath myself. I was happy to hit the road back to LA when we finished. I guess I’m just a city boy at heart. The resulting album, Weekend In Monaco, did well, though, reaching number 2 on Billboard’s contemporary jazz chart. It still makes me smile to think how both the recording location and process couldn’t have been further from a weekend on the French Riviera.

Brant and I still had one more trip to Tokyo left in us, so, after working on Russ’s project, we headed back to Japan to work with T-Square on more great new age/jazz fusion. By now, we had gotten used to each other, and the tracking and mixing was like a hot knife going through butter. There were good hearts all the way around, and we shared plenty of laughs about the funny, warped fusion of Japanese-to-English translations. There were no pocket translators back then. It’s funny the things you remember, like the sign in English at our hotel swimming pool that said, ‘Lets Swimming,’ or the hotel menu items like ‘Frogen Yozert’ and ‘Coffee & Salad.’ Or the time I became constipated from eating too much rice and had to try to explain using hand gestures at the drug store that I needed a laxative. I think the druggist thought I had lost my mind. And when I heard mixed-up expletives like ‘Shit You!’ or ‘Eat My Fuck’ for the first time, I almost wet my pants laughing.

Brant and I were becoming very successful co-conspirators in the studio. Forging ahead together, our flags were flying high. It seemed like we were getting a reputation for mixing smooth jazz/fusion artists, and we were making a good living at it.

On the surface, it seemed like everything was going great, but I began to feel uneasy that my career was on a slowly descending glid path, my days of working with big superstars slowly fading into the distance. One smooth, easy-listening fusion band merged into another. It was like musical chairs, with many players being interchangeable from one band to the next. I began to feel we were creating soundtracks and scores for nonexistent motion pictures. The recorded performances themselves were brilliant, virtuosic, and compelling, but there was no actual storytelling with three to four minutes of purely instrumental pop music.

Looking back, I think I’d had my fill of jazz/fusion. It was just too much of a good thing. I was, and still am, a political creature of the 60s and 70s. I always felt that music was supposed to be a powerful force for social change. Most of the jazz/fusion I worked on left me feeling that something was missing; I was becoming simply a hired gun, a journeyman recording engineer, and mixer, not an equal partner in the creative process.

A welcome change came in 1993, when Dave Wakeling, singer-songwriter in The (English) Beat, and Kate Karam, communications and campaign director for Greenpeace, asked me if I would be interested in producing an album for Greenpeace Productions. The project would feature live recordings from a who’s who of rock and pop stars. The album, to be titled Alternative NRG, aimed to share Greenpeace’s mission to raise awareness of its global warming and climate change initiatives and the viability of solar power.

This was in 1993, long before most people were thinking seriously about climate change and green energy sources. I loved the concept and the challenge, and I jumped right in without hesitation. My first call was to Guy Charbonneau, to secure him and his excellent remote truck, Le Mobile. Guy was and still is a complete tech geek. He immediately understood the concept of green solar power and recognized the project’s value. He and his crew kept our wheels turning.

When Brant and I came on board, Greenpeace had already begun constructing a thirty-foot trailer called Cyrus, in which they installed a set of batteries sourced from one of the organization’s previous polar expeditions. These were very heavy, lead-acid batteries that weighed more than two hundred and fifty pounds each and stood over three feet high and two feet wide. There were two banks of twenty-five of them in total, one on each side of the trailer. Then they installed a solar array on the trailer’s roof to keep the batteries charged and added a power distribution system for charging them and feeding electricity to Le Mobile. We had to be sure to keep them topped off with distilled water so that they remained operational. The trailer was big and very heavy. It was towed by a big diesel rig that ran on vegetable oil.

Guy and his crew drove Le Mobile and Cyrus in convoy across the country, charging the batteries using the sun en route. Traveling from one venue to the next, while ensuring that we would have enough juice to record the show, was a constant challenge. There were a few close calls when a show would run overtime because of encores. Brant and I would follow Le Mobile and Cyrus by car or plane, focusing on producing and recording each show. When we were rolling, it felt like we were in combat, standing on the bridge of a U-boat, running deep on batteries, shoulder to shoulder into the fray.

I remember recording Soundgarden in Seattle and their self-important manager coming by after the show to pick up the master tapes. They wanted to review the show, select which song to use, and fix some vocals. The manager parked his fancy brand new Beemer next to the truck, left it running, bounded up the stairs and into the truck, and grabbed the masters, without even a ‘thank you.’

A minute later, he came back into the truck.

‘Where’s my car?’ he yelled.

I told him I had absolutely no idea. I’ll never forget the stunned look on his face when we discovered that some stranger had seen the car running, got in, and driven off with it. It was stolen from right under our noses.

Once we were off the road, we mixed the entire project at Le Mobile’s headquarters in Burbank. Looking back on our adventure, I realized that the musical performances could have been more varied. Some were great, others not so much. Just like our days in Rio, everything was a first take—there were no take twos. For me, there were a couple of real standout performances: R.E.M.’s rock-steady version of ‘Drive’ and U2’s ‘Until The End Of The World.’ The album also featured songs by James, Midnight Oil, The Disposable Heroes Of Hiphoprisy, Soundgarden (with Brian May), The Jesus & Mary Chain, UB40, Annie Lennox, PM Dawn, The Soup Dragons, Yothu Yindi, Sonic Youth, Boo-Yaa Tribe, and L7. And there I was, once again, standing on the cutting edge, producing an album entirely on solar power. That was not an easy task in 1993, but we achieved our goal. The effort took almost a year, and it was a major audio engineering and logistical tour de force. We were subsequently given Environmental Media Award for our efforts.

Chapter 22

Immersive audio (at last)

In 1996, I attended an industry conference at the University Of Southern California featuring the latest advances in audio for music and film. The theater was set up with projection and 5.1 theatrical audio. The ‘5.1’ refers to the number of audio tracks and the speaker placement: left, center, and right in the front; left and right behind; plus one subwoofer.

One of the sessions was led by Tomlinson ‘Tom’ Holman, a film theorist, audio engineer, and the inventor of Lucasfilm’s THX (Tomlinson Holman’s eXperiments) sound process for movie theaters. There was a full house. They played U2’s film Rattle And Hum in Dolby 5.1 digital, and it was an ear-opener. Here, at last, was what Malcolm and I were doing with quad monitoring while we were recording Stevie at the Record Plant in 1972. Now, over twenty years later, it was becoming a reality, accessible to anyone with the proper home audio system.

During the Q&A, I stood up and asked Tom plenty of questions. My motives were different from his. He was oriented toward supporting motion pictures with audio. I was more interested in developing the technology specifically for music. After the session, I was approached by David Del Grosso, one of the leading figures in the development and marketing of systems to replace analog with digital cinema sound.

‘If you think this sounds good,’ he said, ‘you should hear what we’re doing at DTS.’

I took him up on his offer and drove to his studio in nearby Camarillo for a visit. The next thing I knew, I was neck deep in my next adventure.

When Brant and I arrived at David’s, he introduced us to Terry Beard, the creator, audio visionary, and founder of Digital Theater Systems (DTS). Terry had come up with the idea of having timecode striped onto the film with a synchronization system to run a CD-ROM with the DTS 5.1 soundtrack on it. It was a very different approach to that of his main competitor, Dolby, who kept the soundtrack on the film itself.

Terry knew all about the work of the very brilliant brothers Stephen and Mike Smyth and their team, Paul Smith and Patrick Watson. The Smyths were initially based in Northern Ireland. Terry brought them and their team to DTS. They did the science and developed the process that allowed full bandwidth 5.1 discrete surround capability on a CD-ROM, which could then sync with a time code printed on the film. The idea was brilliant because you could store much more data on a CD-ROM than on film, and the more bits and bytesElevenLabs, the better the dynamic range and resolution. Not only that, but instead of changing costly film printsElevenLabs for foreign language versions, you could use the same film but substitute a foreign language CD-ROM.

DTS hit the big time when Terry got in touch with Steven Spielberg and played him a new remix he had done of Close Encounters Of The Third Kind. Spielberg loved it and championed DTS sound for his next picture, 1993’s Jurassic Park. By the time Brant and I arrived on the scene, DTS was already an enormous success. There were more than a thousand theaters equipped to play itElevenLabs, and it had become known for its stellar audio quality.

DTS stood pugnaciously nose-to-nose with Dolby. The DTS complex had offices, labs, a warehouse in the back stacked with decoders for the home theater market, and a production studio for setting up feature films with DTS CD-ROM soundtracks. They were hard at work with all the Universal Studios titles.

Brant and I hit it off with Terry and David, who wanted to talk to us about a record label they had created, DTS Entertainment, initially for the release of the Jurassic Park soundtrack, which was to be the first film score to be released in 5.1 surround sound. Now, 5.1 surround could come out of the recording studio’s control room and into the home. Finally, I would be able to create genuinely immersive mixes that were not front-loaded. Rather than a soundtrack intended to support a moving , this would allow the music to stand on its own.

Brant and I jumped on board without hesitation. The job had two parts. The first—and not so easy, as it turned out—was to find recording artists interested in remixing their current catalog into 5.1. The second was the new challenge of mixing those recordings into DTS 5.1 surround and delivering them on a Red Book CD. (The Red Book is the audio standard for compact discs that would work with the majority of CD players at the time). To seal the deal, DTS’s chairman, the avuncular and insightful Dan Slusser, had committed to the concept of a 5.1 music-only CD format, and he put the company’s money where its mouth was.

We made our deal and got to work. My first task was to find a studio, and it didn’t take long to discover the Enterprise Magnolia Boulevard in Burbank. It was the latest hot studio in town, and for good reason. Craig Hundley, the owner, had named the studio after the starship Enterprise from the original Star Trek TV series because, as a child actor, he had played Captain James Kirk’s nephew, Peter Kirk, on a few episodes of the show, and he remained a huge fan of the franchise.

We stopped by the studio and met the manager, Tom Brown, who offered us a cappuccino and a tour. The Enterprise was a fantastic place that lived up to its name. Studio M, up two flights of stairs in the attic, was an acoustical space built with intention. It was small and private and had its own lounge and bathroom. Its compactness, coincidentally, turned out to be an advantage for 5.1 nearfield, ear-level monitoring, as it was similar to how consumers would hear it at home. The pièces de résistance were a brand-new, fully automated Neve Capricorn recording and mixing console, five Genelec main monitors, and a pair of Genelec subwoofers.

Craig, it turned out, also owned an audio equipment rental company called Audio Affects. We had struck paydirt. With Audio Affects and an open budget from DTS, we had access to any outboard gear we desired. We were in audio engineering heaven.

‘The Capricorn was brand new,’ Brant recalls, ‘so nobody knew how to operate it. Neve sent us a babysitter: Morgan Martin. He gave us technical support and took notes for Neve about bugs that we discovered and found solutions for. As a matter of fact, there were two bugs named after me, Brant Bug One and Brant Bug Two. It had something to do with the auxiliary sends not coming back with data I had written. God help you if you went back more than one mix pass. It took forever!

‘I remember when we were mixing Seal’s “Prayer For The Dying” at 3am, our mix would not play back. It turned out that I had a habit of holding my finger on the fader before I made a mix move. The constant touch of my hand was being registered as automation data. Once our studio tech, Dan, saw this, we went through and cleared it all, and our mix became one one-hundredth the size.

‘I’ll never forget Morgan asking me what I wanted to automate. My response was, Everything at any time! Fucking groundbreaking is what that was.’

Brant’s encyclopedic brain recalls that we had a PCM 5000 processor, which was the precursor to the classic System 6000, plus a Publeson A, a couple of Eventide H3000s, four SPX90s, four SPX900s, the Lexicon 200 reverb for binaural plates, and an EMT 250 using all quad outputs, plus a couple of analog LA 2A and 1176 tube compressors and some Massenberg and Pultec EQs. We had to interface them all at the patch bay and provide 110 AC power for each one, so there were wires everywhere.

The really big deal was that having six channels of audio instead of two changed the rules on how we were mixing the music. For the next two years, Brant and I got to perfect the art of mixing music in 5.1 surround. We learned what we could and couldn’t do, technically and musically. Nothing stood in our way. The only limitation was finding artists and record companies willing to take a shot at it. Many of them were territorial about having other engineers, producers, or record companies monkeying around with their work. In many cases, it was more about politics than the music.

We had many curious folks trudging up the two flights of stairs, often uninvited, to see what we were up to. We were always happy to let them look over our shoulders. After the first few times, I was no longer surprised by listeners’ tearful, emotional reactions to the music. DTS heightened the music’s emotional impact. It wasn’t like the old days when only Malcolm and I could experience immersive sound. Now, the music and the end listener could occupy the same space. I could always tell, by looking at the faces of those experiencing it for the first time, that they were teetering on the edge of tears.

I remember suggesting, somewhat tongue-in-cheek, that we place a box on the wall with a glass front and a sign reading ‘In Case Of Emergency Break Glass.’ Instead of a fire extinguisher, the box would contain crying towels or Kleenex. I got a lot of pleasure from it, and I was smiling inside.

Some of the more memorable projects we remixed in 5.1 included Seal’s aforementioned ‘Prayer For The Dying,’ The Beatles’ ‘Yesterday,’ ‘On Bended Knee’ and ‘The Water Runs Dry’ by Boyz II Men, Marvin Gaye’s album Forever Yours, and the live concert album Pavarotti & Friends For War Child. Many of EMI Records’ classical titles, including the opera Aida, were beautifully recorded in quad. Brant and I successfully synthesized center and sub-channel and then mastered them in 5.1. Working on these selections brought me back to my classical music background.

And then what we hoped would be our crowning moment: Michael Jackson’s Thriller. DTS provided us with a digital multitrack master, and we did an incredible 5.1 mix on the Studer console at Expression Studio in Oakland. We were working on The Gluey Brothers’ album on location in San Francisco when DTS sent us the master tapes. Sadly, there were politics galore and turf battles going on around our remix, so I don’t think Michael ever got to hear it. His lawyer, John Branca, refused to grant DTS the right to use the masters, so our version never saw the light of day.

Since 1967, first in New York, then Chicago, and finally Las Vegas, the Consumer Electronics Show has been a showcase for the industry’s latest and greatest gear and innovations. The Vegas show that we attended with DTS in 1998 was no exception. The company pulled out all the stops. The big dollars were flying in every direction—the exhibit, staff, hotels, food, drink, and more.

Our plan was to create what you’d now call a very high-end home theater. The challenge was to build this inside a very large, reverberant, but acoustically isolated concrete box in a corner of the Las Vegas Convention Center’s main exhibition hall. The first thing I did was call my old friend, studio architect John Storyk. If anyone could pull this off, it would be the guy who designed Electric Lady, TONTO, and Studio B at the Record Plant. He got on board enthusiastically and came up with the goods.

We covered the walls with heavy, black theatrical drapes. hen, John designed six free-standing, twelve-foot high, book-hinged panels with RPG diffusers mounted on them to scatter the sound waves in multiple directions. This created a more even and natural sound field within the room. We covered the floor with thick carpeting. At the end of the room, the crew installed a cinema-grade screen. n the middle of the room, a truss with a top-end, high-definition projector mounted on it here was a large, over-the-top, audiophile 5.1 surround system with two subwoofers. It performed way beyond our expectations and was a beautiful platform for DTS to show its wares. Our ‘theater’ had a capacity of around twenty-five people, and there was never an empty chair.

I was tapped, by default, to lead two daily segments devoted to music mixed in 5.1 surround. I told stories of the recordings and shared theories about how our brains know where a sound was coming from. My stories were garnered from the years of hands-on recording and mixing music in surround, but it was clear that they had their start with mixing for Stevie and creating the sonic landscapes he envisioned in his mind.

I realized then that I was no longer just the man standing behind the curtain. I had moved centerstage. After that week in Vegas, DTS sent me out on the road to tell their story and share the world of surround sound. Company manager Lorr Kramer came with me to give me some logistical support.

Lorr and I remain friends to this day, but it was around this time, for a variety of reasons, that Brant and I felt our relationship with DTS start to unwind. Our first releases were just beginning to reach the street, and DTS was certainly making every effort to do the right thing. The company did a beautiful job supporting the studio production, the packaging, and what I saw as audiophile trade advertising. I was busy mixing and not privy to the marketing strategies, but I slowly realized that DTS wasn’t a record company and didn’t understand the game. I was purely focused on what was tumbling out of the loudspeakers and, once again, was very naïve about the business side of the music business.

I remember looking at the first published release schedule. All of the titles were mixed by Brant and me, and they were all intended to demonstrate the technology, of course, but DTS seemed to be focusing too much on the process, not the content. Radio promotion, for example, is a big part of any record company’s promotion strategy, but it wasn’t possible to play a DTS-encoded track on the radio. So, how do you sell a recording that you can’t hear unless you have a DTS home theater? The other big problem was that the marketing lacked direction. One minute it was Boyz II Men, the next breath it was classical music.

DTS’s customer base was primarily owners of expensive home theaters. They were mothers and fathers, and their taste in music was very different from their kids. The younger generation wasn’t interested in listening to music in their parents’ home theater. DTS wanted to bring as many different artists, producers, engineers, and established record labels to the table as possible. Still, there wasn’t a game plan for dealing with the major artists and their record companies. Napster was also looming on the horizon, inspiring fears of piracy of high-resolution music. Getting A-list record companies to invest in new, financially unproven technology was nearly impossible.

The heads of the company knew they were in trouble. Before long, there were significant changes at the top. The new management faces wanted to change direction, with an emphasis on big pop and R&B acts. I proposed a more conservative approach: a focus on recording original classical content like the Beethoven string quartets, live jazz, and small ensembles, with the actual performances recorded natively in 5.1 surround. I had just remastered a series of twenty-year-old quad EMI classical selections, which we recycled cost-effectively from the EMI library. I thought that this would fit well with our older, audiophile audience and then allow DTS to sign artists directly, own their original content, and grow slowly over time, licensing multichannel masters owned by major labels. This was not what upper management wanted to hear, however, and so, in the space of a few months, I found myself out of a job, on the outside looking in.

The efforts at DTS went on for a few more years. The label scored a few notable acts, but it never really caught on. Ultimately, DTS turned its attention away from selling and promoting music, and the label died a merciful death.

These days, we deliver immersive audio for everyone via apps that live on every smartphone. Like many great ideas, the DTS surround music project was simply ahead of its time. It was seen as a nuisance and an unnecessary expense to recording artists and record companies. They didn’t see its potential because there was no real commercial market for it.

Kids today still don’t listen to records in their parents’ home theaters, but subscription services like Apple Music and Spotify pay for the cost of the surround mixes in an effort to offer a superior listening experience, and everybody wins. I see a day coming very soon when most records will be specifically recorded and mixed in surround, spurring a new wave of creativity, as it did for us at DTS. Once again, as I have discovered throughout my career, technology drives the art. I am deeply grateful to have lived long enough to see and hear it unfold.

Meanwhile, if recording albums in DTS 5.1 was ahead of its time, releasing motion pictures in 5.1 on DVD and Blu-ray was right on time. It would provide an opportunity for me to start a successful business that would endure for many years. Finally, I was going to become an entrepreneur—this time, for real.
