Chapter 23

Mi Casa: bringing surround home

It was the summer of 1999, and with the DTS project behind me, I moved back from Burbank with my housemate Brookie’s help and into my familiar old digs on Fuller Avenue. The landlord remembered me, so I didn’t have to go through the rigamarole of applying for a new lease. Instead, I just picked up where I had left off. The building was right at the gate to Runyon Canyon Park, and mine was a two-bedroom unit on the second floor, with a huge living/dining room that faced the park. It was the perfect location for me and my newly acquired dog, a Vizsla puppy I named Hannah.

I was once again working freelance—or, more accurately, I was unemployed, letting my fingers do the walking through my Rolodex, looking for my next gig. I was doing some serious thinking about what my next hill to climb would be. As part of that, I was listening to as many feature films as I could that had been released in 5.1 on DVD, the place where surround audio had finally found a home. I found myself in the crosshairs of a revolution in the sonic side of music and film production.

At the time, the problem with the DVD format was that the audio transfers of theatrical soundtracks sounded horrendous. For theaters, companies used an equalization standard called the X-curve that was designed to suit large audiences in movie house auditoriums. They thought that the X-curve would work in the newly developing home theater spaces. The tagline was ‘Bring the movie theater experience into your home.’ The only problem was that it didn’t work. The manufacturers of home-theater equipment tried adding a button called ‘Cinema RE-EQ’—a sort of one-button-fix-all solution that they thought would solve the X-curve issues, but it only added to the misery and confusion.

Humans experience sound within a 360-degree bubble. Audio need not always be in front of us to tell us something, and sometimes it’s more powerful when it isn’t. With immersive audio, we had opportunity to get home video audiences to suspend their belief in reality and live, sonically, inside the motion picture. I knew that a well-balanced, fully immersive mix would make any motion-picture soundtrack more detailed, personal, and emotional and could emulate the stellar quality of the immersive music-only mixing that Brant and I did at DTS.

With that in mind, I had the idea to build an audio postproduction studio that would be balanced for home theaters, not big movie theaters. Our sole purpose would be to restore, remix, and remaster motion-picture soundtracks for the home. We would later brand our approach ‘nearfield mixing’ (I believe we were the first to use this term), and it would catch on in a big way.

Brant and I set out to build one of the first (if not the first) fully digital home-theater audio postproduction studio in the living room of my apartment. It was literally in my house, hence the name of the company, Mi Casa Multimedia (as in, ‘mi casa es su casa’). My intuition and timing were perfect. We were at the beginning of the digital revolution in audio, and just starting to reap its benefits, when the company was born.

My living room was very large for a nondescript LA apartment and took up the entire corner of the building. I got the master bedroom and bath, Brookie got the small guest room off the hallway by the front door. The bedrooms acted as buffers around the living room and isolated us from the next-door neighbor’s apartment. It was perfect, or so I thought.

Brookie oversaw furnishing and decorating the place, just Bobby Anderson didElevenLabs back in the days of EdieElevenLabs and Ciao! He was a denizen of all the antique and used-furniture stores along Magnolia Boulevard, around the corner from the house on Lamer Street. He knew all the shopkeepers, and he was always dragging in something—a lamp, an easy chair, a kitchen table, mirrors, pots and pans, everything you imagine—for the house. In his former days, Brookie had been a window designer and set dresser for several upscale 5th Avenue department stores in New York. Everything he brought home was well-chosen, giving the house a warm, eclectic, bohemian feeling.

I was still well-known and had a solid reputation in industry circles, so when I started calling around looking for equipment, manufacturers and vendors were eager to offer me their ‘special deals.’ In exchange for my endorsement, they would give me excellent credit, huge discounts, and access to B-stock items—models that were a year old or had minor cosmetic scratches or imperfections. Thse deals were welcome because I didn’t have a lot of money at that point, just an idea and a plan.

When all the studio gear arrived, we set it up in the middle of the living room. We oriented the living room so that the kitchen and dining alcove that I’d turned into my office were at the back. There were large windows facing the park on the left. It was very quiet.

Brant and I had been working together since 1987, and for the last two years, behind the digital recording console at the Enterprise, we’d been mixing music in 5.1 for DTS. Brant’s hands were all over the latest digital technology, so he focused on setting up and integrating our gear. He became the chief engineer. Once the studio was set up, we used the DTS 5.1 mixes we did at Enterprise as benchmarks to help calibrate and measure our new room’s performance. The results were beyond our expectations.

My first call was to my old friend and former assistant from my Record Plant days, Peter Chaikin.He was now running the pro audio department at Yamaha. I told him what I was up to and asked, ‘Pierre, am I doing the right thing?’

‘Moishe,’ he replied, using my old nickname from the East Village days, ‘do it, and the clients will come.’ How right he wasElevenLabs.

Pierre talked me into buying a Yamaha 02R digital recording console with extra cards, bells, and whistles. He offered it to me below cost and with extended credit—yet another friends-and-family deal. We got five B-stock LSR speakers directly from JBL that had switches on the rear panel to allow room tuning adjustments. We set them up on ear-level stands. The subwoofer we ended up with was a JBL 4645 with an eighteen-inch speaker and a Badini amplifier to power it. The speakers and the placement were pretty much the same as what Brant and I had been mixing on at the Enterprise.

SADiE was a British company making software for digital multitrack recording, editing, mixing, and mastering. They were eager to get in the door of the lucrative LA recording scene. They knew of my reputation and were keen on us having a SADiE setup, so their rep brought a system to the apartment, left it with us to check out, and remained attentive to our every question and need.

Our first SADiE system was a 24/96 model, not unlike the Pro Tools digital audio workstations that were also just coming to market. What separated SADiE from everybody else was the third-party plug-in suite they offered from another British company, CEDAR. They had a set of digital tools called ReTouch and DeNoise, plus a few others, that could either localize unwanted sounds accurately in both time and frequency, or isolate broadband noise like tape hiss and hum. With CEDAR, we could remove these unwanted acoustic events, replace them with audio derived from the surrounding signal, and leave all the remaining good audio untouched.

The SADiE was powerful and became the heart of fully digital recording studio that had taken shape in my living room. Some of our processing gear was of the old-fashioned analog variety, some was digital. In those days, a studio was more like a prop plane assembled piecemeal. Every reverberator, equalizer, or compressor was a separate physical piece of equipment with a particular function. Each had its own AC power supply and patch points, which Brant organized in a patch bay. We had two Tascam DA98 eight-track digital tape recorders running eight-millimeter cassettes, a Sony Digibeta video deck that we used to input and synchronize the movies from the outside world and output our finished product, a TC Electronic 6000 quad digital reverberator, and a bunch of limiters, compressors, and equalizers. Without all this emerging digital technology, Mi Casa would never have been possible.

Another significant advantage was that we were a postproduction studio, not a recording studio, so we did not need multiple microphone inputs and an acoustic studio. We could record one or two tracks of vocals or an instrument with microphones if we used headphones.

We were all set up, ready to go, and I was confident in our capabilities. I began knocking on the doors of movie companies that were busily transferring their feature films from the big screen to the small screen. Fortunately, one of my first contacts was with New Line Cinema, a scrappy independent production and distribution company.

I managed to book a call with Evan Edalist, New Line’s digital guru, who told us they had an extensive library of feature films that had already been released theatrically. hey were now in the process of reauthoring them on DVD and spending a lot of time and money to create the finest quality high-definition transfers possible. Evan had a vision to future-proof the releases. Although DVDs, at the time, could only reproduce standard-resolution pictures, he sensed that it was only a matter of time before the technology would evolve to the next level.

New Line was not a major, old-school movie studio like Paramount or Warner Bros, where the big boys had multiple layers of management, unions, and departments for everything. This was the opposite. With just a couple of small offices in New York and LA, they were one of the most iconoclastic and innovative of all the independent film production companies. Their stock in trade was low-budget features, partly because they couldn’t afford big-name talent. They focused on hiring the new, bright, and upcoming writers and directors. They were just finding their feet, which turned out to be good for us because we were also new, bright, and driven, and totally unknown in the postproduction film community. They farmed out their postproduction work to indie vendors around town. There were just a few decision-makers, everybody knew everybody, and there was no red tape to contend with. It was like a big family, which proved to be a huge benefit for us.

Not long after the call with Evan, I found myself on my way to his office. They had a few floors of offices in a building on the fringe of Boys Town in West Hollywood. I showed up with my friend Dan Kavanaugh, an accomplished television producer who was also in the business of making TV commercials and DVD content for some of the same studio clients as Mi Casa. He was way more familiar with film production than I was, and I felt I needed to cover my ass because I knew very little about the film part. My concerns were unfounded. There was a lot of intense conversation, stories, laughing, and good cheer. We realized that we were all on the same page.

Evan explained that after spending so much time and money on creating the movie, something more had to be done with the soundtracks. They weren’t doing the pictures justice. He said they didn’t have any deep insight into the audio mixing or mastering process. They were experts in film postproduction, but not so much in audio. He wanted me to help solve this puzzle. I left the meeting excited and inspired.

With the enormous success of DVDs, my premise of remixing specifically for home theater was an idea whose timing was perfect. A few days later, we got a visit from Evans’s left-hand man, Jesse Torres, who came by the apartment to see what we were up to for himself. I could only imagine what he was thinking as he walked across the courtyard pool deck the back of the building and up the one flight of stairs to my place. I’m sure that it wasn’t what he expected. We were the antithesis of a well-established, high-end audio postproduction house. rankly, we didn’t know what to expect either.

Thankfully, Jesse went with the flow and sat down in the sweet spot behind the console. Brookie, wearing a long, loose-fitting kaftan, floated by with a cup of coffee and some rugelach pastries. Then Hannah, my beautiful Vizsla puppy, came by to say hello, and she put us all at ease. We sat behind Jesse and started playing some of the 5.1 music-only mixes and videos we had done for DTS. It only took a few minutes for Jesse to see our potential. He called Evan, who gave us the go-ahead for a test.

Later that same day, Jesse called and announced that he was sending over our test project. It was a film called Pleasantville—a highly optimistic story about social change and personal growth from conformism and repression transitioning into color. The black-and-white TV picture and the sound of the audio from the 1950s gave way to the diversity and self-expression of color TV and the audio of the late 60s. It presented a real sonic challenge.

The next day, a messenger arrived with the DigiBeta version of the film and its DA98 5.1 audio, which was a combined music-and-effects print master that had been prepared for exhibition in movie houses. The audio was on separate tracks, one for each of the speakers the track would be played through: left, center, right, left surround, and right surround on tracks one through five, a low-frequency subwoofer on track six, all the dialog on track seven, and a blank track on eight to add anything new. We transferred all of it onto the SADiE, locked the audio to the picture, and got to work.

Having the separated dialog, music, and effects, we were able to remove all the residual tape hiss and other noise induced by the 35mm magnetic films. We removed all the thumps, clicks, and pops, re-equalized the mix, and level matched as needed with SADiE’s CEDAR DeNoise and ReTouch. Then we rebalanced the internal dialog levels from one character’s voice to the next and spread the dialog out into the front channels, bringing it forward in the sound field.

We did the same cleanup to the music and effects and moved them from the front of the mix out and into the room. Next, we created a new subwoofer track that included the music tracks that were not included in the original sub-track. The soundtrack became very present, occupying the same space as the listener. It became immersive, a more emotional, detailed, and satisfying experience. We were remixing and mastering the soundtrack, much as we had done in 5.1 with music recordings. The end result was definitely not an imitation of a movie house but rather something different, a new way to experience the film.

Our firstborn with New Line knocked it out of the park. The studio folks loved the results of our work on Pleasantville. Evan gave us the green light, our wheels started turning, and we became a part of the New Line family. The projects started to flow. It was the beginning of a relationship that endured for more than ten years.

Jesse began spending more and more time with us, trudging across the pool deck and up the sleazy back stairwell to my second-floor apartment. He was into every detail of what we were up to and kept us coordinated with the directors, the creatives, and the rest of the New Line folk. Everything we did had to pass muster with him first. Our relationship turned into a friendship that continues to this day.

Financially, we had been running on fumes, so I was pleased to finally put some money in the bank. A whole string of New Line catalog titles like House Party and House Party 2, The Corruptor, and several more—too many to remember—showed up right away. There were some major works and some notable highlights. Total Eclipse was about a homoerotic love triangle and the life and loves of the tormented French poet Rimbaud. I think I identified with the story because of my own sexuality. The movie featured a brilliant eighteen-year-old heartthrob named Leonardo DiCaprio. I thought it was a brave film for Leonardo to make.

Total Eclipse was the first movie we upgraded from a basic stereo mix. We fed the stereo stems through our Dolby decoder and got a surround mix back. We printed the decoded stem onto our SADiE system, allowing us to treat and manipulate the dialog, music, and effects separately and giving us control over where we placed the elements in the 5.1 nearfield space.

One day, Jesse arrived and asked suspiciously, ‘You guys know how to mix, right?’ With that, he gave us the movie Se7en. This was to be our first movie mix from fully separated stems of music, dialogue, and effects—an infinitely more complex and creative assignment.

The day before the film’s director, David Fincher, arrived to check out the finished version, Jesse told us, ‘Okay, everything sounds great, but you guys have to get a bigger television. I cant ask David Fincher to watch his movie on a thirteen-inch screen.’

I rented a twenty-eight-inch high-definition monitor from Audio Affects, and Fincher showed up with his sound designer and sound editor, Ren Klyce. They were very no-nonsense, all business. As they watched and listened, Brant and I hovered back in the kitchen because we didn’t want them to feel that we were staring at them for the slightest reaction to what we had done. Which, of course, we were.

When part one finished and Brant inserted part two into the DigiBeta machine, you could’ve cut the tension with a knife. They were so serious. They gave us no indication whether they loved it or hated it. When the movie finally finished, I stepped forward and sheepishly asked, ‘Well?’

There was a pregnant pause, and then Fincher spoke.

‘I’m sorry, I was focused on something else. It’s fantastic!’

Brant and I breathed a huge sigh of relief.

Fincher did have some suggestions. He had been taking notes the whole time, and Brant and I sat down at the console with him and Wren and addressed his one at a time. We discovered that our process allowed directors like David, who cared about their work, to perfect their films. Usually, they would be rushed through the theatrical mix on the sound stage, which was complex and very expensive. Working with us gave them the time and freedom to focus on the sound in much greater depth. With our nearfield monitoring approach, they could hear every detail and be hands-on with one last loving embrace to perfect and finish their film. Fincher took full advantage of it.

The star-studded art movie Magnolia was the second feature film written and directed by the gifted auteur Paul Thomas Anderson, who was just coming off his breakout hit, Boogie Nights. As was often the case, the elements were delivered to us in a shopping bag with no direction. We were becoming trusted to do what was needed to create a perfect surround experience at home. As we went to work on Magnolia, however, we found there were several sequences that sounded wrong. In the scene where it starts raining live frogs, all the hits as the frogs landed sounded distorted. So, we did our best to clean and rebalance the soundtrack.

When Anderson came to screen our mix, the first thing he said was, ‘What happened to the sound of the frogs?’ Apparently, he had intended to have noise and distortion in his sound design, which was a part of the story’s fabric, and we had removed most of it. Clearly, he hated what we had done. He stood up and told us he had a meeting at his office and would be back later.

We waited and waited until finally, we called Jesse, who told us that Anderson had left for New York hours earlier to see his then-girlfriend, Fiona Apple, perform at Madison Square Garden. We never saw Anderson again, and we never got to finish Magnolia.

While we were working on Final Destination, the film’s director, James Wong, came in to see the movie and make his final adjustments to the audio. At the beginning of the film, there’s a huge onscreen explosion as an airliner blows up in the sky. It was deafening, and it shook the room. It was at that moment that my upstairs neighbor, who had been more or less tolerant of all our loud sounds for the past few months, finally had enough, and he snapped. I guess he must’ve been a bowler, because he repeatedly hurled what sounded like a bowling ball on the floor of his living room. All the pictures on our walls and the dishes in the kitchen were rattling. I ran upstairs in a panic and offered him a hundred dollars to stop.

Despite this, Wong loved what we did with his movie. As he was on the way out of the door, though, he turned to us with a suggestion.

‘Maybe you guys should find a different place to work.’

How right he wasElevenLabs. That’s precisely what we did.

We were getting busier and busier. New Line would regularly deliver their titles by messenger, without even announcing that they were sending something our way. We were overwhelmed, and we needed to grow. Brant would spend eight to ten hours a day working in the living room. In the early days, we had always been in someone else’s recording studio, but now that we were working in such close proximity, seven days a week, Brookie and Brant started getting on each other’s nerves. Tensions mounted, and with our move to a different location in the wind, Brant delivered an ultimatum: him or Brookie. With great reluctance, I had to ask Brookie to move on. He headed home to Rhode Island and settled into a new life. Although we spoke on the phone several times afterward, I never saw him again. He passed away from pneumonia a few years later.

I got on the phone and started calling friends and realtors in search of a new landing pad. I wasn’t finding anything that struck my fancy. Instead, I saw tacky tract houses from the 60s, built with drywall, in the sunbaked San Fernando Valley. It was entirely by chance when, one evening, I went out to take Hannah for a walk and found the house of my dreams, just two blocks away from my apartment on Fuller, at the edge of old Hollywood subdivision called Outpost Estates.

The house was on the corner of Hillside Avenue and El Cerrito and had been designed and built in the Mediterranean style of the 1930s. There was a ‘For Rent’ sign in the front yard, and the landlord, Olga, was in the house, prepping the place so she could rent it out again. She told me that the past tenant was a cellist with the LA Philharmonic who used to teach his students in the living room. She warmed up to me when I told her who I was and what I was up to, so she gave Hannah and me a tour.

The house had the build quality you would expect from high-end home built in Bel Air and had a wonderful patina of age and permanence. It sat on a corner lot, keeping it private and distant from the neighbors. I could visualize building three studios without disturbing the aesthetic of the place—or the neighbors. The house had thick plaster walls (ideal for sound isolation), marvelous 1930s woodwork, lighting fixtures, and hardware—all the things I was looking for. And it was close to our Hollywood clients. The serendipity of it all was that it was right under my nose all the time, practically next door to me. It was love at first sight. I leased the place on the spot.

It was rumored that the house was the former Hollywood home of 1930s film star Bela Lugosi. That story only added to the sense of history and panache. A few days later, we moved our gear into what had been the dining room. We put my old oriental rug on the floor and got to work. I set up my belongings in the former master bedroom and bathroom on the second floor. I was in heaven.

And so, the next twelve years of my life began. It took nearly two years and more than a hundred thousand dollars out of our now abundant cash flow to painstakingly restore and retrofit the old Mediterranean-style home, one studio at a time. My first call was to my old friend, the architect John Storyk. John went to work designing the studio specifics. It was like we were touched by the DNA of our past. Mi Casa was the grandchild of Electric Lady, TONTO, and the Record Plant. The house’s ambiance blended beautifully with the acoustics we needed. We aimed to create three discrete and distinctly contemporary living-room listening experiences, each designed to emulate a home theater environment. We turned the living room, dining room, and bedroom on the second floor into top-notch audio post-mixing rooms. Each one had its own unique monitoring system, and we ensured that our finished mixes worked in all three. The living room and dining room on the ground floor became studios A and B. My master bedroom, bathroom, business office, Studio C, the guest room, and the tape library were all on the second floor.

Turning a home from the 1930s into a fully functioning postproduction audio studio while remaining sensitive to the original architecture was a challenge. A few days after we moved in, Brant was already at work in our jury-rigged studio in the former dining room, and I was standing at the front door with my dog Hannah at my side, when a couple walked up the path and introduced themselves as my next-door neighbors. Their house was across the patio and pool, behind high ivy-covered fence.

We had a polite conversation. They told me that they were in the motion-picture business. I said that we were as well, and that I was building a studio to do audio postproduction work. Since they were in the business, I thought they would appreciate what I was up to. A day or two later, a legal document was slipped under the front door saying that I was breaking zoning laws and I should cease and desist immediately. You could have knocked me over with a feather. I’m sure they expected us to be making all kinds of noise with rock’n’roll musicians coming and going at all hours of the day and night. Of course, that wasn’t at all what we had in mind.

A few days later, after checking on the law, I found a big loophole. I informed the neighbors that I had a ‘home studio’ for my use and not a commercial recording studio. There was nothing they could about that. Home studios were permitted under the zoning laws. At the time, technology was making it possible for just about any recording artist who wanted one to have one. All the commercial studios in town were up in arms because it was costing them dearly in business. It was a close call for us, but we managed to squeak by.

We found a sensitive and sympathetic contractor, Tony Clearwater, who had a lot of experience building recording studios. He had a crew of three: a cabinet maker/artisan named Cliffy, plus Javier and Juarez, who we called Senior and Junior, a hard-working Latino father-and-son team who did all the heavy lifting. I can’t remember how we met Tony, but he proved to be exactly what we needed. He was a musician himself, he really understood what we wanted to do, and he had the skills to make it happen. Over twenty years later, we are still close friends, and I am the godfather of his little girl, Tia.

Our first steps were invisible but essential. We double-glazed all the windows and the French doors that opened onto the patio and pool. Then we installed a ten-ton air-conditioning unit, which we tied into the old forced-air heating system. We replaced the old furnace with an air handler and mounted the compressor on an isolated pad in the backyard. Fortunately for us, I knew an air conditioning man, Adolf, from my days building out the Record Plant, and he extended us credit for the equipment and the labor.

Next, we hired an electrical contractor who installed an isolated and balanced power system that required a three-hundred-pound transformer, which we bolted to the wall in the basement. We needed new breakers, wiring, and outlets to support all the computers and recording gear with clean electricity throughout the house. It was a major undertaking.

From there, we built a massive soundproof wall and a door at the back of Studio A. We did a mirrored version of the wall and door in Studio B, which created a hallway that both studios opened nto and acted as a buffer between studios. We went out of our way to match all the new doors and woodwork with the original designs and finishes in the house.

Conceiving, designing, and installing all the wiring was in Brant’s hands. Everything we wanted to integrate had to be hardwired. There was no wireless Bluetooth technology. It was intricate, time-consuming, and expensive. But once it was completed, it was invisible.

Tony found a little person, well under five feet tall but very thin and lithe, who could crawl into all the voids under the house. The longest run was to the front of Studio A. Another ran under the kitchen and dining room, snaking up inside the back wall of the house to get to Studio C. Studio B was the easy one, as it was next door to the machine room. We ran the wiring, including the computer monitor, keyboard, mouse, USB extenders, and audio tie lines, inside a four-inch-diameter plastic PVC pipe. We plugged the ends with steel wool to keep the mice and other little critters from making a meal of the wiring.

Finally, we filled all the walls and ceilings with very dense blown-in insulation. When we finished, Mi Casa was sonically isolated, with no sound leaking out from the studios or outside sounds leaking in. The whole place felt solid, and walking around the house, it looked like we hadn’t done anything at all, which was precisely our intention.

Brant and I were on the same page with what we wanted Mi Casa to be. After all, we had been working together for more than ten years, becoming experts in surround audio. Our success was due to our nearfield mixing techniques. The speakers were mounted very close at ear level and not more than ten feet in front of the listener. It had only recently become the state of the art for home video, but the emotional power of immersive audio was nothing new to me.

We were getting busier and busier, thankfully. The cash started to flow in, and with some creative financing, I was able to keep the construction going. For nearly the first two years, construction co-existed with production. The running question was, ‘What will the studio look like when it’s finished?’

People asked why I was investing so much in a rental property. Considering our cash flow, I didn’t have to pay any interest to lease or finance anything, so, in the long run, it was the most cost-efficient solution. And I was grateful to the friends who helped us get started. My evolving partnership with Brant worked because we had developed very different missions and jobs. My overview was strategic: running the studios, business operations, PR, clients, sales, and general business. I was fighting the war. Brant’s overview was tactical: overseeing all things technical and fighting battles one film at a time.

I found myself spending less and less time with my hands on the console. I missed the real-time analog process, with all things happening at the same time under my hands, like a live performance. I found engineering digital audio and computers tedious and not my cup of tea, although the results were stunning. Instead, I settled in, building my Mi Casa Empire.

Our work at Mi Casa could be described as ‘forensic audio.’ We would clean all the audio tracks and put them back together, fully optimized and ready to be remixed for nearfield full-frequency home theater surround sound.

In short order, we became overwhelmed with work. Not only was New Line keeping us busy, but now Artisan Entertainment, Lionsgate, and MGM were booking us for projects. Our studios were working day and night. We slowly hired a full-time staff, supplemented by a revolving cast of characters who would work for various lengths of time. There was always someone staying in the spare bedroom. Throw in a few dogs—by this time, Brant’s two mutts, Jake and Pickles, had joined Hannah and had the run of the place—and Mi Casa was an ongoing hive of production that never closed.

We always kept the kitchen stocked with deli food, sliced roast beef, turkey, coleslaw, potato salad, salty black olives, and occasionally, more esoteric things like a couple of my favorites: pickled herring and cold borscht. There was a second fridge in the pantry with a full selection of ice-cold soft drinks. I knew that people always worked at their best on a full stomach, so everybody was welcome to help themselves. I was always ready to order in a meal or throw a few steaks or beef ribs on the barbecue. Then, wed all sit around the dining table, share a snack, lunch, or dinner, and talk shop. It was Mi Casa, after all, and I wanted every to feel fed, relaxed, and at home. On the business side, having a fully stocked kitchen limited long offsite lunches and drinking away from the studio. It kept everybody focused on their work—and, considering what we billed our clients, it was worth the expense.

Brant and I were more than happy to take our clients out for a great, fully deductible Japanese dinner in Beverly Hills or, on occasion, a ski weekend at Big Bear or Mammoth Mountain. We were a big and very happy family.

There was a private, heated pool in the backyard. Our clients rarely used it, but they loved sitting on the patio and eating at the table under the umbrella. Hannah was the pool’s primary user. Occasionally, on the rare Sunday when we were closed and I was alone, I would bask in the nude by the pool, smoking a joint or sharing intimacy with a friend. It reminded me of the antics in the jacuzzi at the Record Plant from back in the day.

I joined a gym and committed to getting in shape. I was working out like a madman with a trainer, and soon I was in fighting shape at a slim one hundred and seventy-five pounds. I was using black market HGH (human growth hormone) and testosterone. Before long, I was a muscular gym junkie with a washboard stomach. I felt like I was the king of Hollywood.

I was Mi Casa’s only full-time resident, but I was living over the company store, so I was rarely alone. My bedroom and office were just down the hall from each other. I was living my dream and loving it. We were more than just a thriving, cutting-edge business. We were one big happy family, and we stayed that way for over ten years. Our clients loved it too because there was always somebody around if there was an emergency of some kind.

Although we had many engineers and workers come and go over the years, there are a few who still burn bright in my memory. Around the time we started building Studio A, between working on movies and studio construction, Brant was reaching overload mode. Out of the blue, there was a knock on front door. There, unannounced, was Holger Thiele. Holger was a recording engineer who had recently arrived from Germany and was intent on living the American Dream. His dream had three requirements: an apartment on the Venice boardwalk facing the ocean, a big American car (an Oldsmobile 88), and a really cool job in music and film. When Brant heard about the cool job part of his story, he promptly responded, ‘Well, Holger, two out of three ain’t bad.’

It must have been fate, because Holger arrived at exactly when Brant needed a right-hand man. He was a first-class recording engineer, very disciplined, detail-oriented, educated in the German style, and, like Brant and me and Tony Clearwater, a musician. He soon became our senior engineer.

Holger introduced us to advanced German audio gear like the Junger multichannel limiter/compressor audio processor and Guillotine loudspeakers. He was the only one who could read their manuals. He remained with us for Mi Casa’s entire run. I don’t know if he ended up with that Oldsmobile, but about a year after joining us, Holger bought a very esoteric Moto Guzzi motorcycle. I knew when he was in the studio because he always left his helmet and leathers on the dining room table.

I met John Bird in 1990 or ’91. He was a childhood best friend and bandmate of Brant’s. Brant was helping him rebuild his home studio in the former wine cellar of his mom’s sprawling Mediterranean ranch-style home on Colfax in North Hollywood. The studio had its own entrance that opened nto a large yard with an abandoned tennis court and a wood pile. I would stop by with Brant to check their progress and provide some consultation, and John and I became pals. Besides our common interest in the recording world, we shared a mutual sense of humor and cultural, political, and aesthetic sensibilities. It was obvious that we were cut from the same cloth.

John was an excellent keyboard player who knew his way around a recording studio, so inviting him to join the Mi Casa family was a natural call. ater spent time together in Studio A working on the recording and mixing my first big film production, Tall Ships: The Privateer Lynx, with composer David James Nielsen. John was around for the full ride too, as well as some projects after Mi Casa’s demise, and we remain good friends to this day.

I met Cyril Palacios in the late 90s in Hollywood, around the time that I moved back into the apartment on Fuller Avenue. He had emigrated from Belize. His roots were Guatemalan, Spanish, and Mayan. He was black, tall, and, like me, a gay man. His dad was a star track-and-field athlete who had competed at the Olympics in Mexico City. Cyril must have inherited his father’s genes because he was a natural athlete. He walked for miles all over Los Angeles without a second thought. When we met, he was the synthesizer programmer/player in an electronica-based band I was producing called Fear No Art. It wasn’t long before we became friends. Along with the band’s founder, Jayson Jay, I became his mentor—a relationship that continues to this very day.

Around the time we moved to El Cerrito, we hired Cyril to work at Mi Casa full-time, replacing Brookie as Mi Casa’s majordomo. A computer-literate musician, he looked after the house’s daily operations, all the incoming and outgoing media, the media library, and the growing work-order system, ensuring that the audio postproduction process was running as smoothly as possible. Cyril was driven. While working at Mi Casa, he started taking UCLA extension courses and got straight As. In 2017, with Tony and me sponsoring him, he became a naturalized American citizen.

Cyril shared our love of animals, especially Hannah. With his genetic affinity for walking everywhere, he soon became Mi Casa’s official dog walker. Hannah spent a lot of time with him, and she became the queen of the Runyon Canyon dog park. Years later, when Hannah got sick with cancer, we helped her over the Rainbow Bridge together. She passed away in my bedroom, peacefully, in my arms. She was twelve years old. Cyril and I spread her ashes at her favorite spot in Runyon Canyon Park.

With Brant and our team of upstart engineers, we delivered surround mixes for more than one hundred movies. The list of titles we worked on was nothing short of incredible. We were just a few guys in a house who got to put our hands on some of the greatest films ever made. We were the final stop for the audio. It was truly magical time and place, and at the same time, insane.

Directors like Guillermo del Toro, Brett Ratner, and many others came to work with us in person at Mi Casa, knowing that we would treat their projects with audiophile-level attention. As I look back on the list, it’s still amazing to me: The Lord Of The Rings, Pan’s Labyrinth, My Left Foot, The Sound Of Music, 5.1 remixes of six early James Bond movies, Elf, Austin Powers, Three Faces Of Eve, The Ghost & Mrs. Muir, The Wedding Singer, Wedding Crashers, Hairspray, Jackie Brown, X-Men, An Affair To Remember, the DVD release of Twin Peaks, and so many more. We worked hard to perfect their soundtracks for the home-video market, which was quickly becoming an essential source of revenue for many films. We never sacrificed quality for expediency.

Near the end of 2010, on a sunny Saturday afternoon, I was out for a sail with a few friends in Long Beach Harbor on a small sloop. I spied a beautiful tall ship with raked masts. It looked to be over hundred feet long. She was tied up near the Queen Mary. Sailing close, I could see that she was called the Lynx. The sight of her mesmerized me. I felt that old familiar feeling, and I knew that I had stumbled upon my next adventure.

Chapter 24

There are those timeworn sayings: Oh, you look just like your father, or Your talent for playing the piano runs in the family. It’s in your genes. Those inherited traits also live in our ancestral memories that are somehow encoded into our genetic makeup. Scientists call it epigenetics, the study of heritable traits. If there’s merit to the studies, and I believe there is, then the sea is truly in my blood.

When I saw the Lynx, I remembered seeing a ship like her again and again in my childhood dreams. I later learned that my great-grandfather on my father’s side was a sea captain. Was I having an epigenetically imprinted memory of him and his life? I felt compelled to learn more about the Lynx’s story, and when I did, once again, my life and work made a radical course change.

That day in Long Beach Harbor, I sailed my sloop alongside the Lynx, hailed the skipper, Captain Craig Chipman, and soon found myself aboard a modern-day recreation of a sailing ship from two hundred years ago. After an afternoon sail, I was convinced that her story would make a great documentary film. My lifelong passions for sailing and cinematography were colliding with my concerns about ocean pollution and global warming. In the hopes that the lessons from our past could teach us how to save our planet for the future, I returned to filmmaking.

During the War of 1812, the federal government authorized the creation of a privateer navy—pretty much the only navy our young country had to defend itself from the Brits. These were privately owned ships that engaged in military battles. Most of these vessels, like the original Lynx, were fast, sharp-built Baltimore , built in the shipyards around Chesapeake Bay. Many wealthy landowners and businessmen, including Benjamin Franklin, invested in their construction. It was seen as a patriotic act, and it was also an excellent business.

The privateers attacked, seized, and destroyed many British merchant ships and brought home prize ships and goods that were sold for millions of dollars. The conquering ships also helped themselves to all the latest navigational aids, like compasses, sextants, and the latest charts from captured ships, which went a long way to equip new privateers in the fleet. The proceeds from the sale of the seized goods were then divided between the ship owners, the investors, the crew, and the government.

For me, it was the ship’s architecture and not so much her role as a war machine that fascinated me. Ships like the Lynx were marvels of the latest thinking in maritime design. They were lightly armed, built to sail fast, and highly maneuverable. They represented the very best in what a sailing vessel could be.

The British captured the original Lynx after her first voyage. They carefully documented her construction because she could sail the pants off any British ship of the time. The Baltimore Clippers were considered the thoroughbreds of the fore-and-aft rigged ships of the period.

This new Lynx that I stumbled across in Long Beach Harbor was built from the original plans the British Navy created while fastidiously documenting the ship’s design so they might build such ships themselves in 1812. Their blueprints were still in the archive of the British Admiralty and were the only existing and accurate copies of her design. It was the Brits’ documentation that was used to create a viable set of plans for the modern Lynx. o, in the end, you might say the Brits saved the day.

Today’s Lynx was designed by the noted naval architect Melbourne Smith, who had to adapt the original plans to meet current regulations. The project started in 1997 when noted philanthropist, sailor, and aviation enthusiast Woodson K. Woods set out to build a living museum that could bring American history to life through actual sailing on a real wooden sailing ship. The project cost more than $2.5 million. She was christened on July 28, 2001, in Rockport, Maine.

This Lynx was not an exact replica but a modern interpretation of the original design. Her construction and operation were supported by financial arrangements with the various school districts along the California coast to provide sailing experience and history to their students, with daily classes aboard public sailings on the weekends and week-long history and seamanship programs at sea during the summertime.

I fell in love with the Lynx’s story and offered to produce a documentary film about her. I made a deal whereby the Lynx Foundation would give us unfettered access to the ship at no charge, and, in return, they would have the right to use the film for their own purposes. I would supply the means of production, cameras, crew, editing, and original music. Mi Casa would own the show, and, in my unbridled optimism, I thought I could recoup costs and make a profit through film sales and distribution.

I was a lifelong sailor, environmentalist, and lover of the sea. It began in my youth, sailing on my brother’s sailboat, the Pixie. Many years later, my days in the studio with DEVO and their message about de-evolution, which was all about how we are polluting ourselves to death, opened my eyes to the emerging environmental crises. So, the story of the Lynx felt natural to me. I thought I could use our film to raise awareness about our escalating environmental dilemma.

Looking back on all my years of experience, starting with my hands-on training as a combat photographer in the army, my production of Ciao! Manhattan, and then a decade in film postproduction at the helm of Mi Casa, I knew I could be a self-contained production company. I had the funding, a complete and fully staffed postproduction facility, and nobody to answer to but myself. All my ducks were in a row. I just need to work out the little details, like finding handheld cameras that could support the new high-definition formats and the gear that could survive the rough handling and the salt air. And it had to be equipment that wouldn’t break the bank.

It was a stroke of luck when I told my friend Dan Kavanaugh about the project. He offered to split the cost of the cameras, lighting kits, and so on if we could share the use of the gear. How could I say no to that?

It was the dawn of the digital age in motion pictures, and the Canon XL H1 was the latest and greatest in digital cameras. I knew of them from their outstanding quality lenses for still photography. The day after agreeing to our deal, Dan and I found ourselves sitting in front of a salesman at Birns & Sawyer, a film production and camera store, and bought ourselves a pair of Canon XL H1s. They were affordable and perfect for documentary filmmakers like us. The deal, which included a Wafian digital video recorder and a rudimentary lighting kit, came in at around $30,000. We were in business.

The cameras enabled us to record and play back high-resolution HDV 1080i images on a mini DV-format videotape in-camera, while simultaneously spitting out a non-compressed digital stream of the image data through broadcast industry-standard HD-SDI output connectors. The cameras recorded at twenty-four frames per second, the same frame rate as a movie film. The other key part of our setup was the newly developed Wafian HD-SDI video recorder. We used it to record and store all the camera video and audio. It was one of the first few to be manufactured, and was a bulky, cumbersome beast. The Wafian ran on a unique codec called CineForm, which, years later, became the basis for the GoPro cameras we take for granted today.

It wasn’t long before a pile of shiny new equipment was in the dining room at Mi Casa. The project was coming together, and it was time to find a technically savvy camera crew who understood digital technology.

Cyril, our studio’s majordomo, brought Dana Sol to the table. She wasn’t in the slightest bit intimidated by the new, all-digital workflow. Dana was tough, hard-working, and hands-on. I liked her still photography. She owned a heavy-duty laptop and was already well versed in the latest video editing software.

E.J. Emmons was a gentle and compassionate soul. I had known him since the 70s as a fellow recording engineer, record producer, photographer, and most importantly, a first-class recording studio tech who was thoroughly versed in the new digital technologies. Coincidentally, he and his partner, Vir, were developing a website for Mi Casa, so he was already aware of what I was up to.

With our team in place, we fired up the gear and got to work. It was one thing to own all the equipment and another to integrate it into a manageable workflow and get used to it before filming on the Lynx, so we decided to shoot a trial run to get our hands on the cameras and figure everything out.

Our proof-of-concept piece was a short film called Beach Run. It didn’t take much convincing to get Holger out of the studio and into his full leathers and helmet to ride his Moto Guzzi motorcycle along the Mulholland Highway on a sunny afternoon. He was leaning into the curves, and his ride ended with his front wheel touching the ocean on the beach in Malibu. We filmed him with two chase cars. It was exhilarating.

A day or two later, Dana and I set up an edit bay in my office and transferred all the HDV footage from the cameras. We began learning how to use the Wafian, which was not without its issues. The owner of the company, David Taylor, came by Mi Casa and did some handholding to help us overcome our hiccups with the recorder. Our unit was serial number three, so we were treading new ground.

Once we had proven to ourselves that we could make it all work, I asked Dana and E.J. if they were ready to rough it and head out to sea on the Lynx. Needless to say, they were ready to rock, and so it all began.

I’ll never forget our first days of filming, eating, sleeping, and keeping the equipment working in the hot, wet, and salty seagoing environment aboard the Lynx. I felt adrenaline along with feelings of peace and harmony in the challenges that I didn’t anticipate. By this time, I knew the Lynx’s story by heart, and other than a rough plan in my head, there was no script per se. It was Combat Photography 101 all over again, and I loved it.

The story was going to be about teamwork, working with nature, the sea, and the sailors driving the ship, their seamanship, navigation skills, building confidence, self-reliance, and conquering fear. Everyone depended on one another to know and do their jobs, especially in heavy weather, when they were tested to the limits of their endurance. It was a rigorous, character-building experience for all who sailed on the Lynx, and it proved to be a strong foundation and life lesson for the future.

I knew a lot about sailing but only a little about a tall ship like the Lynx, with its raked masts, square topsail, and crew of nine. The Lynx was nearly one hundred and twenty-two feet long from bowsprit to stern and weighed ninety-four tons. Her sails, standing, and running rigging were complex, with over one hundred and thirty halyards, sheets, lines, and ropes. Each one had a name and a use. The first task for any trainee, including Dana, E.J., and me, was to identify and name every single one of them. That’s where the term ‘learning the ropes’ comes from. Facing the power of the sea is an intense and humbling experience. I soon realized why most of the world’s navies still train their cadet officers on tall ships. I also came to understand that other old saying, ‘We’re all in the same boat.’ I was a long, long way from my days teaching sailing on my little sloop, the Pixie, on Long Island Sound in 1957.

We had become filmmakers at sea. Under the steady hand of Captain Craig, we also became his trainees, learning how to sail the Lynx, the ways of the ocean, the routine of the sequences of the points of sail, all while knowing where and when to point the cameras to get all the action without getting ourselves or the crew in danger.

We ended up with three XL H1 cameras for the first few months, so Dana, E.J., and I each had a camera. We usually had one tied to the Wafian for the key HD SDI shots, with all three cameras running simultaneously for some of the primary interviews. We used the onboard HDV cassettes for the rest. We would each appear in our own individual cameos with the crew, which I encouraged.

Taking the camera up the mast in a good breeze at the end of a hundred feet of wire proved rather daunting. We installed the Wafian below decks in the main cabin. The only way for us to get the non-compressed HD SDI ignal stream to the Wafian was to use fifty-foot extension cables. At the end of each day’s work, we would transfer all the HDV footage from the cameras onto the Wafian, along with the HD SDI that we already had on the device. We would view our dailies and plan what we wanted to cover during the next day’s shoot.

During the summer, even at night, it was over a hundred degrees and very humid below deck. There was no shower on board, so things got grimy and a little ripe. The aroma of cooking and freshly baked bread from the galley was the only thing that saved us. After being at sea for a few days, we either went overboard for a swim when the skipper allowed, or sometimes,

There was often heavy weather, but nothing compared to the wildest ride I can remember on the Lynx.

We were heading south on a transit run from San Francisco to Newport. It was 2am. The wind picked up to gale force, and we were flying along on a broad reach with a reefed mainsail and a jib. It was all white knuckles as we were sliding down the waves, almost surfing. The crew was in their storm gear, performing together, as one because of their training.

Below decks, anything not tied down was flying around the cabin. It was pitch black, and any thought of trying to capture the scene on film was the furthest thing from my mind. I was hanging on for dear life. The storm finally blew itself out by the early dawn.

And then there were the transcendental times. I remember being underway at night, lying half-awake in my bunk, hearing and feeling the waves pounding against the Lynx’s hull. Feeling the power of the sea just a foot away from my head, I knew that this was where I belonged.

Meanwhile, Mi Casa was running full blast with significant film projects in-house from New Line, MGM, and Lionsgate. I was on the phone, in meetings, in the studio, with clients, and doing business with some of the greats in the film world. I had a lot on my plate.

For me, there were no boundaries between working in the studio and being a filmmaker at sea. Depending on the ship’s availability and the specific voyage, I would go sailing, sometimes with Dana and E.J., or alone with my camera for an afternoon, a day, or longer. Then, we would edit what we brought back to Mi Casa. I was finding any excuse to sail on the Lynx. I became an unofficial crew member, standing watches, cautiously going aloft on occasion, and taking my turn at the helm.

After nearly a year of shooting, we had hours of interviews, action sequences, and B-roll in the can. Now we had to create a script and weave a compelling story about a voyage on the Lynx. We had our work cut out for us.

Although she didn’t have a lot of experience, Dana became our editor by default. We had done all of the cinematography and had an intimate knowledge of what we had in the can. We were using the new, somewhat temperamental CineForm high-definition codec that no else knew, so I saw no reason to bring in an outsider. We set up a video editing bay with several big screens, Dana’s laptop, a massive stack of hard drives, the Wafian recorder/player, and a connection to the studio’s master clock. Mi Casa had the best audio postproduction engineers in the business. We were constantly sharing back and forth as Brant and the other Mi Casa engineers would lean over our shoulders, making pronouncements and comments. Everybody was an expert.

As for music, I knew that the film needed something romantic and classical. What I had been doing in R&B, rock, and jazz just wouldn’t fit the bill. Hiring a big-time film composer and a big union string date recording to picture was financially out of the question. Whatever I would do for an original score would have to happen electronically at Mi Casa. Considering my background in electronic music and producing records, I welcomed that approach with open arms.

While editing, I created a temporary score by pulling parts from the TV series Victory At Sea by the brilliant Broadway composer Richard Rogers. I added a few bits from Pirates Of The Caribbean, too. Closing my eyes, I could feel the wind and the waves, which was precisely what I was looking for in the finished product.

It was by chance that I met the composer David James Nielsen in a group chat on LinkedIn. We shared a few notes and a phone call, and then David came by the studio for a little show-and-tell. David was at home with computer-based synth programming. I learned that he’d graduated from the highly regarded Scoring for Motion Picture and Television program at USC. There was no question in my mind that he had the chops. He was also aware of my credits as a recording engineer and music producer, especially my history with electronica and TONTO. When he learned of my classical music upbringing, we both knew we were on the same page.

He played some of his music for us. Dana and I showed him what we had with the temporary score and discussed the direction we had in mind. Then he went home and composed a theme for the show. His results pushed all my buttons.

I knew then that David was the man for the job.

David had an Apple Pro computer and used Logic for composing and mixing. He also had an excellent collection of live sound samples from the Vienna Symphonic Library and another collection called Drums Of War. We had some samples at Mi Casa, and the rest came from the Apple stock library for Logic Pro.

Connecting David’s computer to the console and the big studio monitors helped with the composing and recording process. The score was composed and performed digitally, ‘in the box,’ except for a violin part on one cue and Brant playing guitar on two others. There wasn’t one bar of music written on a piece of paper.

I spent a lot of time in the studio with David working on the sounds, instrumentation, and arrangements. He was creative and compassionate. It was a joyful time for me, and the memories of what I learned from my days as a music student at Tanglewood came rushing back.

I asked Dan Kavanaugh to write the narration for the film. With his vast experience as a filmmaker/documentarian in his own right, and as one of the originators of the TV show America’s Most Wanted, he was eminently qualified to do the job. Although he had never set foot on the Lynx, he knew the whole story in detail.

As we edited, I read a temporary version of the narration for place and context. In my wildest dreams, I would have had James Earl Jones perform the final narration. But this was the real world. We tried several narrators, but everyone thought my delivery was the most emotional and convincing, so I ended up doing it myself.

Once all the recording was completed, we brought the dialog, music, and effects stems up to the mixing console, where we could access the power of the studio. First, Brant mixed the music, and then, with John Bird at the helm, we mixed the dialogue, music, and effects to picture in Studio A.

Creating the film was both inspirational and educational for me. It was the culmination of all the paths that led me there: music, filmmaking, synthesizers, immersive audio production, and creatively managing a team. It reawakened my social activism with a newfound concern for the environment.

In the film, Captain Craig shares some insightful thoughts on the dangers we were facing then that are only amplified now: ‘We all need to get on the same page at the same time, and all start pumping together because if we don’t, the planet’s going down. Global warming is gonna take over. Global warming will fix everything. If we don’t take care of it, global warming will definitely take care of us.’

‘Be excellent to each other and to your ship’ I can’t help but think that this is an updated version of the motto on the wall at Stockbridge, ‘All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights.’

Tall Ships: The Privateer Lynx was released in early 2011. Halfway through its production, I had the idea to do more with what I’d learned aboard the Lynx about the intricacies of making movies on sailing ships. I envisioned a documentary film company telling seagoing adventure stories under the credo ‘Sailing does not build character; it reveals it.’ So I started a production company called Safe Harbor Pictures. I wanted to share my passion for sailing and the ocean in a way that would speak to sailors and dreamers alike. What could be better than stories of world voyaging on tall ships, ocean crossings, and training in traditional seamanship?

To be successful, Safe Harbor Pictures had to be credible to the sailing community, the film industry, and our potential audiences. I knew I couldn’t do it alone. My first job was to bring a team of inspired entertainment professionals and technology experts to the table who loved seafaring.

We were invited to present Safe Harbor’s vision, hopes, and dreams at the San Diego American Sail Training Association conference. It was the perfect opportunity to officially introduce ourselves to the sailing community and help us get ship owners on board. We presented a short piece that we had just shot on the Lady Washington. The ship was local, and the short film was of the same quality as our work on the Lynx.

We called the film Sail Away. I hoped it would be the first of many high-quality films and web series that would inspire the global tall ships community to come together and share their seagoing adventure stories. We soon started getting some interest in producing projects on a much larger scale. There were stories about tall ships and those who sailed on voyages to the South Pole, racing around Cape Horn at the bottom of the world, hauling cargo and passengers from Central America to Europe, and the great training ships from many navies of the world. We started taking meetings with agents at William Morris and executives at IMAX.

The headwinds were only getting stronger, though. Even with a noble cause, all the enthusiasm, good ideas, intentions, energy, and dedication, we couldn’t find the money, a distribution deal, or a hero at a major studio, network, or production company to stay in the game and make our projects a reality. In the end, I couldn’t afford to keep Safe Harbor Pictures afloat. And, to make things even harder, Mi Casa was having some serious troubles of its own.

Chapter 25

Stepping back from the edge

Maybe I should’ve been paying more attention to my core business. Before I even realized that Mi Casa was on a slippery slope, things had already gone from bad to worse. Our top client, New Line Cinema, the upstart independent that had made it big, was abruptly dismantled. The financial failure of The Golden Compass had been the last straw. Warner Bros, which purchased New Line, had hoped the film would be on par with their wildly successful Lord Of The Rings franchise, and had spent $180 million on the film’s development and another ton of money on marketing, yet it only grossed $70 million in the United States. The ‘suits’ at Warners sent New Line’s founders packing, and the company lost its mojo and its soul. The firings continued, with a severely scaled-down New Line operation moving out of West Hollywood and onto the Warner Bros lot. There, sadly, it became a shadow of its former self, and with that went the majority of our business.

As well as losing our biggest client, we began to find that Mi Casa’s innovations in restoration and nearfield mixing were no longer quite so exclusive. Our techniques were being copied by other postproduction houses in town. A financial executive from New Line said to me one day, ‘You know that you are very expensive, but we like listening to your monitoring systems and the care you put into your work.’ I should have read between the lines and taken that as a warning that they were looking further afield.

I had been distracted from the work at Mi Casa by the production of Tall Ships. I had hoped that generating our own content would fill in the slowing business—it was sort of a ‘Hail Mary’ shot—but it wasn’t to be.

Serious tensions were mounting with Brant, too. After more than twenty years of working together, we had stopped listening to each other. Now was the time when I needed him more than anything, and he made himself unavailable. We hadn’t co-signed our credit line, and he didn’t seem bothered by the fact that our debts were all on my shoulders. I felt increasingly powerless, and I knew he was not standing my side. I was running out of answers. Brant had become an employee instead of a partner, demanding hourly payment. In a marriage, even a professional one, it’s supposed to be ‘through thick and thin,’ but I found myself standing alone.

Brant began acting unilaterally. We were no longer a team. I stopped listening to our mixes because he wasn’t interested in what I had to say. I had always listened to everything before it went out the door, and looking back, I’m sure the chaos was noticeable in the final product. I started getting some negative feedback from our clients.

Brant and I continued to grow further and further apart, but living over the company store, so to speak, there was no place to retreat, and I was feeling more and more isolated. And when I finally found a close relationship with a young man named Bryan, some of my staff made his life miserable, and he fled the scene. Familiar, destructive patterns were repeating themselves.

Lying in bed morning after morning, I’d get maybe a few minutes of peace before the anxiety would come crashing in like a freight train—and then I was off to the races. Sure, we had other clients—Artisan Entertainment, Lionsgate, MGM—but New Line had been the heart of our business, making up over seventy-five percent of it. When that fell apart, I started calling everyone I could think of, looking for work. I’m sure, looking back, they could hear the desperation in my voice. I was scrambling to keep the doors open, draining the credit line I’d personally guaranteed—a move that turned out to be one of my biggest mistakes. When it all came down, the debt was all mine.

The weight of it all, the anxiety, debt, and desperation just became too much. I burned through what I had left, and after ten years of success, Mi Casa collapsed. And just like that, my phone stopped ringing. People disappeared. The only one who didn’t was Janel Silverstein, who’d been in my corner since we met in 1990. She stuck with me through the good, the bad, and the downright ugly.

Since my Stockbridge days back in ’57, when I first started smoking marijuana, I’d tried a bit of everything on the drug menu. Somehow, I dodged the worst of the music industry’s drug scene through the 60s, 70s, and 80s. But this time I felt so alone, so cut off, that when I got my hands on a prescription for Ativan and then Xanax, two pills a day turned into six or more. It was the only way I could quiet the fear and numb the panic. The truth is I didn’t really care if I lived or died.

I felt stuck, lost, hopeless, with no way out. I’d spend hours slumped in my massage chair, unable to move. I literally wore the thing out. My hands shook so badly I couldn’t even sign my name.

With bankruptcy hanging over my head, I finally hired a lawyer: Alik Segal. His firm was called BK Fortress (BK for bankruptcy), which actually made me feel, for a moment, like maybe I had a fighting chance. Janel, as always, had my back, riding the subway with me to downtown LA. My first and only time on the Metro. I stood in front of the judge, and he asked how I planned to repay the debt.

‘Your honor,’ I said, barely able to get the words out, ‘you can’t get blood out of a stone.’

I called an old friend in the used audio and film equipment business who auctioned off all of Mi Casa’s equipment to partially satisfy the credit line. I left the studio for the last time and ended up living in a bedroom in Van Nuys with my new rescue pup, Betty, at the home of sympathetic friends, Arlen and Dorothea. I stayed there for a little more than a year. Tony Clearwater, the contractor who helped build the studios, bought the Mi Casa house from our landlord, restored it to a residence, and flipped it, making a handsome profit. I had lost it all.

A former employee who had struggled with drugs and alcohol himself over the years offered to take me to an Alcoholics Anonymous meeting at the Log Cabin in West Hollywood at 7:30 in the morning on May 12, 2012. I found myself with fellow drunks and druggies and soon realized that was where I belonged. I sat listening to others like me, and I surrendered. I was fortunate to realize that there was something wrong before it too late to recover. I am one of the survivors, thanks to the grace of God and the program.

My recovery became one of the most critical projects in my life. Alcoholics Anonymous and Narcotics Anonymous rooms are filled with many talented people, some famous, most not, who, just like me, have decided not to throw it all away, but rather to pick up and do more with their lives. I spent the first weeks sitting at the back of the room listening to others’ stories. Slowly, I started sharing my story with the group. It took several months to taper down and detox, and what a bumpy ride that was. Over those months, I learned that sharing and caring had put me on the road to recovery. It was then that I fully realized that the program was saving my life, and for once, I stepped back from the edge. A few people I met there have become my new sober circle of friends. We look out for each other, sponsor newcomers, and sometimes even collaborate in work and business. With gratitude and daily commitment to the program, I got back on my feet, sharpened my mind, and returned to my life and my work.

Epilogue

Epilogue

Still in the game

Stevie and I have stayed in touch over the years. He called me to offer his condolences on Malcolm’s passing, and I felt the bittersweet memories of our days gone by. Then, in November 2021, out of the blue, he asked me to program a TONTO bass part for a song on his new album. I said yes, of course, but I wondered if I still had it in my head and hands. After all, it had been more than twenty years since I had touched a big Moog.

Stevie had one in his warehouse at his studio, Wonderland. I brought along Brian Kehew, a tech, for supportElevenLabs. At first, the synth wouldn’t play at all. Then Brian reset all the ribbon cables, and it started working. Stevie arrived a 10pm, and we began. I listened to the song a few times and got to work. It all just came back to me. The tuning was an issue, as usual, so we reluctantly tried several other digital synths, but they didn’t have the fat sound of the Moog. This was a familiar problem for me, and I suggested giving it one more try. By now, the synth had been turned on for almost two hours, and gradually the tuning fixed itself. It just needed to warm up. The joys of working with analog gear!

Stevie has consistently acknowledged Malcolm and me and our contribution to his work over the past several years. During his ‘House Full Of Toys’ benefit concerts, he made a point of giving us recognition from the stage. I am deeply grateful for this, as it has allowed me to find closure and recognition for our shared past.

In recent years, I’ve become more of a teacher and tech evangelist, which feels perfect for an old gray-hair like me. I often present at professional music and audio conventions, sharing what’s new, looking to the future, and telling stories from the past. I’ve appeared on many occasions with John Storyk on live panels at events like the NAMM show and in his Berklee College of Music master classes online. I still support him on his visits to L.

I serve as an advisory board member for HEAR 360, an immersive audio technology company specializing in spatial sound for virtual reality, augmented reality, and 360-degree media. Unlike Dolby Atmos, which is object-oriented and initially designed for motion pictures, HEAR 360 uses a single-sided channel-based 7.1.4 format (the .4 indicates four speakers mounted above the listener), eliminating the need for decoding during playback. This makes it a superior choice for music recording, mixing, and mastering, and delivering seamless, high-fidelity immersion without unnecessary complexity. I believe in solutions that empower composers to integrate spatial sound naturally into their creative process, and HEAR 360 provides that.

As I write this, I’ve been sober for over thirteen years. I’ve returned to music and video and even built another small home studio the Nest. I’m still producing a few records and soundtracks, usually with bright young engineers and musicians who I mentor and encourage just as I did Howard and Brant back in the day.

From my musical childhood through Stockbridge and Tanglewood to learning photography courtesy of Uncle Sam, I got an excellent education that allowed me to develop the skills, insight, and empathy to get the artists I worked with to perform to the limits of their potential. When it came to recording vocals and pioneering the synthesizer, working with Stevie was my master’s degree. And that was a two-way street. I learned a lot from Stevie, and he learned a lot from Malcolm and me. Our work together left me with high expectations and standards I maintain to this day.

My life was the best school I could have ever attended. Each step moved me further toward becoming a successful synth aficionado, producer, and engineer. Looking back over my life’s journey, I can see how my nature and my nurture, my genetics, my mentors, and the creative cast of characters I was blessed to work with, all played their role in making me the person I am. It’s only taken eighty years, but now I get it.

My excitement and sense of wonder have never changed since I first touched a handheld film camera or Moog synthesizer or discovered the real emotional impact of immersive audio. It’s about technology inspiring creativity, and how context, including where a sound is coming from, affects how we create. Computers and apps are today’s folk instruments. AI can help accelerate creation. Mobile devices and earbuds are the new rooms in which more and more people experience music and film. If art—to be art—needs an element of originality, the latest technology and new venues will always inspire new ideas.

Just as I benefited in my career from cutting-edge advances, the latest generation of producers and artists are being gifted technologies, like AI, that can empower them. But they should never be controlled or subdued by these potent tools. Instead, they should create, compose, and write for the new instruments and spaces that technology offers them. As long as that continues, we will always be delighted and enlightened by art as we’ve never seen, heard, or experienced before.

In 2013, TONTO was acquired by the National Music Center in Calgary, Alberta, Canada, where it was restored and remains in its original form. It is still in use there to this day, and its influence on modern contemporary music is undeniable. There is talk about me making a voyage to Calgary with a couple of great players to make some new music with TONTO one last time. That would be wonderful. Stay tuned.

The big challenge for me is that I’m still facing my future. I am confronted with the same time-honored question, ‘What do you want to do when you grow up?’ I’ll let you know when I do. I’ve never had a clue about a strategic plan for my future, and I’ve never been sure what tomorrow would bring. Now, with my Higher Power’s hand on my shoulder, it is still unfolding before me, which is exactly how it was meant to be.

End Credits
